# 道德真经四子古道集解

**The Four Masters' Ancient Dao Commentary on the *Dao De Jing* (Preface)**

**Title:** *The Four Masters' Ancient Dao Commentary on the Dao De Jing*.
**Author:** Ke Houcai, a man of humble talent.
**Volume:** Ten.
**Source of the base text:** *Zhengtong Daozang* (正统道藏), *Tongshen Bu* (洞神部), *Yujue Leisure* (玉诀类).
**Collated edition:** *Wuqiu Beiqing* (无求备斋) shadow-printed *Daozang Jiyao* (道藏辑要) from Qing Jiang Yutang’s publication (hereafter referred to as the *Qingkan Ben* 清刊本).

---

**Preface to the Four Masters' Ancient Dao Commentary on the Dao De Jing**

I, your servant, am a nameless rustic from the marshes, one who has never sought advancement nor cared for worldly gain.
Upon reaching adulthood, I became deeply enamored with the pleasures of tranquility and simplicity, delving into the arts of *Dan Jing* (内丹经, Inner Alchemy) and divination.
In my later years, I read the works of ancient scholars, pored over the teachings of the *Zi* (诸子, Hundred Schools), and probed the profound mysteries of *Zhuangzi’s* (庄子) *Dao*, finding meaning in every passage—truly profound and far-reaching.

Observing the interpretations and annotations of other schools, I found their words often frivolous and extravagant, giving rise to conflicting doctrines, where red and purple (orthodox and heterodox) are indiscriminately mingled. Alas, they stray so far from the original Dao of antiquity, losing its true essence—how lamentable!

Only the works of the four masters—*Zhuangzi* (庄), *Liezi* (列), *Wenzi* (文), and *Gengzi* (庚)—are the true teachings of the *Five Thousand Words* (五千言), passed down directly by *Laozi’s* (老氏) disciples, each expounding upon the same Dao with consistent intent.
The other commentaries, though numerous and verbose, merely rely on ink and speech for their claims, offering no substance, no grounding in truth—how can they compare with the four masters?

In my youth, I once followed the court and wandered the capital, where I had the fortune to encounter the lofty Dao.
The lecturers there touched lightly upon the *玄关* (Xuan Guan, Mystic Pass), yet spoke only of emptiness and voidness, unable to convey even a fragment of the true Dao.
Reflecting inwardly, I felt no guilt, for my own understanding of the Dao was deep and self-attained.
I wished to rescue the multitude from their manifold obstructions, to mourn the Way’s failure to prevail, yet feared that those ensnared in doubt and confusion might rise in noisy contention.
Thus, I gathered the four masters’ works, cited the authentic *Jing* (经), and compiled them into one volume—ten in total—
to dismantle the redundant claims of others, and named it *The Four Masters' Ancient Dao Commentary*.

I also wrote *Ten Volumes of Commentary on the Classics and Histories* (述经史疏十卷) to serve as its counterpart.
Now, by fortune, this discourse is complete.
I do not presume to rely solely on my own conjectures,
but hope it may benefit both those of my own time and as a lesson for all ages.
May those of goodwill not scorn it,
and in moments of rest, take up their brushes and record it directly.

Composed on the first day of the Year of *Jǐhài* (己亥, 1915) in the *Dàdìng* (大定) era,
by **Ke Houcai** (古襄寇才质), with reverence.

---

**Volume One: The Four Masters' Ancient Dao Commentary**

**Chapter One: "The Dao That Can Be Spoken Of" (道可道)**

This chapter in the *Nanhua Jing* (南华经, *Zhuangzi*) states:
*"The silent, unspoken teachings of Heaven and Earth—without action—are what the ancients held in highest regard,
and what the Three Sovereigns (三皇) shared in common."*

What did the ancient rulers of the world do?
They simply followed *Heaven and Earth’s* (天地) Dao of non-action.

In antiquity, the *Great Dao* (大道) was not named, and great eloquence was not spoken of.
The *Great Dao* was manifest yet not proclaimed,
and words were eloquent yet did not reach their full extent.
Who in later ages understood the unspoken teaching and the unspeakable Dao?
If any could grasp it, then this is the *Heavenly Treasury’s* (天府) Great Dao of non-action,
and the silent, unspoken teachings of Heaven and Earth.

Heaven’s nature is without desire,
and non-action brings forth transformation naturally—
no teaching, no speech.
The Dao that can be spoken of—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经, *Dao Yuan* 道原篇) says:
*"The affairs of benevolence and righteousness arise from human contrivance,
responding to changes and moving with the times—
changes that come from the seasons, where nothing is constant.
Thus, the Dao that can be spoken of is not the constant Dao."*

Not the constant Dao—
*The Dongling Jing* (洞灵经) says:
*"The later ages’ Dao of benevolence, righteousness, ritual, music, and human affairs—
the so-called ‘teaching that can be spoken of’—
is merely an adornment of words and a failure of governance,
not the constant Dao."*

The name that can be named—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The study of books arises from human speech,
which comes from wisdom—
yet wisdom is deceitful and false,
knowing nothing of the Dao.*
Those words inscribed on bamboo and silk,
or carved into metal and stone,
that can be passed down to others—
these are not the true treasures of the Dao.
Thus, the name that can be named is not the constant name."*

Not the constant name—
*The Dongling Zhenjing* (洞灵真经) says:
*"In later ages, many recite medical texts,
or study arts and crafts,
calling their spoken explanations ‘words that can be spoken of’—
mere clever embellishments, not the true words."*

Without name, there is the beginning of Heaven and Earth—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"Without name, there is no form,
and the Great Dao without form is stillness and non-action.*
The Dao of constancy, the unspeakable Dao—
vast and profound indeed!
This is the *Heavenly Treasury’s* (天府) Great Dao without teaching."*

With name, there is the mother of all things—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"With name, there is form,
and Heaven and Earth with form are stillness yet without speech.*
The constant speech, the unspeakable words—
vast and profound indeed!
This is the *Heavenly Treasury’s* (天府) Heaven and Earth without speech."*

Constantly without desire, one may observe its mysteries—
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"There is the Dao of Heaven,
which is honored through non-action.*
Heaven and Earth possess great wisdom,
yet remain detached and effortless.
The Great Dao is non-action,
its mysteries unfolding naturally."*

Constantly with desire, one may observe its perils—
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"There is the Dao of humanity,
which is humbled through action.*
Humans cultivate petty wisdom,
yet remain detached and effortful.
The Dao of humanity is action,
its perils born of contrivance.*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"Action leads to harm and peril—
this is the end of contrivance."*

These two Dao—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"Following Heaven is playing with the Dao,
while following humanity is mere worldly learning.*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"The Dao of Heaven and the Dao of humanity—
how distant they are!
One must not fail to discern them."*

**Shared Origin—**
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"Unite differences to form the same."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The Sage engages in teaching,
uniting hearts and aligning paths."*

**Yet with Different Names—**
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"Dissolve the same to form differences."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The Five Emperors and Three Kings of later ages pursued different affairs and divergent paths,
the Five Emperors differing in Dao yet bringing ruin to the world,
the Three Kings differing in affairs yet adapting to the times and changing their words."*

**The Mysterious Unity**

*"The same is called the Mysterious."*

*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The sages of antiquity harmonized their hearts with Heaven and their essence with the Dao—
this is called the profound, wondrous, and boundless Way of Non-Action."*

*"Mysterious beyond mysterious,"*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"The Dao is deep beyond depth,
yet it gives birth to all things;
Divine beyond divine,
yet it refines all things.
The gateway to all wonders."*

*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The Dao stands alone yet gives rise to ten thousand things—
thus, the roots of all affairs emerge from the single gateway of the Dao."*

*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"All things arise yet no one sees their root;
they emerge yet no one sees their gateway.
Its coming leaves no trace,
its going knows no bound—
no gate, no door,
yet it moves freely in all directions.
The vast and boundless sovereign."*

---
**Chapter Two: The Unspoken Teaching of Heaven**

*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) speaks:
*"The Sage penetrates the numinous treasury,
becomes human through alignment with Creation,
holds the Mysterious Virtue in heart,
and transforms with divine swiftness—
without a word, yet stirring the world.
Thus, the Sage moves Heaven’s heart and transforms it."*

Therefore, the teaching of the unsaid is vast beyond measure,
the argument of the unsaid is the Way that is not a Way—
if one attains it, it is called the boundless Dao of Heaven’s treasury,
the Non-Action of Heaven and Earth’s silent teaching.
All things transform spontaneously,
requiring no partial completion.
To speak of "beauty" is to mar it—
the people detest what is not good.

---
**The World**

*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"Later ages ruled without the Dao—
they were the world’s bandits.
Action, though useful, cannot govern.
When the ruler acts, the people also act.
All know beauty when it is called beauty—*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经, *□微明篇*) says:
*"Later ages knew learning was shallow compared to the Dao,
but the ancients did not know learning was shallow compared to the Dao.
Now, they know learning is external to desire,
but the ancients did not know learning was internal to nature.
Who in later ages knows that knowing learning is ignorance of the Dao?
Who knows that ignorance of learning is true knowledge of the Dao?
Thus, when all know beauty as beauty, it becomes evil.
It becomes evil."*

*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The Dao cannot be heard with the ears—
hearing is not the Dao, but learning.
The Dao cannot be seen with the eyes—
seeing is not the Dao, but learning.
The Dao cannot be spoken with words—
speaking is not the Dao, but learning.
Thus, it becomes evil."*

*"All know goodness when it is called goodness—*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经, *□无始*) says:
*"The ancients did not know learning,
yet they were deep in the Dao.
Now, they know learning,
yet they are shallow in the Dao.
They do not know the inner nature,
but know learning as external desire—
thus, in the midst of purity, they sigh:
'The ancients did not know learning,
yet they knew the Dao.
Who in later ages knows that ignorance is true knowledge of the Dao?'
Thus, when all know goodness as goodness, it becomes ungoodness.
It becomes ungoodness."*

*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"The Dao cannot be heard with the ears—
hearing is not the Dao, but learning.
The Dao cannot be seen with the eyes—
seeing is not the Dao, but learning.
The Dao cannot be spoken with words—
speaking is not the Dao, but learning.
Thus, it becomes ungoodness."*

---
**The Interdependence of Opposites**

*"Being and non-being generate each other,"*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The ruler’s Way lies in the interplay of being and non-being."*

*"Difficulty and ease complete each other,"*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The ancients governed with upright teaching—
easy and it would surely succeed.
Later ages governed with perverse teaching—
difficult and it would surely fail.
To abandon what is easy and pursue what is difficult
is to court failure—
thus, difficulty and ease complete each other."*

*"Long and short define each other,"*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"In ancient times, the people were unspoiled,
and long and short did not contrast.
Later ages, with their actions, made long and short define each other."*

*"High and low incline toward each other,"*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"In ancient times, the people were unspoiled,
and high and low did not incline—
light and heavy balanced, untilted, without overturning.
Later ages saw high and low incline toward each other."*

*"Sounds harmonize with each other,"*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"The teaching of the Great Person is like sound to its echo."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"Like an echo responding to sound,
the ruler sings above, the people respond below."*

*"Before and after follow each other,"*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"The teaching of the Great Person is like form to its shadow."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"Like a shadow mirroring form,
before and after follow each other."*

---
**Thus, the Sage**

*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The origin of the Great Dao lies in conforming to Heaven.
The highest sages conform to Heaven.
To conform to Heaven is to follow the Dao of Heaven and Earth—
emptiness, stillness, and Non-Action—
this is called Heaven and Earth.
To dwell in Non-Action,*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The ancient kings of the Way dwelled in Non-Action.*
To practice the teaching of the unsaid,*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The ancient kings of the Way practiced the teaching of the unsaid.*

*"All things*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"Now, when we count things by number, though they exceed ten thousand,
we call them 'ten thousand things' for the sake of naming and recitation—
thus, we say 'ten thousand things.'"*
*—are made yet not claimed,*
*The Nanhua Jing* (南华经) says:
*"Heaven does not give birth yet all things are nourished;
Earth does not give birth yet all things are transformed."*
*—born yet not possessed,*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The Dao gives birth to things,
yet no one sees what nourishes them—
thus, all things grow.
This is called the divine and boundless Way of Non-Action."*
*—done yet not relied upon,*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"The Dao completes things,
yet no one sees what it does—
thus, all things are complete.
This is called the divine and boundless Way of Non-Action."*
*—and the deed accomplished without taking credit.*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"To not take credit is to practice Non-Action.
Non-Action means no dwelling, no speech, no action.
The Great Dao is Non-Action—
its deeds are accomplished without knowing what is done.
Only by not taking credit*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (通玄经) says:
*"To not take credit is to dwell in Non-Action,
to move without action, to speak without words."*

Thus, it is not abandoned. *The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*"The Dao’s Non-Action makes talent arise naturally.
The Perfect Man’s virtue—
unadorned, yet nothing can separate from it."*

**Chapter 3: Not Exalting the Virtuous and Wise**

This chapter of *The Nan Hua Jing* (□ *Tian Dao Pian*) speaks:
*"In ancient times, the virtue of emperors and kings took Heaven and Earth as their foundation, Dao and virtue as their master, and Non-Action as their constant. Non-Action means governing the world with abundance.
Thus, the sage-rulers of the most ancient times ruled the world with empty hearts and full bellies—this was Non-Action.
They always kept the people ignorant, desireless, and without daring to act, not exalting the virtuous, the wise, or wealth.
Non-Action brings governance.
Not exalting the virtuous—
*The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*'In the age of the Great Dao and supreme virtue, the virtuous were not exalted, the capable were not employed. The ruler was like a fallen branch, the people like wild deer. They made the people not contend.'
Making the people not contend—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (□ *Xia De Pian*) says:
*'In later generations, human nature and disposition all desire to surpass others and resent those who outdo them. Desiring to surpass breeds contention; resenting those who outdo breeds grievance and contention. Grievance and contention arise, then the heart is disordered and qi reverses.
The ancient sage-kings retreated from contention and grievance—when contention and grievance do not arise, the heart is governed and qi flows smoothly.'
Thus it is said: Not exalting the virtuous, making the people not contend.
Not valuing hard-to-obtain wealth—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (□ *Shang Yi*) says:
*'The ancient way of governing a state led the people, not valuing hard-to-obtain wealth, not prizing useless things, making the people not steal.
*The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*'The ancients hid gold in mountains, pearls in depths—they did not profit from wealth.'
Not seeing what is desirable—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The ancient sages did not see what was desirable; their hearts and spirits were still, thus they were without desire, making the heart not disordered.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'In later generations, rulers indulged in beauty, not restraining their conduct, and the state fell into chaos, accumulating to the point of licentious calamity. Thus, those who govern must be cautious in what they inspire.
*The Chong Xu Jing* says:
*'Later rulers’ licentiousness led to the people’s licentiousness, but the ancient sages were serene and desireless, and the people naturally remained simple.'
Thus, the sage’s governance—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The foundation of governance lies in Non-Action. Thus, the sage’s governance is empty, still, and tranquil, cultivating the Dao of Nature, serene and without action, yet the world is governed.
Empty the heart—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'In governing, an empty heart attains the Dao. The sage empties the heart of all things, seeing insufficiency.
Seeing insufficiency, thus able to perfect virtue, while the extravagant do not endure.
Fill the belly—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The ancient sages guarded the inner while not losing the outer, thus their chests and bellies were full and their cravings few.
Weaken the will—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'In governing, a weakened will attains the Dao. A weak will and strong affairs—calm and undisturbed, yet naturally attaining.
Strengthen the bones—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The governance of the Perfect Man abandoned cleverness, erased artifice, thus aligning with the divine and boundless Way of Non-Action.
When the divine attains the inner, the five viscera are tranquil, thoughts balanced, sinews and bones strong, ears and eyes keen—firm and unexhausted, nothing they cannot reach.
Always keeping the people—
*The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*'The ancients practiced Non-Action, thus governed the world with abundance. The ruler above practiced Non-Action, the people below also practiced Non-Action.
Ignorant and desireless—
*The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*'The ancients aligned with ignorance, their virtue not departing. Aligning with desirelessness, the people remained simple.
Making the knowing dare not act—
*The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*'The ruler above must practice Non-Action to govern the world; the people below must act to serve the world—this is the unchanging Dao.'
Practicing Non-Action—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'Practice Non-Action, manage without managing, know the unknown.'
*The Nan Hua Jing* says:
*'The emperor’s duty lies in Non-Action—then nothing is ungoverned.'
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The ancient sages were serene and without action, yet nothing was left undone. Serene and without governance, yet nothing was left ungoverned.'*

**Chapter 4: The Dao’s Effortless Flow**

This chapter of *The Nan Hua Jing* speaks:
*"The Dao’s effortless and harmonious qi nourishes all things.
It is everywhere, wherever it is, it exists.
In ants and crawling creatures, it does not fail to nourish and rear—why does the Dao humble itself to blunt their sharpness?
In tiles, bricks, soil, and stone, it does not fail to complete—why does the Dao, instead, further scatter their confusion?
In weeds and grasses, it does not fail to grow—does the Dao humble itself to soften its radiance?
In excrement, urine, and filth, it does not discriminate against filth—it equals them with dust.
Deep and vast, as if it might exist—
I do not know whose child this great form is;
it mirrors the Emperor’s primordial Heaven, the Dao’s qi.
The Dao’s effortless flow—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The Great Dao’s yin-yang harmonious qi is pure and clear.
And its use—
*The Tongxuan Jing* (□ *Wei Ming Pian*) says:
*'The Dao’s effortless flow is used, yet it is not full.
Sometimes it does not overflow.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The Dao—harmonious qi, effortless yet not full.
Deep, as if the origin of all things.
Blunting their sharpness—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The Dao blunts sharpness—there is no cruelty or evil.
Dissolving their confusion—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The Dao dissolves confusion—there is no contention or disorder.
Softening its radiance—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The Dao softens its radiance—there is no grievance or resistance.
Equaling the dust—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The Dao equals the dust—it unifies with filth.'"

The Dao abides as if it exists.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Heaven, through it, is clear—
serene and still."*

I do not know whose child it is.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"I do not know whose child it is."*

It precedes the Emperor.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Ran Qiu asked Zhongni:
'Can the child of the formless Dao
be the ancestor of the formed Heaven and Earth?'
Zhongni said:
'If there is something that precedes Heaven and Earth in form,
is it not all things?
Things arise from things—
the Dao is not the maker of things.'*

**Chapter Five: Heaven and Earth**
This chapter of *The Nanhua Jing (Zhi Beiyou)* speaks:
*"Heaven and Earth possess great beauty yet do not speak,
the four seasons have clear laws yet do not debate,
all things have their natural order yet are not explained.
Ancient sages observed Heaven and Earth’s effortless beauty,
grasped the natural principles of all things—
thus the sage does nothing,
the great sage takes no action,
observing Heaven and Earth’s effortless Way.
They do not teach the petty virtues of benevolence and righteousness,
nor the many words of artificial deeds,
treating the people as grass-dogs,
following the natural winds of the sage—
thus the world is governed."*

Heaven and Earth are not benevolent.
*The Tongxuan Jing (Natural Section)* says:
*"The Way of Heaven and Earth takes virtue as its master,
the Dao as its decree,
things self-regulate.
Most subtle within,
it does not honor rank through deeds,
nor demand ritual to be solemn—
thus the Dao stands without teaching,
its radiance shines without scrutiny.
The Dao stands without teaching—
it does not deprive people of their abilities.
Its radiance shines without scrutiny—
it does not harm their affairs.
Those who teach the Dao
act against virtue and harm things—
thus yin-yang, the four seasons, and the Five Phases
share the Dao yet differ in principle,
all things share sentiment yet differ in form.
The wise do not instruct one another,
the capable do not receive from one another—
thus Heaven and Earth are not benevolent,
treating all things as grass-dogs."*

Treating all things as grass-dogs.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Heaven and Earth do not act artificially—
they rectify the Dao, and things naturally arise.
Yin-yang and the four seasons do not create all things,
timely rain and dew do not nourish plants—
when divine resonance meets,
yin-yang harmonizes,
all things are born."*

The sage is not benevolent.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The sage establishes laws to guide the people’s hearts,
letting them follow their nature—
thus the living have no virtue,
the dead have no grievance.
Benevolence, love, righteousness—
these are narrow ways.
The narrow enter the great and are lost,
the near travel far and are confused.
The sage enters the great without losing his way,
travels far without confusion,
constantly empty and self-guarded—
this is called Heaven’s virtue.
Thus the sage is not benevolent,
treating the people as grass-dogs."*

Treating the people as grass-dogs.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The people follow their ruler like grass follows the wind,
their transformation is like wind swaying grass."*

Between Heaven and Earth,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Earthly resonance—this is the multitude of holes.
Resonance—
the Great Block exhales, its name is Wind,
blowing in myriad ways,
yet letting things be natural.
All things roar and cry—
who commands them?
It is as if they cast away their keys."*

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Human resonance—this is the bamboo pipes.
Wind acts without force—
if it acts, all things roar and cry,
movement becomes sound.
A cool wind brings small harmony,
a gusting wind brings great harmony—
like blowing through human resonance,
bamboo pipes, or keys."*

Empty yet unyielding,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The highest sage follows Heaven’s empty stillness and effortless action—
this is the Way to govern Heaven and Earth.
Knowing the Way of empty stillness,
one can begin and end—
this is called Heaven and Earth."*

Moving yet ever emerging.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
*"The Tang ruled the world,
wandering the broad streets,
heard children’s songs:
*'Establish the boiling people,
none but your extreme.'*
Not knowing, not knowing,
following the Emperor’s decree.
Tang asked joyfully:
'Who taught you this song?'
The child said:
'An ancient poem.'*

Many words lead to exhaustion.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Later generations know books yet the Dao declines,
know numbers yet virtue declines.
Those who rely on numbers labor without fruit,
those who rely on books and numbers
are born from clever speech.
Words arise from intelligence—
the clever are deceitful and false,
unaware of the Dao.
Many words lead to exhaustion—
better to guard the Middle."*

*The Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Qi and Lu abound in clever devices,
there are those skilled in books and numbers."*

Better to guard the Middle.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The foundation of teaching lies in removing petty achievements.
Those who embody the Dao
are at ease and unexhausted,
their spirit transforms effortlessly—
the world subtly responds.
This is called Heaven and Earth’s unspeakable teaching—
vast and boundless!
If later ages know this,
it is called the great Dao of Heaven’s treasury,
Heaven and Earth’s unspeakable teaching."*

**Chapter Six: The Valley Spirit**
This chapter of *The Nanhua Jing* speaks:
*"The Great Dao is formless,
yet it gives birth to emotions, ghosts, gods, and the Emperor—
it generates Heaven and Earth.
Heaven receives one breath—
clear and empty, it governs its movement,
Earth receives one breath—
settled and tranquil, it holds its place.
Thus the Valley Spirit of Heaven and Earth does not die—
this is called the divine mystery of yin-yang’s primal mother,
the creative force.
The primal mother—
the gateway of yin-yang,
the root that moves Heaven and Earth.
Heaven is continuous, as if it exists,
Earth uses it without effort.
The Dao—
the root of Heaven and Earth.
The Valley Spirit does not die,
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
*'Heaven and Earth constantly generate and transform—
never ceasing to generate,
never ceasing to transform,
the four seasons—
the Valley Spirit does not die.'
This is called the primal mother.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
*'The Great Dao constantly generates and transforms,
nothing it does not generate,
nothing it does not transform—
yin-yang—
this is called the primal mother.
The gateway of the primal mother,
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
*'The Great Dao’s yin-yang—
doubtful, its Way is unfathomable,
the gateway of the primal mother.'"

This is called the root of Heaven and Earth.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Heaven and Earth’s four seasons—
their cycles endlessly return,
never ceasing to return—
this is called the root of Heaven and Earth.

Continuous, as if it exists,
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Continuous, as if it exists.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Great Dao’s utmost subtlety—
continuous, as if it exists,
thus able to govern Heaven’s movement.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Heaven’s movement.

Used without effort.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Used without effort.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Earth’s use is natural.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Used without effort,
thus able to sustain Earth’s endurance.

**Chapter Seven: Heaven Long, Earth Enduring**
This chapter from *The Nanhua Jing* [☐ *Yang* Section] speaks:
The Great Dao—
the greatest of *qi*.
Heaven and Earth—
the greatest of form.
The Dao acts impartially;
Heaven and Earth are without favor,
thus able to endure.

Therefore, the ancient sages, when about to abdicate,
offered the throne to rulers who upheld Heaven’s impartiality,
covering all like the heavens.
The realm endured long,
their bodies perished yet they lived on,
their bodies abandoned yet they survived—
was it not because of their lack of favoritism?
Thus their favor was fulfilled.
Heaven long, Earth enduring.

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Heaven covers without favor,
covering all without exception.
Earth bears without favor,
bearing all without exception.
Thus Heaven and Earth could endure long.

*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Great Dao—
the greatest of *qi*.
Heaven and Earth—
the greatest of form.
The Dao acts impartially;
Heaven and Earth are without favor,
thus able to endure.

Because they do not seek self-generation,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
As Heaven’s own height,
Earth’s own depth,
the Sun and Moon’s own luminosity—
why should they seek to prolong themselves?
Because they do not seek self-generation,
thus able to endure long.
Thus able to long endure.

*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Heaven and Earth go without favor,
neither repelling nor attracting,
thus able to endure long.

Therefore, the sages—
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Tang and Zhao abdicated to Shun,
who did not refuse and accepted it.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Emperors and kings properly abdicated,
the Three Dynasties properly succeeded—
timing their era,
opposing customs was called usurpation.
When in accord with the times,
following customs was called the righteous path.

Delaying the times,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Dao of Heaven and Earth takes the later as primary;
the sage follows the Dao’s retreat,
thus securing safety through retreat.

Yet their bodies perished first,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Retreat, thus able to lead—
this is what Heaven and Earth accomplish,
taking the later to gain the first.

Abandoning their bodies,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Dao of Heaven and Earth takes the small as foundation;
the sage follows the Dao’s smallness,
thus securing protection through humility.

Yet their bodies survived.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Small, thus able to become great—
this is what Heaven’s Dao accomplishes,
taking loss to gain preservation.

Was it not because of their lack of favoritism?
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient sages preserved with Heaven and Earth,
impartial and just.
Thus their favor was fulfilled.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The sages of old had no private virtue,
their lack of private virtue—
their lack of private fortune.

**Chapter Eight: The Supreme Good as Water**
This chapter from *The Tongxuan Jing* [☐ *Dao Yuan* Section] speaks:
Water’s Way—
above, it becomes Heaven’s rain and dew,
below, it becomes Earth’s moisture,
all things cannot but live,
all affairs cannot but succeed.
It bears Heaven’s gifts and gives to all,
nourishing all things,
sharing with Heaven and Earth—
this is called supreme virtue.

Water’s gentleness nears the Dao;
the ancient sages modeled Heaven’s goodness,
like water’s supreme virtue.

The supreme good as water,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
None are softer or weaker than water.
Water benefits all things,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s Way—
above, it becomes Heaven’s rain and dew,
all things cannot but live.
And it does not contend,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water flows without contention,
swift in departure yet not delayed.
It dwells where all despise,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Its blessings extend even to gnats,
yet it seeks no reward.
Thus it nears the Dao.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water aligns with Heaven and Earth’s vastness—
this is called supreme virtue.
Water’s gentleness nears the Dao.

Dwelling is good in humility,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s Way—
below, it becomes Earth’s moisture,
all things cannot but live.
The heart is good in depth,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s breadth is boundless,
its depth unfathomable.
Giving is good in kindness,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water enriches the world yet exhausts no virtue.
Speech is good in trustworthiness,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water supplements what is lacking,
bearing Heaven’s gifts and giving freely.
Governance is good in order,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s ebb and flow—
its excess is beyond measure.
Action is good in capability,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s utility penetrates metal and stone,
strong enough to overcome all.
Movement is good in timing.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water nourishes all things without precedence.

Only because it does not contend,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s ability to fulfill Heaven and Earth’s supreme virtue
lies in its flexibility and reach.
Thus it incurs no blame.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Water’s Way—
all affairs cannot but succeed,
its extent endless, its depth boundless,
it encompasses all life,
benefits the people without exhaustion.

*The Dao De Jing: Four Children’s Ancient Dao Exegesis, Volume One Complete*
*The Dao De Jing: Four Children’s Ancient Dao Exegesis, Volume Two*

**Chapter Nine: "Holding and Filling"**
This chapter, from *The Tongxuan Jing*, speaks of the last age’s rulers—dukes and marquises—whose arrogance and extravagance cannot endure. *The Zhuangzi* says:
*"The ancient sages destroyed the reckless surge of ambition."*
Wealth and nobility, these two, are the surge of ambition.
If the heart remains undisturbed, it is upright.
Uprightness means emptiness, stillness, non-action—and yet nothing remains undone.

*"Holding and filling"*—
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"Filled, it overflows—one must not indulge to excess."*
*"Do not push to the limit."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* (*Wei Ming* section) says:
*"Holding and filling—better to stop in time."*
*"Prodding and sharpening"*—
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"Sharpened, it dulls—one must not indulge in arrogance."*
*"It cannot long endure."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Prodding and sharpening—it cannot long endure."*
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Weighing harm and gain—better to stop in time."*

Gold and jade fill the hall—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Ancient sage-kings dwelled in fullness without overflow, thus enduring.
The last age’s overflowing perishes.
None can guard it."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Dwelling in great fullness without spilling, filled yet unlacking—
thus one long preserves wealth.
The last age cannot guard it—losing the world."*

Wealth and nobility bring arrogance—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Born noble, one grows arrogant; born rich, one grows extravagant.
The last age’s arrogant and extravagant perish.
They invite their own ruin."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Few are the wealthy and noble who, without self-restraint, do not perish."*

When achievement is complete and fame is secured—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Heaven and Earth reach their limit and reverse; fullness brings depletion.
The sun and moon are proof—
at zenith, they decline;
at fullness, they wane.
How much more so for mortals!
Retreat is the Way of Heaven."*
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"When a ruler grows arrogant, he indulges to excess—
none who do so escape ruin.
Thus, when achievement is complete, retreat is the Way."*

**Chapter Ten: "Carrying the Vital Essence"**
This chapter, from *The Zhuangzi* (*Tian Dao* section), speaks:
The six qualities—emptiness, stillness, softness, weakness, simplicity, non-action, purity, and naturalness—
these are the form of the Dao.
Heaven and Earth are balanced; virtue reaches its peak.
Thus, emperors and sages rest their thoughts and quiet their hearts.
Rest brings emptiness, stillness, non-action—and the world is governed,
nothing left undone.

Therefore, the divine persons of antiquity—true persons, perfect persons, sages, Daoists, virtuous ones—
embodied these six qualities:
emptiness, stillness, softness, weakness, simplicity, non-action, purity, and naturalness,
unmixed with things.
Their virtue was supreme, the Way of Heaven and Earth—
thus they were called divine and sage.
The truly authentic, through non-action, governed the world—
thus they endured.

*"Carrying the vital essence, embracing unity"*—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The divine persons of antiquity embodied the Dao in emptiness and stillness—
'emptiness' is the Dao’s dwelling.
Emptiness means the heart bears no burdens;
desires are not loaded—
this is the utmost emptiness.
Machinations and cleverness do not burden the sovereign’s heart;
unified, all affairs are complete."*
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"The divine person rides the light—
with form, he perishes.
This is called the divine person.
Can he truly be unattached?"*
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"The ancients aligned inwardly with the Dao, doing nothing.
Their unity made them companions of Heaven.
Nowadays, they conform outwardly but not inwardly—
doing much, they follow others."*

*"Focusing the vital breath, attaining softness"*—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The ancient sages embodied the Dao, guarding softness and weakness—
softness is the Dao’s strength;
weakness is the Dao’s power.
Softness and weakness are the Dao’s operation.
Accumulated softness becomes strength;
accumulated weakness becomes power—
without accumulation, this never occurs."*
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"Sage governance—
officials act without losing propriety,
the capable are promoted without losing their talents,
all emotions and affairs are revealed,
actions and words are spontaneous—
thus the world transforms.
When the hands move, the fingers point—
people from all quarters come together.
This is called sage governance.
Can one be like an infant?"*
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"The ancients transformed outwardly but not inwardly—
non-acting governed within, yet they did not act outwardly.
Nowadays, they transform inwardly but not outwardly—
acting outwardly, they do not govern within."*

*"Purifying and gazing into the mysterious"*—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The ancient perfect ones embodied the Dao, guarding simplicity—
'simplicity' means the heart bears no burdens.
Neither liking nor disliking—
this is the utmost simplicity.
Simplicity and ease—
this is Heaven and Earth’s balance."*
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"The perfect person’s heart is like a bell—
neither repelling nor inviting,
responding to things without hoarding,
thus overcoming things without harm.
This is called the perfect person.
Can one be without flaw?"*
*The Zhuangzi* says:
*"The ancients governed inwardly without action,
yet did not govern outwardly with action.
Nowadays, they govern outwardly with action,
yet do not govern inwardly without action."*

*"Loving the people, governing the state"*—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The ancient Daoists embodied the Dao, guarding non-action—
'non-action' means guarding stillness.
Guarding stillness makes one king of the world.
Stillness is the Dao’s mirror.
Unchanging unity—
this is the utmost stillness.
So-called non-action—
does it mean things do not come when drawn,
or go when pushed?
Pressured, it does not respond;
moved, it does not stir;
rigid and stagnant, it does not flow;
rolled and grasped, it does not scatter.
Does this mean private desires do not harm the public Way?
Desires do not injure proper virtue—
thus ever empty, ever non-acting,
embracing simplicity, holding purity,
not clinging to things—
this is called non-action."*

Can one attain non-action?
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancients savored the Dao without abandoning it,
while the moderns abandon the Dao without savoring it.
To abandon the Dao and rely on learning is perilous;
to forsake virtue and employ talent leads to困境.

The opening and closing of the Heavenly Gate,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient True Persons embodied the Dao, guarding purity.
Purity—this is the Dao’s essence.
Unmixed with things, the utmost purity.
Still, it aligns with yin’s closure;
moving, it aligns with yang’s opening.
To reach the point of no excess in pleasure—
then there is no lack of joy.
No lack of joy is the utmost joy—
this is how True Persons wander the Dao of purity.

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The ancients, True Persons—
their sleep was dreamless,
their waking free of worry,
their spirit pure,
their soul unexhausted,
empty and tranquil,
thus harmonizing with Heaven’s virtue.
Purity unmixed—
this is the Way of nurturing the spirit.
The Way of purity
is guarded solely by the spirit,
so *sū* (素) means unmixed with anything,
*pú* (纯) means unblemished in spirit.
To embody purity and simplicity—
this is called a True Person.

Can one attain non-duality?
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Now, observing the ranks of True Persons above,
delving deep into the intent of virtue and Dao;
below, examining the common customs of the world—
this is enough to shame and disgrace the shallow and impure.

Clarity and openness in all directions,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient virtuous embodied the Dao, guarding simplicity.
To have no worry, no joy—
this is the utmost virtue.
Confucius asked of the Dao, and Laozi said:
*"Rectify your form,
unify your gaze—
Heaven’s harmony will arrive.
Gather your wisdom,
rectify your measure—
the spirit will come to dwell.
Virtue will be your vessel,
the Dao will be your abode.
Your pupils like those of a newborn calf, without desire,
your form like withered wood,
your heart like dead ashes.
True and real, yet not scheming—
thus self-sustaining, boundless,
heartless in plotting,
clear and open in all directions—
can one attain non-knowing?"

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The virtuous person—
dwelling without thought,
moving without deliberation,
not storing up right and wrong, beauty and ugliness—
all within the Four Seas rejoice in sharing benefits as ease,
find security in mutual provision,
lost as an infant separated from its mother,
wandering as one who has lost the path.
Wealth overflows yet they know not its source,
food suffices yet they know not its origin—
this is called the bearing of the virtuous person.

Can one attain non-knowing?
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancients of old were clear, entering simplicity,
returning to non-action, restoring purity,
upright without guile—
thus the people were easily guided.
Utmost loyalty restored purity—
the people had no deceit or concealment.
Now, those of later generations who govern through cleverness
labor their minds yet remain unclear;
those who govern through scheming
toil their hearts yet achieve no success.

The nurturing of life and its sustenance—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
These six principles of governance—
the ancient kings thus held the realm without action and yet prevailed.
The Dao generates and sustains without contrivance—
this is called the Mysterious Virtue.

To generate yet not possess,
*The Nanhuajing* says: To generate yet not possess.
To act yet not rely,
*The Nanhuajing* says: To act yet not rely.
To grow yet not dominate,
*The Nanhuajing* says: To grow yet not dominate.
This is called the Mysterious Virtue.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The sages of the Great Antiquity held the Mysterious Virtue in heart,
and their transformations moved like the divine—
no decrees issued, yet customs shifted,
mores transformed—
this was the silent teaching of the unspoken Way,
the boundless and vast.
Few know this teaching of non-action,
this Way of the unuttered.
How profound and great it is!
If later ages could but know this,
it would be called the Dao of Heaven’s Treasury, the Way of Non-Action.

**Thirty Spokes Converging at the Hub (Chapter Eleven)**
This chapter from the *Nanhuajing* [missing character] *Mati* [or *Mashui*] says:
In my view, the ancients who governed well did not rely on fixed rules.
The people of old had natural inclinations—
they wove and clothed themselves,
they tilled and fed themselves—
this was called harmony of virtue.
Unified yet without factions,
this was called Heaven’s release—
the people were pure and simple, without artifice or craft.
Later generations, the potters said:
“I excel in shaping clay—
round forms conform to the compass,
square forms to the square.”
The carpenters said:
“I excel in working wood—
curved forms fit the hook,
straight forms align with the plumb.”
Yet clay and wood by nature do not seek to conform to compass, square, hook, or plumb—
and yet ages praise them as “the potter and carpenter excel in shaping clay and wood.”
Thus, they fostered artifice and deceit—
this too is how later rulers, teaching the crafts of earth and wood,
exceeded in artifice and deceit.
In ancient times, white jade was left uncarved—
who would make it into *gui* or *zhang*?
Pure simplicity was left unbroken—
who would carve it into sacrificial vessels?
Thus, to ruin simplicity for tools—
this was the excess of later artisans’ craft.
*The Dongling Jing* says:
In the age of perfect governance,
plain and undyed carriages and garments made people envious,
desires were sparse and light—
people took joy in their labor and service.
This was the foundation of governing the state.
In the decadent later ages,
ornate and elaborate carriages and garments made the vulgar emulate them—
people lost sincerity and purity, shaming simplicity while valuing extravagance.
To shame simplicity and value extravagance—
this is called the *fufu* (floating and insubstantial),
the ladder to a state’s ruin.
Thus, in later ages, the Five Emperors introduced carriages, dwellings, and the three implements—
they shamed simplicity and valued extravagance.

Thirty spokes converge at one hub,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient governance, artisans did not indulge in excess craft—
their carriages were plain and uncarved,
thirty spokes converged at one hub.
When their essence was absent,
the carriage’s use emerged.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Later artisans indulged in elaborate craft—
carriages became extravagantly carved,
taking years to complete,
benefiting expense more than use.
To mold clay into vessels,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient governance, artisans did not indulge in excess craft—
their vessels were plain and undecorated.
When their essence was absent,
the vessel’s use emerged.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Later artisans indulged in elaborate craft—
vessels became excessively carved and inlaid,
taking years to complete,
benefiting expense more than use.
To pierce doors and windows for dwellings,
*The Nanhuajing* says:
Anciently, they dwelled in nests and caves—
called the *Youcang* (Nest-Dwelling) clan,
eating acorns and chestnuts by day,
sheltering in trees by night.
When their essence was absent,
the dwelling’s use emerged.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Later, under a single ruler’s authority,
they exhausted the people’s strength to indulge the senses—
their ambitions fixed on palaces, pavilions, ponds, and enclosures.
*The Dongling Jing* says:
To seize control of affairs through earthworks—
this is called great calamity.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Qi and Lu abounded in ingenuity,
with masters of earth and wood.
Thus, they took what was present as profit—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Now, shaped tools serve their purposes.
To serve purposes is to complete tools.
Thus, labor yields profit,
but useful tools destroy the Way.
To take what is absent as use—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient Way was formless—
this was the beginning.
The beginning was simplicity.
Thus, the unnamed was taken as use—
to use simplicity is to avoid tools.

**The Chapter of the Five Colors (Chapter Twelve)**
This chapter from the *Nanhuajing* says:
In ancient times, perfect joy lay in preserving life,
merely by preserving non-action.
But people of the later ages—
who among them could attain non-action?
What the world honors are wealth, nobility, longevity, and virtue.
What they delight in are bodily ease, rich flavors,
fine garments, beautiful colors, pleasing sounds.
What they despise are poverty, lowliness, and evil.
What they suffer are:
the body not at ease,
the mouth not tasting rich flavors,
the form not adorned in fine garments,
the eyes not feasting on beautiful colors,
the ears not hearing pleasing sounds.
If these are not obtained, great anxiety arises—
fearing harm to the body,
they injure their essence and damage their nature—
how foolish and deluded they are!

Moreover, losing one’s nature has five forms:
One, the five colors dazzle the eyes, making them dim;
Two, the five sounds assail the ears, making them deaf;
Three, the five stinks assail the nose, bewildering the forehead;
Four, the five tastes dull the palate, making the tongue parched;
Five, taking and rejecting disturb the heart, causing the nature to scatter.
The finery of feathers and tassels—
this is the extreme of color;
The sounds of bells and drums—
this is the extreme of music.

The palate’s indulgence in sweetness is the extreme of taste.
These five extremes—color, sound, scent, flavor, and desire—
are all great harms that shorten the lives of the people.

This warns the degenerate rulers of the last age,
who indulge in sound and color, abandoning Dao and losing the world.

The five colors blind the eyes.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ Nine Guards Section] says:
*"One: The five colors dazzle the eyes, making them dim."*
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"The eyes behold the dazzling hues of brocade and embroidery,
and are thus bewildered, forgetting their purpose—this is called chaos."*

The five tones deafen the ears.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Two: The five sounds assail the ears, making them deaf."*
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"The ears are assailed by the clamor of bells, drums, pipes, and strings,
and are thus bewildered, forgetting their purpose—this is called chaos."*

The five flavors parch the mouth.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Three: The five tastes dull the palate, making the tongue ulcerate."*
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"The five stinks assail the nose, bewildering the forehead.
The five tastes dull the palate, making the tongue parched.
The mouth indulges in the flavors of grain, livestock, wine, and rice wine,
and is thus bewildered, forgetting its purpose—this is called chaos."*

Hunting and galloping madden the heart.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Four: Taking and rejecting disturb the heart, causing the nature to scatter."*
Hunting and galloping disrupt the people’s seasons and exhaust their strength.
The degenerate rulers dissect fetuses and burn the altars,
overturn nests and shatter eggs,
burn forests and drain marshes to catch fish—
phoenixes no longer soar, and qilin no longer roam.
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"The people of Handan, on the first day of the first month,
presented doves to Lord Jianzi, who rejoiced greatly and rewarded them.
A guest asked why. Lord Jianzi said:
‘On the first day, I release them to show my benevolence.’
The guest replied:
‘The people, knowing you wish to release them,
compete to capture them, and many die.
If you truly wish them to live,
you should forbid the people from capturing them.
Capturing and releasing them—your kindness does not compensate for the harm.’"*

Rarity in goods leads to misconduct.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"In the degenerate age, those seeking wealth vie for the difficult,
and jade and pearls are coveted by all under heaven."*
The degenerate rulers quarry mountains, extract gold and jade,
dig for pearls and abalone, melt copper and iron—
and all things wither and fail to flourish.
Thus, the Sage says:
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Now all under heaven are bewildered by sound, color, scent, and taste—
who can rectify them?"*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The foundation of rectification lies in abandoning sound and color.
The gentleman cultivates upright qi;
the petty man cultivates perverse qi.
To align internally with nature and externally with righteousness,
to move in accordance with principle without being enslaved by things—
this is upright qi.
To indulge in flavors and lust after sound and color,
without heeding future calamity—
this is perverse qi."*

For the sake of the belly,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The eyes delight in colors, the ears in sounds,
the nose in fragrance, the mouth in taste—
all united in pleasure, never departing from desires of gain and harm."*
The ears, eyes, mouth, and nose know not their own desires;
the heart alone governs them, each attaining its fill—
thus, it is clear that desire cannot be exhausted.

Not for the sake of the eyes,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The eyes delight in the five colors, the mouth craves flavors,
the ears lust after the five tones, the nose after the five scents—
the seven orifices contend, harming the one true nature,
daily drawing in perverse desires and exhausting the celestial harmony.
The body itself cannot be governed—
how then can one govern the world?"*
Thus, abandon these and take that.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Thus, abandon these and take that—
and calamity from Heaven will not arise.
If one obtains this, forgetting that,
then blame departs and fortune arrives."*

**Chapter Thirteen: On Honor and Humiliation**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* speaks of the ancients’ abandonment of turbulent ambition.
Name and profit, honor and display—these are turbulent ambitions.
If the heart remains undisturbed, then it is upright;
upright, it is empty, tranquil, and without action.

Tang yielded the world to Yú Yóu, but Yú Yóu refused.
He then yielded it to Zīzhōu Zhīfù, who said:
*"To be made king is acceptable.
Yet I suffer from melancholy illness and must attend to it—
I have no leisure to govern the world."*
The world is the heaviest of burdens,
yet he did not use it to harm his life—
how much less would he harm other things?
Only those who do not cling to the world
can entrust themselves to it.

Shun yielded the world, and Shàn Jiàn said:
*"I stand amid the cosmos,
wearing fur in winter and hemp in summer,
plowing and planting in spring to labor,
harvesting and storing in autumn to rest.
I rise with the sun and rest with the moon,
wandering freely between Heaven and Earth,
and my heart and mind find contentment—
why should I concern myself with the world?"*
Alas, the Master does not know me!
Thus, he declined and departed into the deep mountains,
and none knew his whereabouts.

How much more so the warring states’ rulers,
who contend for fame and profit,
ruining themselves and their nations—
for they take honor as their affliction.
One who can yield the kingship to entrust the world,
who values life and honors the body,
can thus endure long.

Honor and humiliation strike like a shock.
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Name is a guest; the body is the host.
Name is the guest of the body,
yet the foolish pursue only the guest.
If there is a name, there is honor;
if the name is lost, there is humiliation.
Honor brings ease and pleasure—
this aligns with nature.
Humiliation brings sorrow and suffering—
this violates nature."*

Treasure great affliction as the body.
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"To cling to a name and burden the body
is to invite ruin and death—
how much worse than mere ease or suffering?"*

What is meant by honor and humiliation striking like a shock?
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"When honor and humiliation are established,
then one perceives their affliction—
how can one not be cautious?"*

Honor is supreme,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ Dao Original Section] says:
*"Wealth, nobility, and renown."*

「Having a name is honor and esteem, with the highest virtue as its pinnacle.
Shame is the lowly state,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ Dao Original Section] says:
*"Poverty and obscurity lack a name."*

Without a name, one is lowly and shamed—
this is the fate of the base and despised.
To gain it is as if struck by a shock,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Profit is the beginning of fortune,
yet profit and harm share the same gate."*

To lose it is as if struck by a shock,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Fortune is the precursor of calamity,
and calamity and fortune dwell as neighbors."*

This is what is called *honor and humiliation striking like a shock*.
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Discern the distinction between inner and outer,
and clarify the boundaries of honor and shame."*

What is meant by *treasuring great affliction as the body*?
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Now, those who take the profit and harm of the Spring and Autumn era as great affliction—
they must not fail to observe them carefully.*

*My great affliction lies in this:*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The will has desires,
yet forgets its afflictions."*

*For me, having a body is the affliction,*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The foolish are bewildered by petty profit
and forget their great affliction."*

*When I have no body,*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"If one does not seek a name, there is no exhaustion;
if one does not seek profit, there is no harm.
What affliction do I have?"*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The body’s completeness is its foundation;
wealth and nobility are but its lodging."*

Thus, *treasuring the body as the Dao for all under Heaven*,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ Upper Virtue Section] says:
*"The ancients who could honor life—
though wealthy and noble, they did not let a name exhaust their body,
though poor and lowly, they did not let profit burden their form.*

*Now, those who receive noble titles from their ancestors—*
their life’s origin is distant indeed,
yet they lightly court its loss—
are they not bewildered by name and profit?"*

*Treasure the body as the Dao for all under Heaven,*
and one can entrust all under Heaven;
*Love the body as the Dao for all under Heaven,*
and one can lodge all under Heaven within it.
If one can entrust all under Heaven,

*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"King Danfu of the Great [State] resided in Yin.
The Di people attacked him,
offering tribute of silk, furs, dogs, horses, jade, and pearls—
all were refused.
The Di people sought only his lands.
King Danfu said:
*'To dwell with a man’s elder brother while slaying his younger,
to dwell with a man’s father while slaying his son—
I cannot bear it!*

*Let them occupy the lands themselves!*
*What difference does it make whether they serve me or another?*

*Moreover, I have heard this:*
*'Do not use what nourishes you to harm your nourisher.'*

*He departed with his staff and chariot,*
and the people followed in procession,
thus establishing his state beneath Qishan.
Truly, King Danfu of the Great [State]
can be said to have honored life.
He loved the body as the Dao for all under Heaven."*

*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"The ancients who could honor life—
though wealthy and noble, they did not let nourishment harm their body,
though poor and lowly, they did not let profit burden their form.*

*Now, the people of this age who hold high office and noble rank—*
they all fear losing it,
seeing profit yet lightly risking their lives—
are they not bewildered?"*

Thus, *treasuring the body as the Dao for all under Heaven*,
one can lodge all under Heaven within it;
*loving the body as the Dao for all under Heaven*,
one can entrust all under Heaven to it.
If one can lodge all under Heaven,

*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"The people of Yue slew their lord for three generations.
Kingzi Sou was distressed by this,
and fled to the Danxue Grotto.
When Yue had no lord,
they sought Kingzi Sou but found him not,
and followed him to the Danxue Grotto.
Kingzi Sou refused to emerge,
so the people of Yue fumigated him with mugwort
and conveyed him in a jade carriage.
Kingzi Sou grasped the reins and ascended the chariot,
gazing heavenward, he cried:
*'Lord! Lord!
Why must you not abandon me?'*

*Kingzi Sou did not hate being lord—
he hated the exhaustion of being lord.*
*If Kingzi Sou were such,*
he could be said to have not let the state exhaust his life—
this was indeed what the people of Yue desired as their lord."*

**Chapter Fourteen: "Seeing Yet Not Seeing"**
This chapter, *The Chongxu Jing* [□ Heavenly Auspices Section], speaks:
*"In ancient times, the sage followed the Dao of yin-yang and emptiness-nothingness,
to govern the manifest forms of Heaven and Earth.*

*Heaven and Earth, with their manifest forms,*
are born from the Dao of yin-yang’s formlessness—
thus, Heaven and Earth’s manifest forms arise from the Dao of yin-yang’s formlessness,
and so there is the *Taiyi* [Great Unity],
the *Tai Chu* [Great Primordial],
the *Tai Shi* [Great Beginning],
and the *Tai Su* [Great Primitive].

*Thus, the Taiyi is the beginning of the spirit,
the Tai Chu is the beginning of qi,
the Tai Shi is the beginning of form,
and the Tai Su is the beginning of substance.
When Heaven and Earth’s qi, form, and substance are complete yet separate,
it is called *Hun Dun* [Chaotic Primordium].
*Hun Dun* refers to Heaven and Earth’s mingling and separation in formlessness.

*Seeing yet not seeing,*
hearing yet not hearing,
pursuing yet not attaining—
this is called the *Great Dao*.
*The Dao has no form yet stands firm,*
and so it is called *natural*.
If one can shape the primordial transformations of antiquity,
the beginning of Heaven and Earth,
the mingling of yin and yang in chaotic formlessness—
this is called the *Tai Ji* [Great Polarity].
The eightfold principles of the Great Dao
are the first of its eighty-one chapters."*

**Seeing yet not seeing** is named *Yi* [Equality/Level],
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"The Tai Chu is the beginning of qi,
and seeing yet not seeing."*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Tai Chu has a name—
seeing yet not seeing its hue,
this is called the *qi of emptiness-nothingness*.
*Substance emerges from emptiness,*
and though formless, it gives Heaven and Earth their colors."*

**Hearing yet not hearing** is named *Xi* [Rarity],
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"The Taiyi is the beginning of the spirit,
and hearing yet not hearing.
It is also called the *Tai Ji*."*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Tai Ji has a name—
hearing yet not hearing its sound,
this is called the *ultimate subtlety of yin-yang*.
*Life emerges from nothingness,*
and though soundless, it gives Heaven and Earth their voices."*

**Pursuing yet not attaining** is named *Wei* [Faintness],
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"The Tai Shi is the beginning of vital essence,
and pursuing yet not attaining."*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Tai Shi has a name—
pursuing yet not attaining its form,
this is called the *subtle and mysterious state*.
*Form emerges from the faint,*
and though formless, it gives Heaven and Earth their shapes."*

These three—
they cannot be fully explained,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Great Dao of yin-yang,
in stillness, has no body, no hue, no sound—
thus, the Dao cannot be spoken of."*

The Dao is without inquiry,
inquiry without response.
*The *Zhuangzi* says:
"The Dao is without inquiry,
inquiry without response—
it cannot be spoken of.
The mind is perplexed and cannot know,
the mouth falters and cannot discuss.
Thus, it blends into oneness.
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Tai Su is the origin of substance—
when Heaven and Earth’s qi and form are present yet undivided,
this is called *Hun Dun* [Primordial Chaos].
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Before Heaven and Earth took form,
they blended into oneness.
Above, it was not bright,
below, it was not dark.
*The *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The Dao is formless,
prior to the Tai Ji yet not exalted,
beneath the Six Extremes yet not profound.
It cannot be named—
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Dao, when most vast, has no form or measure,
the Primordial, when most vast, has no bounds.
Returning to nothingness,
this is called the *form of no-form*.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Dao is the harmonious qi of yin-yang—
this is the *form of no-form*.
Without substance’s image,
this is called *vague and elusive*.
*The *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The Dao of non-being,
without form or appearance,
dimly vast and empty—
all day you gaze yet see no color,
listen yet hear no sound,
grasp yet find no shape—
this is called the *vague and empty Dao*.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Dao of Heaven is vague and elusive, boundless,
far yet unending, near yet without limit—
this is called the *Great Dao’s essence*.
Approaching it, you see no head,
following it, you see no tail.
*The *Zhuangzi* says:
*"The Dao of Great Clarity
has no beginning, no end—
no beginning, no end at all.
Holding to the ancient Dao
to govern the present.
*The *Zhuangzi* says:
*"Ran Qiu asked Confucius:
*'Before Heaven and Earth existed,
could one know the ancient beginning?*
The Master replied:
*'Yes. The ancient Dao is like today’s Heaven and Earth—
no ancient, no present,
no beginning, no end.
To know the ancient beginning,
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Of old, the sages followed the Dao of yin-yang’s emptiness,
to govern Heaven and Earth’s forms.
Heaven and Earth arise from the Dao of yin-yang’s emptiness—
this is called *Dao Ji* [Dao’s framework].
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Thus, Heaven and Earth’s forms arise from the Dao’s yin-yang’s formlessness,
giving rise to Tai Yi, Tai Ji, Tai Chu, Tai Shi, Tai Su—
this is called *Dao Ji* [Dao’s framework].

**Chapter Fifteen: The Ancient Virtuous Scholar**
This chapter from the *Tongxuan Jing* [Guard Weakness Section] says:
*"Of old, the Three Sovereigns had a warning vessel called the *You Zhi*—
when empty, it stood upright;
when full, it overturned.
Things at their peak decline,
the sun at zenith moves,
the moon at full wanes,
music ending turns to sorrow.
Thus, the wise and learned guard with simplicity,
the many-knowing and eloquent guard with restraint,
those who bestow virtue widely guard with modesty,
the mighty and bold guard with caution,
the wealthy and noble guard with frugality.
These seven principles
are how the ancient kings governed without action,
thus enduring forever.
Those who follow this Dao do not seek fullness—
only by not seeking fullness
does one avoid ruin when new things arise.
The ancient sages who governed well
held to the seven guards of humility.

The ancient virtuous scholar,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* [On Benevolence Section] says:
*"The ancient governors of the world
were subtle, mysterious, and penetrating.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The sage holds the Dao,
empty, still, subtle, and mysterious,
thus perfecting virtue without action,
penetrating and vast, yet nothing is left undone.
Deep and unknowable.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Without action yet nothing left undone,
thus governing the world with tolerance.
Only the unknowable
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"He who attains tolerance
acts without action yet achieves;
he who fails to attain tolerance
must act, and disaster follows.
Thus, he strives for tolerance:
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Thus, he governs the world with tolerance.
He says:
*'Cautious as one crossing a great river in winter,
hesitant as one fearing four enemies,
solemn as a guest,
melting like ice about to thaw,
plain as uncarved wood,
muddy as turbid water,
vast as a valley—
this is governing with tolerance.
Cautious as winter crossing,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Cautious as one crossing a great river in winter—
not daring to move forward,
retiring without presuming to lead,
guarding retreat and yielding.
Hesitant as fearing four enemies,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Hesitant as fearing four enemies—
fearing injury,
guarding softness and weakness, not daring to boast.
Solemn as a guest,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Solemn as a guest—
humble, reverent, respectful.
Humble, reverent, respectful—
self-effacing, honoring others.
Melting like ice about to thaw,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Melting like ice about to thaw—
not daring to hoard,
self-depleting, not daring to be firm.
Plain as uncarved wood,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Plain as uncarved wood—
not daring to be complete,
self-deficient, not daring to be whole.
Vast as a valley,
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Vast as a valley—
not daring to overfill,
seeing insufficiency, not daring to exalt oneself.
Muddy as turbid water.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Muddy as turbid water—
not daring to be clear and pure.
Not daring to be clear and pure—
dwelling in filth and humiliation, not daring to be fresh.
Who can settle turbidity with stillness, slowly clarifying?
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Turbidity settled with stillness, slowly clarifying.
Who can endure stability with prolonged motion, slowly giving life?
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The empty, when hollowed, slowly fills.
Those who preserve this Dao
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The Dao retreats, thus it leads,
guards softness and weakness, thus it prevails,
self-effaces, thus it is exalted,
self-depletes, thus it is firm,
self-deficits, thus it is complete,
sees insufficiency, thus it assists,
dwells in filth and humiliation, thus it is renewed.
Those who follow this Dao do not seek fullness."*

Not desiring fullness.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Of old, the Three Sovereigns had a warning vessel called the *yòuzhī* cup—
when empty, it stands upright;
when full, it overturns.
Only by not being full
does one avoid ruin while preserving what is new and complete.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Only by not being full
does one avoid ruin while preserving what is new and complete,
for the Dao acts without action yet accomplishes all."*

**Chapter Sixteen: *Zhì Xū Jí* (Extreme Emptiness)**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* states:
*"Ancient worthies lived long without artificial exercises,
for they neither forgot the heart nor cultivated the spirit,
neither neglected the Dao nor guarded stillness—
this is the Way of Heaven and Earth,
the virtue of the Sage’s non-action.
Thus it is said:
*'Serene, tranquil, still, and without contrivance,
comparable to the evenness of Heaven and Earth,
the simplicity of the Dao’s virtue—
the Sage rests thought and quiets the mind,
plain and unadorned, serene and undisturbed,
so that worries cannot enter,
evil *qi* cannot assail.
Thus their virtue remains whole and their spirit undiminished,
and their years grow long.
Thus, pure and unmixed,
still and unchanging,
undisturbed and without contrivance—
this is the Way of nurturing the spirit.*

*The Dao acts without action,
can be transmitted but not received,
can be attained but not seen.*

*Pengzu obtained it from the Dao,
from the age of You to the Five Hegemons—
a span of eight hundred years.
The Venerable Laozi obtained it from the Dao,
living over five hundred years—
no one knows his end.

Ancient Sages, guarding stillness,
extended their lives to a thousand years.*

**Extreme Emptiness**
*The *Tongxuan Jing* [*□ Dao Yuan Pian*] says:
*"Ancient worthies valued self-cultivation over governing others—
nearly attaining the Dao.
Thus it is said: *Extreme emptiness*,
*steadfast stillness*.
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Those who guard steadfast stillness and act without contrivance
are those who guard stillness.*
*The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Nothing surpasses emptiness,
nothing surpasses stillness.
Emptiness and stillness—
this is attaining their abode.*

*All things arise together,*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"All things arise together—
spring births, summer grows.*
*I observe their return.*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"I observe their return—
autumn harvests, winter stores.*

*All things, teeming and active,*
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Spring’s breath unfolds, and all grasses sprout—
all things teem and move.*
*Each returns to its root.*
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"When autumn arrives, all treasures mature in name,
returning to their root in stillness.*

*Returning to the root is called stillness,*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Grasses and trees have roots—
thus, twice-bearing trees, their roots must be injured.*
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Uprooted plants wither past half their height,
yet they do not know their ailment.
Stillness can mend the sick,
massaging the eyes can rejuvenate the aged,
tranquility can halt sudden death.
Stillness is called *returning to the Mandate*.
*The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Now you are but a thing—
how can you return to your root?
It is easy—
only the Great Person can do so.*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"In the ancient past, True Persons inhaled and exhaled yin and yang,
and all living things looked up to their virtue in harmony.
At that time, their essence was hidden and profound,
self-formed in purity and simplicity,
before purity dispersed and all things thrived in ease.*

*Returning to the Mandate is called constancy,*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"From the august Fu Xi to Shennong to the Yellow Emperor,
dimly aware, diligently striving,
each sought to shed their childlike ignorance
and awaken between Heaven and Earth—
their virtue was harmonious but not unified.*

*Knowing constancy is called clarity.*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"In the age of the Five Emperors and the Xia-Yin,
they grasped Heaven and Earth’s principles,
regulated the four seasons,
harmonized the yin fields,
and thus all people trembled in reverence,
listening and watching in awe—
governed yet without chaos.*

*In the age of the Three Kings—Xia, Yin, and Zhou—
their desires clung to things,
their brilliance was seduced by externals,
and their life and fate lost their truth.
Not knowing constancy,*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"When the Zhou declined,
purity dispersed, simplicity departed,
the Dao became the falsehood of literature,
virtue became the ritual of music,
cleverness and artifice arose,
ancestral teachings doubted the Sage,
ornate deception coerced the masses,
and the world became muddled and chaotic.
Frivolous action brings ruin.*
*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"In the Warring States, the feudal lords rebelled,
the people took warfare as their trade,
disasters arose, calamities multiplied.
Raising armies was difficult,
sieging cities led to indiscriminate slaughter—
countless tens of thousands died in battle,
the aged and weak perished from hunger and cold beyond reckoning.
From then on, the world never again knew peace in life and fate.*

*Knowing constancy is called tolerance,*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:*
*"When stillness prevails, there is harmony;
when emptiness is embraced, there is flow.
The highest virtue is non-action,
and all things are contained within it.
To contain is to be impartial.*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:*
*"Those who seek to cling to reputation
must create affairs.
When affairs arise, they abandon impartiality for private gain,
forsake the Dao and indulge their desires—
thus, the Way of Impartiality prevails while private desires are blocked.
To be impartial is to be sovereign.*

*The *Nanhua Jing* says:*
*"In stillness, one becomes a Sage;
in action, one becomes a sovereign—
yet both are rooted in non-action.
To be sovereign is to align with Heaven.*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:*
*"The clear and empty—
that is Heaven’s luminosity.
Heaven’s luminosity is the Dao’s mirror.*

*The *Dongling Jing* says:*
*"The empty is the Dao’s essence;
the still is the Dao’s foundation.
The Dao endures eternally.*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:*
*"The Dao of emptiness and stillness—
Heaven’s vastness, Earth’s longevity.
Though the body perishes, it does not fail.*

*The *Tongxuan Jing* says:*
*"The root of nurturing life lies in emptiness and stillness.*

*The *Nanhua Jing* says:*
*"Great non-action governs the world,
small non-action nurtures the body and life."*

*End of Volume II, *Ancient Commentary on the Four Daoist Classics of* The *Dao/Way* and Its *Virtue/Power**.*

#1 *Essence of the Dao and Upholding Non-Action*: The character *Dao* was originally omitted; added according to the Qing-printed edition.

#2 *Ancient Shang’s Talent and Quality*: Some editions suspect the omission of the phrase *"Bo Le (伯乐) was skilled in treating horses"* after the word *"say."*

**Volume III, *The Essence of the Dao and Upholding Non-Action*: *Ancient Shang’s Talent and Quality***

**Chapter Seventeen of the *Tao/Way*’s Supreme Perfection**
This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* states:
In ancient times, the sage-kings addressed flaws and reformed institutions,
completed matters and then altered their approach;
their excellence lay in harmony,
their shortcoming in *authoritative measures* (权).
Later generations’ Dao says:
Without laws, governance cannot be established;
without knowing *rites and moral principles* (礼义), laws cannot be formed.
Laws can kill the unfilial,
but cannot make people filial;
can punish thieves,
but cannot make people virtuous.
When the sage-kings ruled above,
they clearly showed what was good and evil,
guided the people with *non-punitive* (经非誉) instruction,
advanced the worthy and virtuous,
relegated the unworthy and base,
and laid aside punishments, never using them.
The Three Kings made the world fear punishments and dare not steal or plunder—
how could this compare to the ancient Dao’s simplicity and purity,
where there was no thievish heart at all?
Teaching should not be harsh;
removing harshness brings self-regulation.
If one does not know the principle of *non-action* (无为) for the individual,
but instead relies on *punishments and governance* (刑政) as a remedy,
this is no different from
digging a channel to stop water,
embracing firewood to extinguish flames—
the more you add, the more it overflows;
thus, *stirring to calm* (扬止沸) only makes the disturbance worse.
Those who know the principle of *non-action* governance
need only remove the fire.
Hence it is said:
*I uphold non-action and the people transform themselves,*
*I love stillness and the people become upright.*
The Supreme Perfection—
the people below know its existence.
The *Tongxuan Jing*’s *Natural Chapter* (□自然篇) says:
The Supreme Perfection is *spontaneous transformation* (神化);
in ancient times, the Three Sovereigns (三皇) nurtured this transformation,
issuing no commands yet the people followed.
When the sage-kings ruled above,
they clearly showed what was good and evil,
laid aside punishments, never using them—
this was the foundation of governance.
Courts were uncluttered, with no litigious disputes,
fields were cultivated, with no pretense of martial tools;
thus, the Supreme Perfection—
the people below know its existence.

**The Next Level:**
They were close to them and praised them.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The next level was the Five Emperors (五帝), who issued commands,
preventing wrongdoing but without rewards or punishments.
The next level:
They were feared and reviled by them.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The next level was the Three Kings (三王), who relied on punishments and rewards,
levying punishments to suppress violence,
granting rewards to encourage virtue.
The people feared punishments and dared not act wrongly—
this was not *law and order* (法令) being upright above,
but the people below submitting in fear;
this was the end of governance.
*Trust was insufficient,*
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*When words are the same, trust lies in their sincerity—*
*sincerity precedes words;*
*when commands are the same, obedience lies in their truth—*
*truth lies beyond the commands.*
The Xia ruler did not break his word,
the Shang people swore oaths,
the Zhou people made alliances.
Yet there were still cases of dishonesty.
The *Dongling Jing* says:
This shows that the world’s dishonesty
comes from rulers not being diligent and clear.
*It was because they valued words.*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*When the ruler speaks, the subjects act;*
*when the subjects speak, the ruler acts.*
*When the ruler speaks, it is always applied;*
*when the subjects speak, it is applied contingently.*
Ancient sage-kings knew how to use *contingent measures* (权),
so when they spoke, it must be believed,
and their words must always be fulfilled.
When their deeds were accomplished and their purposes realized,
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*Ancient sage-kings*
*refined themselves inwardly*
*and governed the people outwardly;*
*when their deeds were accomplished and their purposes established,*
*they dwelled beside Heaven,*
*upholding non-action yet achieving all things.*
The people said they acted *spontaneously*.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*Sincerity is the word of the virtuous;*
