*loyalty is the intention of the virtuous.*
When loyalty and sincerity manifest inwardly,
they move and respond outwardly—
this is the transformation of the worthy and sage.

---

**Chapter Eighteen: The Great Dao Declines**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* states:
In ancient times, during the era of the Great Dao and supreme virtue,
the time of *He Xiu* (赫胥氏),
the people did not know what to do,
their actions had no fixed direction;
they carried their food in their mouths and walked joyfully,
they drummed their bellies and wandered at ease.
The people could thus remain pure and simple,
possessing loyalty and filial piety.
When the Three Kings (三王) suspended *benevolence and righteousness* (仁义) to soothe the world’s hearts,
the Great Dao declined, yet *benevolence and righteousness* arose,
and the six relationships (六亲) became discordant.
Then the Five Hegemons (五霸) bent and twisted *rites and music* (礼乐) to rectify the world’s punishments,
and *wisdom* emerged, bringing great *deceit* (伪).
States became chaotic and confused,
and the people began to strain for cleverness,
competing to gather *skillful advantages* (巧利),
unable to be restrained.
This was also the fault of the Three Kings’ *benevolence and righteousness*—
though they achieved minor success,
their teaching of cleverness and wisdom bred *thieves and deceit* (贼诈) among the people.
The Three Kings relied on *punishments and governance* (刑政) under the pretense of the Dao,
and took shelter in *righteousness* (义).
*Benevolence and righteousness* were the Three Kings’ *temporary dwelling* (蓬庐),
sufficient only for a night’s stay,
not for long-term residence—
they were not the enduring Dao.
When the Great Dao declined,
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
*In ancient times, during the era of the Great Dao and supreme virtue,*
*rectitude was natural, yet they did not call it benevolence;*
*mutual love was natural, yet they did not call it righteousness.*
*Following one’s nature was called the Dao;*
*attaining one’s natural endowment was called virtue/power (德).*
Thus, the Five Emperors’ Dao dispersed into human virtue/power (德).
*Benevolence and righteousness* arose;
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Three Kings’ virtue overflowed into *benevolence and righteousness*,
and when *benevolence and righteousness* were established,
the Dao and its virtue/power (德) declined.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*The Three Kings’ governance of the world*
*was called "benevolence and righteousness through action,"*
*and their governance brought the greatest chaos.*
When *benevolence and righteousness* advanced the worthy,
the people turned to *mutual deceit* (相贼),
insufficient to enrich the people—
sons began to kill their fathers.

When *wisdom* emerged,
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*The Five Hegemons’ Dao narrowed,*
*later relying on rites and wisdom.*
Those who relied on wisdom
brought disorder to the hearts of the people;
the wise produced *fraudulence* (奸),
and *fraudulent customs* (奸俗) became the wind of *state collapse* (亡国).
*Great deceit* arose;
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
*When the Five Hegemons’ rites and music spread widely,*
*the seven states waged war with their armies,*
*and the world fell into chaos.*
When rites and music relied on wisdom,
the people turned to *mutual deceit* (相盗),
insufficient to enrich the people—
subjects began to kill their rulers.

When the six relationships (六亲) became discordant,
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
*The six relationships were discordant,*
*the old and young lacked order—*
*this was the concern of the common people.*
*Filial piety and kindness* arose;
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
*In ancient times, during the era of the Great Dao and supreme virtue,*
*filial sons did not flatter their parents—*
*this was the height of a son’s virtue;*
*serving one’s parents brought kindness and filial piety.*

When states became chaotic and confused,
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
*The court lacked loyal ministers—*
*this was the concern of chaotic states and the lords.*
*Loyal ministers* arose;
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
*In ancient times, during the era of the Great Dao and supreme virtue,*
*loyal ministers did not flatter their rulers—*
*this was the height of a minister’s virtue;*
*serving one’s ruler brought loyalty and steadfastness.*

**Chapter Nineteen: "Discarding the Sage and Abandoning Wisdom"**

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, the sages established teachings and governed by first examining their ultimate outcomes, seeing how they fostered virtue. In later ages, when the people knew scriptures, the Dao declined; when they knew calculations, virtue declined; when they knew contracts, trust declined; when they knew mechanical arts, substance declined.

Minor cleverness harms the true; minor skill harms the Way; petty debate ruins governance; harshness injures virtue. In ancient times, the Great Way was unobstructed and straightforward, so the people were easily guided. In the age of perfect governance, all were at ease, so the lower ranks did not harm the higher. In the age of supreme loyalty, they returned to simplicity, and the people had no deceit or concealment.

When they composed written records, it was to oversee and manage all affairs—so the simple remembered, and the wise recorded events. But when [governance] declined, they used deceitful writings to exonerate the guilty and condemn the innocent.

Those who scrutinize the traces of ink and parchment do not know the principle of non-action as the root of order and chaos. Cunning and deceit took root, thieves and bandits multiplied, and the more the ruler desired, the more the people schemed.

To use writing and cleverness to generate solutions is like stirring water and hoping for clarity—it is difficult. Thus, to stop the boiling, one removes the fire. Those who know the principle of non-action as the root of governance say: *"I take no action, yet the people govern themselves."*

**Discard the Sage, abandon wisdom.**
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
And yet, do you delight in the sage? That aids the people’s artifices.
Do you delight in wisdom? That aids the people’s flaws.

Thus it is said: *"Discard the sage, abandon wisdom, and the world will flourish greatly. The people’s benefit will be a hundredfold."*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Scholars of writing are the source of speech. Speech arises from wisdom, and the clever, though skilled in deceit, do not know the Dao. The people become chaotic through falsehood.

**Discard benevolence, abandon righteousness.**
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
And yet, do you delight in benevolence? That disrupts the people’s virtue.
Do you delight in righteousness? That contradicts the people’s natural order.
The people return to filial piety and kindness.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Abandoning benevolence and righteousness, the people revert to filial piety and kindness, and thus the world’s virtue begins to harmonize in its primal unity. When people embrace their inherent virtue, the world becomes unerring.

**Discard cleverness, abandon profit.**
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
And yet, do you delight in ritual? That aids the people’s artifices.
Do you delight in music? That aids the people’s excesses.
Do you delight in sharp hearing? That makes the people indulge in sound.
Do you delight in bright vision? That makes the people indulge in color.
Then thieves and bandits will cease.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music—selecting the worthy and employing the wise—are insufficient to enrich the people. The people are overly eager for profit, stealing even in broad daylight, digging through walls at noon. Thus it is said: *"Discard cleverness, abandon profit, and great thefts will cease."*

These three [principles]—
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
When the people’s strength and wisdom are exhausted, they rely on deceit. The more they scheme, the more falsehood spreads, and how can the common people avoid it? When strength is insufficient, they resort to deceit; when wisdom is insufficient, they resort to cunning. If deceit and falsehood prevail, whom can one blame? Blame the ruler’s fondness for cleverness and wisdom.

They say: *"The written word is insufficient,"*
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
To value ornate language, to discard substance, to drown in cleverness—this is when the people begin to be confused by superficiality. Without returning to their true nature, it is insufficient to enrich the people.
Thus, this is what is said today.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Mountains produce gold, stones produce jade—
Yet they erode each other in turn.
Humans cultivate learning,
Yet it harms them in return.
Each pursues what they fancy,
Only to injure themselves in turn.

When the ruler is troubled, the people are unsettled;
When the ruler demands much, the people contend.
To govern through learning and then suppress learning—
It is like fanning flames and hoping they will not burn.
This is difficult indeed.

Thus, to stop boiling by adding more water
Only makes the boiling worse.
Those who know the root of governance
Remove the fire itself.

Therefore, abandoning sage-wisdom,
Discarding virtue and righteousness,
Rejecting the worthy and capable,
Erasing artificial conventions,
Prohibiting deceit and falsehood—
Then the virtuous and the base alike align with the Dao.

**Seeing the Unadorned**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, the sage-governed
Practiced clarity and stillness—
True and unadorned,
Empty and effortless,
Returning to simplicity,
Unmixed with the world.

**Embracing Simplicity**
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Ancient sages illuminated the unadorned,
Practiced non-action and returned to simplicity—
Simple and unadorned,
Yet none could rival their excellence.

**Few Private Interests**
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
In the southern lands of Yue,
There was a realm called Jiande,
Where the people were simple and unadorned,
Few in private desires,
Knowing to act but not to hoard,
Giving without seeking return,
Unaware of where righteousness lay,
Unconcerned with where propriety led—
Yet their reckless freedom
Aligned with the Great Way.

**Few Desires**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Ancient sages acted without effort,
Yet the people transformed naturally;
They were detached without desire,
Yet the people returned to simplicity.

---
**Chapter Twenty: "Abandon Learning, Be Without Worry"**
The *Tongxuan Jing* speaks:
In the latter ages, great academies are established to teach—
This is but a skill of governance.
When the Dao is attained, learning is practiced;
When the Dao is lost, learning is abandoned.

All things have their tension and release,
Their flourishing and decay.
When decline comes, they drift without return,
Indulging in lust and color,
Ignoring proper law,
Their corruption spreading to later generations,
Until kingdoms fall—
This is the result of their learning.

To lose the Dao and rely on learning is perilous;
To abandon virtue and employ talent is to invite hardship.
To govern through cleverness is to labor the mind without clarity;
To govern through cunning is to exhaust the mind without success.

When the ruler studies much, the people labor much;
When the ruler is troubled, the people are disordered.
To govern through learning and then suppress learning—
It is like fanning flames and hoping they will not burn.
Thus, abandon learning, be without worry.

The *Tongxuan Jing* [□Dao Yuan] says:
Books of learning are the source of spoken words.
Words arise from cleverness,
And cleverness is deceit and falsehood—
Unknowing of the Dao.
Thus, abandon learning, be without worry.

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Abandon learning, discard books—
Then there will be no worries.

---
**Yes and Ah**
The *Nanhua Jing* recounts:
Laozi asked Nan Rongzi:
*"You come from Chu, do you not?"*
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
[Laozi] used cleverness in his questioning,
Yet Nan Rongzi, in his madness and bending,
Replied, *"Ah!"*
How far apart were they?

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
In the latter ages, the nine schools of scholars
Clutch their texts,
Hold fast to doctrine and law,
Hoping to govern by them—
Like gripping a square handle to fit a round hole,
Seeking what is proper—
This is difficult indeed.

---
**Good and Evil**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Ancient practitioners of the Dao—
Deep practice was called *virtue and power*,
Shallow practice was called *righteousness and justice*,
Thin practice was called *rites and wisdom*.
How far apart are they?

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Anciently, one must first attain *virtue and power*;
Cunning schemes were not employed.
One must return to Heaven’s way,
Govern through non-action—
This is the utmost in peaceful governance.

---
**What People Fear**
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□Shang Ren] recounts:
Wenzi asked:
*"What conduct makes the people love their ruler?"*
Laozi replied:
*"Govern them with due timing and reverence,
As if standing at the edge of a deep abyss,
As if walking on thin ice.
Between Heaven and Earth,
The good are my nurtured kin,
The unrighteous are my foes—
Thus, what people fear,
One must indeed fear."*

**It Cannot But Be Feared**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times,
The ministers of Xia and Shang
Turned against their tyrants, Jié and Zhòu,
And served Tāng and Wǔ instead.
The people of Hù Hóng
Attacked their own descendants
And returned to Shén Nóng.
Thus, what people fear,
Cannot but be feared.

**Endless Wasteland**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
In the latter ages, to govern without the Dao
Is to harm the world.
To make an enemy of the people—
This cannot be done.
Endless wasteland.

---
**The Common Folk Rejoice**
The *Dongling Jing* says:
In the latter ages, when rulers employ people,
They value those whose ears hear well,
Whose eyes see clearly,
Whose words can be spoken—
Thus, the people compete to gain praise,
Adorning their speech to be heard—
Governance fails.

**Like Feasting on the Grand Sacrifice**
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□Ji Cheng] says:
Through diligent and laborious cultivation,
Ten days without food,
Yet it is like feasting on the grand sacrifice—
Learning without satiety,
This is how one sustains oneself.

**Like Ascending Spring Terrace**
The *Dongling Jing* says:
In the latter ages, when rulers employ people,
They value those whose ears hear well—
Then the people transport goods for profit,
Competing for praise.
They value those whose eyes see clearly—
Then the people display their forms and skills,
Striving to advance.
They value those whose words can be spoken—
Then the people practice flattery,
Adorning their speech.

**I Alone Fear**
The *Dongling Jing* says:
In ancient times, the sage-kings employed people
Who were valued not for what their ears heard,
Not for what their eyes saw,
Not for what their mouths could speak—
Yet the people acted effortlessly and naturally.

---
**Its Onset Unseen**
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Scholars learn what they should not learn—
Until they attain the Dao—
Like an infant before it knows harm.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
A three-month-old infant,
Unknowing of gain or loss,
Unspeaking yet transformative—
How vast is this change!

**The Wanderer**

*"Buying Buying"*—as if having nowhere to return.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
To dwell is to act without purpose,
To move is to go without destination,
To undertake is to ride without aim,
To stir is to halt without intent—
This is called the Dao.
The common folk all have excess,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The common people’s learning is laborious,
They listen yet lack clarity,
They see yet fail to discern,
Their offices are untrustworthy,
Their actions bring no order,
Their deeds achieve nothing.
Yet I alone seem abandoned.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Sage’s Dao is simple and humble,
Not listening yet clear,
Not seeing yet insightful,
Not speaking yet trustworthy,
Not acting yet bringing order,
Not doing yet achieving success.
I am but a foolish man’s heart—
Pure and unadulterated.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The vulgar scholars of old,
The Sage did not follow.
The common folk are bright and discerning,
The *Dongling Jing* says:
In the latter ages, those who take people
By their sharp hearing and keen sight—
Their offices multiply yet governance is chaotic,
And all vie for profit.
I alone seem dim.
The *Dongling Jing* says:
In ancient times, those who sought
Unseen, unheard, unspeakable talent—
Their officials were upright yet diligent in virtue,
And the people transformed without words.
Alas, does the ruler know this?
I alone am obscure and veiled.
The *Dongling Jing* says:
In ancient times, when the Way was followed,
Those who sought unseen, unheard, unspeakable talent—
Their officials were upright yet diligent in virtue,
And the people transformed without words.
Alas, does the ruler know this?
Dark as if in eclipse,
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The ancients’ Sages had hearts like dead ashes,
Obscure and dim,
No scheming in mind—
This was their *wuwei*.
Silent, yet as if endless.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The heavens have light—
They do not fear the people’s dimness;
The earth has wealth—
They do not fear the people’s poverty.
Only by following the natural Way of heaven and earth,
Can one master the principles.
The common folk all have means,
The *Nanhua Jing*—Laozi says:
All take what comes first—
The sharp are destroyed.
All seek fortune—
The keen are blunted.
I alone am stubborn, like the vulgar.
The *Nanhua Jing*—Laozi says:
I alone take what comes last,
And receive the world’s defilements;
I alone bend yet remain whole,
And escape blame.
I alone differ from others,
The *Nanhua Jing*—Laozi says:
All take what is real—
I alone take what is void,
With depth as my root,
With restraint as my measure,
And laugh at cleverness without action.
Yet I cherish the nourishment of the Mother.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
To always be broad and accommodating toward things,
Yet never diminished by others—
This can be called the utmost extreme.
Laozi! The ancient sage of boundless truth.

**The Sage’s Virtue and Form (Chapter 21)**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* (from the "□至乐篇" [*Zhi Le Pian*]) speaks:
The Dao is formless yet gives rise to the formed heaven and earth,
Vague and indistinct yet never exceeds existence.
Indistinct yet vague—
Yet no image can be found.
Examining its Way, it first arises yet originally has no arising,
Originally has no *qi*, originally has no form.
Mixed amid the indistinct, vague, and void—
Void transforms into spirit,
Spirit transforms into *qi*,
*Qi* transforms into form,
Form transforms into life—
This is the interplay of spring, summer, autumn, and winter, the four seasons.
Thus it is said: *"Yǎo míng wú xíng"*—profoundly formless,
Yet within it is essence.
Its essence is most true,
Within it are the four seasons’ verities—
This is the Dao’s true essence.
The Sage’s virtue and form,
Follow the Dao alone.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Great Constant Dao,
Flows and permeates,
Never moves in vain,
With yielding and contracting,
With the ebb and flow of yin and yang.
The Dao, as a thing,
Is but faint and fleeting.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Before heaven and earth took form,
Profound and dark,
Faint and fleeting.
Fleeting and faint—
Yet within them are images.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Fleeting and faint—
Images cannot be fixed.
Faint and fleeting—
Yet within them is substance.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Faint and fleeting—
Their application is unyielding.
Profound and dark—
Yet within them is essence.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Again, obtaining the harmonious *qi* of yin and yang,
Only then can heaven and earth, the myriad things, arise and flourish.
Within it is verity.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Dao is formless—
Yet within it is verity.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The Dao has sentiment and verity—
This is the interplay of spring, summer, autumn, and winter, the four seasons.
From ancient times to now,
Its name does not fade.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The four seasons as markers,
From ancient times to now,
Never changing or altering—
This is called the natural principle of heaven.
To observe the myriad people.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Dao stands and gives rise to the myriad things,
Thus the myriad things’ totality,
All pass through the Dao’s single aperture;
The myriad affairs’ roots,
All emerge from the Dao’s single gate.
How do I know the myriad people are thus?
Because of this.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Yin and yang, the four seasons,
Give rise and form to the myriad things.

**Bending Preserves Wholeness (Chapter 22)**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* (from the "□天地篇" [*Tian Di Pian*]) speaks:
The ancients who nurtured the world,
One person without desire—
Yet the world is sufficient;
One ruler without action—
Yet the world is transformed;
One sovereign profound and tranquil—
Yet the people are settled.
Unified in One,
All affairs are complete.
Thus the ancient Sages feigned crookedness and harm to their own abilities,
Embraced the ruler’s *wuwei* as the method
To govern the world,
And the myriad things transformed of their own accord.
Few officials yet governance was clear—
They did not boast of their many talents.

**Bending Preserves Wholeness (Chapter 22 continued)**

Bending preserves wholeness.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The bent seeks to stretch—
Bend an inch to stretch a foot.
The noble one does this.

Crookedness brings straightness.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The crooked seeks to straighten—
A small crook yields great straightness.
The noble one does this.

Hollow makes full.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient skilled rulers modeled themselves on the rivers and seas,
Hollow and low, to achieve their vastness.
Those who follow this Dao do not desire fullness.

Worn becomes new.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Only because it is not full does it renew itself without ceasing.

Fewness suffices.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The affairs of the ancients were few.
What is meant by "few affairs"?
Grasp the essential to match the many,
Hold the simple to govern the broad.
Fewness is the economy of what is held—
Thus, with the Dao to guide it, even fewness suffices to govern.

Excess brings confusion.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The affairs of today’s people are many.
What is meant by "many abilities"?
Cultivated in both martial and civil arts,
Balanced in action and stillness,
Every move and pause fittingly placed—
Many abilities seem to govern all,
Yet the more they seek, the less they gain, unaware of their loss.
Thus, though many, they bring disorder.

Therefore, the Sage:
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancients—when unified in One, all affairs were complete.
The ancients knew One, and the world knew none unknown.
Later ages did not know One, and the world knew none truly.
Embrace One as the model for the world.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancients held to *wuwei* and One—
Hold One, and governance succeeds;
Lose One, and chaos follows.
One is the Dao of no preference,
The root of all things.
Thus, to lose One is worse than to have no ruler.
A ruler must hold to One to govern the people,
Yet tremble and fear daily,
Ever cautious—
Thus, through *wuwei*, the Dao of One is fulfilled.
What is held is strict,
What is governed is vast.

Not self-visible, hence clear;
*The Tongxuan Jing* [*Natural Section*] says:
Not self-visible, hence clear.

Not self-righteous, hence manifest;
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Not self-righteous, hence manifest.

Not self-praising, hence功 (*gōng*) achieved;
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Not self-praising, hence功 (*gōng*) achieved.

Not self-proud, hence enduring.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Not self-proud, hence enduring.

Only the non-contending:
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient skilled rulers practiced *wuwei* and non-contention,
Thus none could contend with them.
Therefore, none in the world could contend with them.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Plain and unadorned, none could contend with their beauty.

Was the saying "Bending preserves wholeness" mere empty words?
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient rulers’ Dao was not for having,
But for *wuwei*.
The wise do not take possession as their affair,
The benevolent do not take position as favor—
This is called *wuwei* and One.
Truly whole, returning to it.

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The ancients valued *wuwei*—
Above, *wuwei*; below, also *wuwei*.

---
**Silent Speech of Nature (Chapter 23)**

This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* speaks:
In the later ages, kings and feudal lords treat the world and their states as their own,
The myriad things as their livestock,
Harboring the world’s vastness,
Possessing the myriad’s abundance—
Their *qi* is full, their wills arrogant,
Like sudden winds and torrential rains, unable to endure.

Thus, the ancient Sages embraced softness and weakness,
Dared not the sudden winds and violent rains,
And so endured.

Modeling Heaven’s non-anger,
Following the Dao as good—
The world endures.

Silent speech of nature.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Those skilled in anger invite many grievances;
Only by following Heaven and Earth’s natural course can one attain sage wisdom.

Sudden winds do not last a morning,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Though the seas overflow, they do not exceed three days.
Sudden winds and torrential rains do not last a day,
Nor even a moment in midday.

Who says this?
Heaven and Earth.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Sudden winds and torrential rains—
They unleash the force of tyranny.
Heaven and Earth cannot endure them long,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Sudden winds and torrential rains do not last a day,
Yet cannot endure and vanish—
How much less so for humans?
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Do not indulge in reckless joy or anger,
Nor favoritism in rewards and punishments.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The ancient Sages shed the burdens of virtue—
When anger and joy do not disturb the heart, it stands upright.
Upright, it is empty, tranquil, and *wuwei*—
Yet nothing remains undone.

Thus, following the Dao,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Therefore, the Sage models Heaven as good,
Does not act in wrathful anger to complete virtue,
And so endures.

The Dao aligns with the Dao,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Thus, above and below align in the Dao—
Above and below align in the Dao, and there is no lordship.

Virtue aligns with virtue,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Thus, below and above align in virtue—
Below and above align in virtue, and there is no servitude.

Loss aligns with loss.
*The Dongling Jing* says:
When blaming others’ faults,
Examine your own goodness first.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Anger controlled, grievances do not arise;
Anger from non-anger—
Anger without color—
This is called calculated gain.

Those who align with the Dao,
The Dao also gains them;
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Dao and virtue—
Like leather and hide—
Far apart, they draw near;
Near, they grow distant.
Examined, they cannot be grasped;
Scrutinized, they are not empty.

Thus, the Sage is like a mirror—
Neither pushing nor welcoming,
Responding to things without hoarding,
Overcoming things without harming.

Those who align with virtue, virtue also gains them.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Virtue without accumulation yet without worry—
This is called *reaching its fullness*.

Thus, the good align weakness with strength,
Turn misfortune into blessing,
Pile stones to form mountains,
Gather waters to shape rivers,
Accumulate virtue to become kings.
Without accumulation, there is no such attainment.

Those who align with loss, loss also gains them.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
How could joy and anger be recklessly expressed?
Joy returns, it must bring anger;
Anger returns, it must bring joy—
Both are unbalanced.
Now, my heart has neither resistance nor compliance.

Faith is insufficient,
*The Dongling Jing* says:
Complete faith brings peace to the world;
Lost faith brings peril to the world.
There is no faith at all.

*The Dongling Jing* also says:
For people lack faith,
Because governance is unjust,
And the ruler is not diligent and clear.

**Chapter Twenty-Four: "The Lame Do Not Stand"**
This chapter of *The Nanhua Jing* states:
The Great Dao perishes through excessive distinctions;
Scholars lose the Dao through excessive methods.
Thus, Yang Zhu, Mo Di, Shi Kuang, Li Zhu,
Gong Tui, Zeng Shi, and those skilled in eloquence,
sensual allure, and varied pursuits—
though their endeavors differ,
they all meet the same fate of losing the Dao.

To pluck the strings of the *liu* and disrupt the six pitches,
To melt and sever the *yu* and *se*—
Plugging Shi Kuang’s ears,
And the world begins to hold its breath in silence.
To extinguish patterns and disperse the five colors,
To glue Li Zhu’s eyes—
And the world begins to hold its tongue in darkness.

**Chapter Twenty-Four: "The Lame Do Not Stand" (continued)**

Destroy the hook and line, abandon the compass and square,
Pluck Gong Tui’s fingers,
And the world begins to possess its own cleverness.

Sever Zeng Shi’s conduct,
Silence Yang Zhu and Mo Di’s mouths,
Reject virtue and righteousness,
And the world’s virtue begins to merge in mysterious unity.

Though one gathers virtue and righteousness—
Even if as skilled as Zeng Shi—
Though one gathers the five tones—
Even if as skilled as Shi Kuang—
Though one gathers the five colors—
Even if as skilled as Li Zhu—
Though one gathers the five flavors—
Even if as skilled as Yu Er—
This is not what I call *plunging into the Dao* or *excellence in virtue*.

What I call *excellence* is merely *mastery of the Dao through non-action*.

Now, losing one’s true nature through the five tones, colors, and true flavors—
Those who take or discard these five matters
Are all great harms to the people’s lives and deaths.
Yet Yang Zhu and Mo Di began to abandon the six tones and colors,
Deeming themselves enlightened—
This is not what I call *attaining the Dao*.

Those like Zeng Shi, Yang Zhu, Mo Di, Shi Kuang, Gong Tui, and Li Zhu—
All externally establish the illusions of cleverness, eloquence, allure, and skill,
And thus disturb the world—
Things the law has no use for.

**The Lame Do Not Stand.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Fused toes and branch fingers on the hand—
These are useless fingers on a tree,
Emerging from nature yet extravagant in virtue.
Those who employ many methods in virtue and righteousness—
Are they not like superfluous organs?
Yet they are not the true path of virtue and the Dao."*

**The Crippled Do Not Walk.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Hanging warts and dangling excrescences—
These are clustered useless flesh on the feet,
Emerging from form yet extravagant in nature.
Those who cluster many methods in the emotions of the five viscera,
Indulge in perverse and twisted conduct of virtue and righteousness,
And multiply the uses of cleverness and insight—
This is not the straight path of virtue and the Dao."*

**Those Who Self-See Are Blind.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Those fused in clarity—
They disrupt the five colors, indulge in patterns,
Is not the dazzling of green, yellow, and variegated brocade?
Yet this is Li Zhu."*

**Those Who Self-Right Are Not Illuminated.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Those excessive in hearing—
They disrupt the five tones, indulge in the six pitches,
Is not the clamor of metal, stone, silk, bamboo, bells, and great *lu*?
Yet this is Shi Kuang."*

**Those Who Self-Praise Achieve No Merit.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Those who branch from virtue—
Plucking virtue to block nature and gain fame,
Forcing the world to play the *huang* and *gu* in service of unattainable laws—
Is this not Zeng Shi?"*

**Those Who Self-Commend Do Not Endure.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Those fused in eloquence—
Stacking bricks, knotting ropes, weaving sentences,
Trapped in debates over solid-white and difference—
Is this not the empty praise of useless speech?
Yet this is Yang Zhu and Mo Di."*

**Regarding the Dao, *The Nanhua Jing* says:**
*"Thus, Yang Zhu, Mo Di, Zeng Shi, Shi Kuang, Li Zhu, and Gong Tui—
All amass minor achievements in cleverness, allure, skill, eloquence, and branch paths,
Yet they are not the utmost rectitude of the world.
I consume *The Nanhua Jing*:
Those ancient ones who rectified the Dao and nature,
Did not lose their innate nature and spontaneous life,
Thus aligning with the Dao—
They did not multiply branches and diverge,
In virtue, they did not stray,
The long did not exceed,
The short did not fall short—
They followed the natural rectitude.

Now, minor achievements in tones and colors—
These sever and extend human nature.
Thus, though the duck’s legs are short,
Extending them brings sorrow,
Though the crane’s legs are long,
Severing them brings grief.
Thus, what is naturally long should not be severed,
What is naturally short should not be extended—
No need to fear sorrow or grief from tones and colors."*

**Superfluous Conduct.**
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
*"To take life as a hanging wart or excrescence,
To take death as a festering ulcer or abscess.
In ancient times, when people contained their clarity,
The world did not melt away;
When people hid their hearing,
The world was not burdened;
When people contained their wisdom,
The world was not confused;
When people hid their virtue,
The world was not perverted.

Yet those like Zeng Shi, Yang Zhu, Mo Di, Shi Kuang, Gong Tui, and Li Zhu—
All externally established minor virtues
And thus disturbed the world—
Things the law has no use for.
Thus, those of the Dao do not dwell among them.*

*The Nanhua Jing*—Laozi says:
*"I am ashamed that virtue and the Dao are not practiced,
Thus I do not accept the perverse doctrines of minor achievements from Yang Zhu and Mo Di.
Therefore, Zhuangzi neither dared to uphold the petty virtues of righteousness,
Nor descend to the perverse conduct of Yang Zhu and Mo Di."*

**End of *The Four Children’s Commentary on the Ancient Dao*, Volume Three, *Tongshen Department—Jade Precepts Category*.**

**Volume Four, *The Four Children’s Commentary on the Ancient Dao*, *Ancient Sheng’s Collection of Talent and Quality***

**Chapter Twenty-Five: *The Primordial Mixture of Things***
This chapter of *The Chōngxū Jing* [□ *Heavenly Felicity Canon*] states:
The Dao/Way is formless and boundless, transforming into one *qi*.
One *qi* undergoes nine transformations and becomes the Primordial Chaos.
The Primordial Chaos is the origin of the forms of Heaven and Earth.
Thus, when things are in a state of primordial mixture,
When yin and yang unfold and divide,
Before Heaven and Earth were born,
The light and clear ascended to form Heaven,
The heavy and turbid descended to form Earth—
The Dao/Way gives birth to Heaven and Earth.

When things are in a state of primordial mixture,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* [□ *Passage of the Mysterious Origin*] says:
This refers to the state before Heaven and Earth came into being.

Before Heaven and Earth came into being,
*The Nanhua Jing* [□ *Southern Bloom Canon*] says:
The Dao/Way is formless,
Giving birth to Heaven, giving birth to Earth,
And existing before Heaven and Earth were born.

Silent and vast,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Only the Dao/Way is formless,
Dark and unfathomable,
Silent and boundless,
Its sound unheard.

Standing alone yet never changing,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The Dao of yin and yang
Exists independently, giving birth to all things,
From ancient times to the present,
Never ceasing to transform.

Circulating endlessly without cessation,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Heaven moves in cycles, Earth remains firm,
The four seasons as its eternal framework.
Hard and soft intermingle,
And all things are born.
Thus it can be the mother of the world.

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
Where the Dao/Way exists,
There is nothing it cannot permeate.

*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Taking Heaven as father,
Taking Earth as mother,
Heaven and Earth are the parents of all things.

I do not know its name,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
I forcefully name it,
And call it the Dao/Way.

I forcefully name it the Dao/Way,
*The Dònglíng Jing* [□ *Canon of the Cavern Spirit*] says:
The Dao/Way is the wondrous operation of the natural order.
Filial piety is the supreme virtue of human Dao/Way.

The Dao/Way encompasses Heaven and Earth,
Nurturing all things,
Shaping their forms and kinds,
Distributing their nature and lifespan.
Its function is supremely real yet never serves as a storehouse for things,
Nor as an official for affairs.
It is the embodiment of effortless action,
Seeking through touch and perception,
Yet nothing can grasp its presence—
I forcefully name it the Dao/Way.

I name it forcefully as *Great*.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* [□ *Sutra of Sincere Essence*] says:
The Dao/Way originally has no sound,
The sages forcefully name it,
And call it *Great*.

*Great* is *Passing*,
*Passing* is *Distant*,
*Distant* is *Returning*.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The Dao/Way, before Heaven and Earth,
Has existed since antiquity.

*Passing* is *Distant*,
*Distant* is *Returning*.
*The Chōngxū Jing* says:
The Dao/Way extends from the farthest antiquity,
Returning to the present day.
Its years are beyond measure,
Heaven enduring, Earth lasting,
Their limits unfathomable.

Thus the Dao/Way is *Great*,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The Great Dao/Way of yin and yang
Is the vastest *qi*.
It dwells beyond Heaven,
Supremely high without equal,
Supremely deep without bottom,
Enveloping Heaven and Earth without surface or core,
Empty yet encompassing all,
Circulating without hindrance.

Heaven is *Great*,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The manifest forms of Heaven
Are the greatest of forms.

Earth is *Great*,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The manifest forms of Earth
Are the greatest of forms.

The King is also *Great*.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
None is greater than the Emperor-King,
Whose virtue matches that of Heaven and Earth.

Within the realm there are four *Great* ones,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
And how can one know
That Heaven and Earth suffice to exhaust the domain of the four *Great* ones?
Yet the King occupies one of them.

*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
When the King is bright, the ministers are bright,
And the realm is at peace.

The King’s law follows Earth,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
None is richer than Earth—
Spring *qi* arises, and all grasses grow,
Autumn’s harvest is right, and all treasures mature.

Earth’s law follows Heaven,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
None is more divine than Heaven.

*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Earth receives from Heaven,
And all things take form.

Heaven’s law follows the Dao/Way,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The Dao/Way is supremely divine,
Guiding the revolutions of Heaven’s course.

*The Nanhua Jing* says:
The Dao/Way is formless,
Covering Heaven and Earth yet never contrived.

The Dao/Way follows Nature.

**Chapter Twenty-Six: *The Heavy as the Root of the Light***
This chapter of *The Tōngxuān Jing* states:
In the later ages, the kings and feudal lords,
Though possessing the power of ten thousand chariots,
Were not content with their wealth and authority—
Heaven’s riches and the world’s prosperity
Could not satisfy their desires.
They grew frivolous and covetous,
Light and restless, losing their foundation,
And thus lost the world.

The heavy as the root of the light,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sages of antiquity acted without doing,
And their transformation was rooted in weightiness, not lightness.
Maintaining weightiness to govern the light,
The world was at ease.

The still as the ruler of the restless,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sages of antiquity possessed the virtue of quietude—
They were tranquil and not restless.
Dwelling in stillness to restrain the restless,
The world was at peace.

The ruler is the root.
Thus the *junzi* [noble/princely person] walks all day,
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
With propriety as their conduct,
With music as their harmony,
Their benevolence and compassion naturally perfumed—
This is called the *junzi*.
The *junzi* thus govern the state,
And the worthy never fail to inquire
Into their labor and ease.

They do not abandon their baggage and supplies.
*The Nanhua Jing* says:
How dare the *junzi* govern lightly?
Though there are palaces and grand displays,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The Dao/Way of the *junzi*
Is to cultivate oneself through stillness.

Dwell in serene detachment.

*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The *junzi*’s Dao/Way—
When still, the lower ranks are undisturbed;
The lower ranks undisturbed, the people remain orderly.
How could the sovereign of a realm of ten thousand chariots—
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The power of ten thousand chariots relies on all things for merit and fame,
With authority so grave, one cannot govern lightly—
Yet govern with levity toward the world?
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
If the power of ten thousand chariots acts lightly, merit and fame will not be fulfilled.
Lightness leads to losing one’s ministers.
*The Tōngxuān Jing*—Wénzǐ asked of the foundation of governing the state,
Lǎozǐ replied: *"The foundation lies in cultivating oneself.
Never have I heard of a state in chaos while the self is governed,
Or a state governed while the self is in disarray.*
If one governs lightly, one loses the foundation of sovereignty.
Agitation leads to losing sovereignty."
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Even the power of ten thousand chariots is insufficient for gratification,
The wealth of the world insufficient for joy—
Yet agitated, with boundless desires,
Indulging desire loses the foundation.

---
**Chapter Twenty-Seven: The Virtue of Benevolent Action**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* speaks:
The virtue of the sovereign and emperor harmonizes with the effortless Dao/Way of Heaven and Earth.
This is to ride the Dao/Way of Heaven and Earth,
To let all things govern themselves while employing the effortless Dao/Way of humanity.
The foundation of virtue and Dao/Way lies in establishing the essential,
In the sovereign’s effortless action, the world is governed.
To always uphold the five virtues of effortless action,
To rescue humanity—when all are virtuous, this is called the essential mystery.
The five virtues: saving the people.
Benevolent action leaves no trace.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sages of antiquity acted without doing,
Their teachings spread without descending from the hall,
And yet transformation reached the four seas—
They acted without leaving a trace.
Benevolent speech leaves no flaws.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Later ages awaited commands through words,
And governing became difficult.
The sages of antiquity issued commands without speech—
The sages of old were thus teachers,
Moving the world with a single word, yet without uttering it,
Their hearts and actions were effortless—
They acted without transmission.
Benevolent planning requires no calculations.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sage unifies all things in one,
And all affairs are complete—
The sage acts without contrivance, without scheming,
Without deliberation, yet all is appropriate.
Benevolent closure requires no locks or keys, yet cannot be opened.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
In ancient times, the sage-kings ruled above,
Making their preferences clear to the people,
Yet punishments were discarded and unused—
Orders were given, yet the people restrained themselves.
Benevolent binding requires no ropes or knots, yet cannot be undone.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sages of old did not impose laws or decrees,
Yet customs shifted naturally—
This was through their hearts and actions in the Dao/Way,
Without action, yet self-regulated.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
In ancient times, restraint was not by ropes or cords.
Later ages relied on ropes and knots for firmness,
Thus their virtue declined.
Therefore, the sages—
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sages of antiquity acted without doing,
Illuminating the world,
Holding the essential of the Dao/Way,
Grasping the essential to rescue the multitude.
Always rescuing people, thus no one is abandoned;
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The world’s affairs cannot be forced—
Follow their natural course, and thus no one is abandoned.
The *Dònglíng Jing* says:
Rescuing flaws is precious in reducing affairs.
Always rescuing things, thus nothing is abandoned.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The transformations of all things cannot be rescued—
Hold their essential and return them, thus nothing is abandoned.
The *Dònglíng Jing* says:
Rescuing flaws is precious in a single decree.
This is called *clinging to clarity*.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The Dao/Way can be hidden, can be manifest,
Can be obscure, can be clear—
Accumulating subtlety forms clarity.
Thus the virtuous become teachers for the unvirtuous;
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sovereign is the people’s teacher.
The unvirtuous become resources for the virtuous.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The sages of old skillfully employed the Dao/Way,
Using what people relied upon to establish merit,
Trusting their weaknesses to their strengths,
Governing many, occupying the essential—
Seeing virtue as if unable to catch up,
Seeing vice as if it were an omen.
Not honoring their teachers,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
Teaching without weariness—this is how one governs the people.
Discarding worthy teachers and good friends,
Few would abandon virtue to become corrupt.
Not cherishing their resources.
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Is it not that the teacher is correct and without error,
The teacher governs and there is no chaos?
This clarifies the principles of Heaven and Earth,
The nature of all things.
Though one may know the great confusion,
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The ancients did not indulge in trivialities,
Nor fall into great confusion;
Did not practice minor kindnesses,
Nor become greatly foolish.
In the later ages, though one knows trivialities,
One is greatly confused by the Dao/Way.
This is called the essential mystery.
*The Tōngxuān Jing* says:
The foundation of governing the multitude lies in one essential—
When the world is at peace, it depends on one person.

---
**Chapter Twenty-Eight: Knowing the Heroic**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* speaks:
In ancient times, how did the Dao/Way hide, yet true and false emerged?
It was because the Five Emperors of later ages hid the Dao/Way in petty achievements,
Thus falsifying the Dao/Way and making it untrue.
In the age of the Great Dao/Way and supreme virtue,
They dwelled with beasts and birds,
Their clans united with all things—
How could they know of benevolence, righteousness, ritual, music,
Or *junzi* and the petty?
In harmony with ignorance, their virtue did not depart;
In harmony with desirelessness, the people remained simple and unadorned.
Simple and unadorned, their nature was pure and genuine, and they governed themselves.
When the Five Emperors of later ages
Climbed and hung with ornaments, calling it benevolence,
Dragged their feet and bound their thumbs, calling it righteousness—
Then the world began to doubt the falsehoods of petty scholarly achievements.
The muddled and licentious became music,
The bizarre and perverse became ritual—
Then the world began to divide into laws, calendars, sounds, and colors.
Thus, the Great Dao/Way of the later Five Emperors scattered,
And they crafted books and tools for governing.
Knowing the heroic,
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The Five Emperors knew the heroic,
And thus created the five tones.

---

The *Tongxuan Jing* [□下德篇] says:
The thunderous roar can be likened to bells and drums,
The changes of wind and rain can be understood through music and rhythm.
Sounds that can be heard can be regulated,
And thus the five tones are attuned.
By preserving its **yin** (feminine, receptive),
One becomes the **dissolution** (*xī*) for all under Heaven.

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
In ancient times, the five tones were not disordered—
Who then created the six *lü* (pitch pipes)?
By preserving its **yin** (feminine, receptive),
One becomes the **dissolution** (*xī*) for all under Heaven.
As the **dissolution** (*xī*) for all under Heaven,
One’s enduring virtue/power does not depart,
And returns to the state of the infant.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient rulers who excelled in governance
Modeled themselves on the **non-action** of the rivers and seas,
Becoming the **dissolution** (*xī*) for all under Heaven,
And their virtue/power did not depart.
Thus, the **Perfect Ones** (*zhìrén*) did not perceive what was desirable,
Their minds and spirits dwelled in harmony,
At rest they embodied virtue/power,
In motion they aligned with the Dao/Way.

By knowing its **white** (bright, manifest),
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The Five Emperors knew its **white** (bright, manifest),
And thus created the five colors.
What can be seen with clarity can be obscured,
What can be discerned in color can be distinguished,
And thus the five colors were differentiated.
By preserving its **black** (dark, hidden),
One becomes the **model** (*shì*) for all under Heaven.

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
In ancient times, the five colors were not disordered—
Who then created patterns and hues?
By preserving its **black** (dark, hidden),
One becomes the **model** (*shì*) for all under Heaven.
As the **model** (*shì*) for all under Heaven,
One’s enduring virtue/power remains unerring,
And returns to the **Non-Extreme** (*wújí*).

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The governance of the ancient Perfect Ones
Was void, tranquil, and still—
They cultivated the Dao/Way of Nature,
And with indifference and non-action,
All under Heaven found harmony.

By knowing its **honor** (flourishing, prominence),
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The Five Emperors knew its **honor** (flourishing, prominence),
And thus established laws and calendars.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The greatest of all, Heaven and Earth cannot contain it,
The subtlest of all, divine brilliance cannot perceive it.
What can be observed in vastness can be measured,
And thus calendars and numbers were set.
By preserving its **humiliation** (abased, lowly),
One becomes the **valley** (*gǔ*) for all under Heaven.

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
In ancient times, white jade was not shattered—
Who then created the *guī-zhuāng* (jade tablets)?
By preserving its **humiliation** (abased, lowly),
One becomes the **valley** (*gǔ*) for all under Heaven.
As the **valley** (*gǔ*) for all under Heaven,
One’s enduring virtue/power is complete,
And returns to the **Uncarved** (*pǔ*).

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient rulers who excelled in governance
Modeled themselves on the rivers and seas.
The rivers and seas act without striving,
Becoming the **valley** (*gǔ*) for all under Heaven,
And their virtue/power is complete.
They did not divide and contend for wealth,
Yet wealth was abundant.
The Sage was pure and unadorned yet did not scatter,
Indifferent and desireless yet the people remained simple.
The **Uncarved** (*pǔ*) scatters, becoming tools—
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
When the Five Emperors established laws and calendars,
Differentiated the five colors,
Distinguished pure and impure,
And tasted sweet and bitter,
Then the **Uncarved** (*pǔ*) scattered into tools.
They set forth **ren** (benevolence) and **yì** (righteousness),
And refined **ritual** and **music**—
Then virtue/power shifted into falsehoods.
The people adorned cleverness to dazzle the simple,
Employed deceit to attack their superiors,
And though some could uphold it,
None could govern well—
For cleverness multiplied while virtue/power waned.

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
In ancient times, purity and simplicity were unbroken—
Who then created the sacrificial ox and wine?
Virtue/power and the Dao/Way were not abandoned—
Why seek **ren** (benevolence) and **yì** (righteousness)?
Human nature and disposition were not straying—
Why use **ritual** and **music**?
Later ages shattered simplicity to make tools—
The fault of artisans.
They destroyed virtue/power to create **ren** (benevolence) and **yì** (righteousness)—
The error of the Five Emperors.
The Sage used them as **officials and leaders** (*guānzhǎng*).

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The **Shu-Wei** (狶韦氏) obtained it from the Dao/Way,
To grasp the **non-action** of Heaven and Earth;
The **Fuxi** (伏牺氏) obtained it from the Dao/Way,
To follow the **Qi-Mother** (气母) of the Dao/Way;
The **Shennong** (神农氏) obtained it from the Dao/Way,
To rule through the **virtue/power of Fire** (火德);
The **Yellow Emperor** (黄帝) obtained it from the Dao/Way,
To ascend the realm of **Jingyun** (景云, "illuminated clouds").
Thus, the **Great Measures** (*dàzhì*) were not severed.

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Hence, one ruler’s decree could govern all under Heaven—
It was like the **one breath** (*yīxī*) of the **Tang and Yu** (唐虞).
They knew the use of **accomplished persons** (*xiánrén*) for the benefit of all under Heaven,
Yet did not know their harm to all under Heaven.
Only through severance—
The outsiders knew this.

**Chapter 29: "The Manifest" (章第二十九)**
This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* states:
In the **end times** (*mòshì*), kings and feudal lords
Treated all under Heaven as their own family,
And all things as their livestock.
They harbored the ambition of all under Heaven,
And sought the abundance of all things—
Thus, their **qi** was full yet their wills were arrogant.
The great contended with the small through warfare,
The small defied and oppressed the lowly,
And none could endure long—
Not in contention, not in the Dao/Way,
Not in the Sage, not in the Perfect Ones.

Earth clings to the low yet does not contend for height—
Thus it is stable and not in peril.
Water flows to the low yet does not contend for swiftness—
Thus it departs and is not delayed.

Thus the Sage acts without action and thus meets no defeat,
Acts without grasping and thus meets no loss.

Those skilled in military formations
Do not know the divine transformations of temple battles and strategic shifts.

Those who love war have never failed to suffer defeat,
Those who contend in battle have never failed to lose.
The skilled swimmer drowns,
The skilled rider falls—
Each perishes by their own fondness,
Thus, those who act meet defeat,
Those who grasp meet loss.
The Seven Kingdoms perished through warring for Heaven under Heaven.

To take Heaven under Heaven,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
A kingdom should not greedily seek what it seeks,
But receive it from Heaven under Heaven.
Not through self-taken virtue and righteousness does one obtain it,
But through giving it.

To contend for fame is to seek what is already possessed—
I see this as unavoidable.
I see this as unavoidable.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Though one grasps it, it will not remain.
Fame cannot be sought and obtained—
It is given by Heaven under Heaven.
What is given returns to Heaven under Heaven,
And what returns is **virtue/power**.
If one lacks **virtue/power**, the people will not return.
Without their return, the use of arms is perilous to the Dao/Way—
I see this as unavoidable.

Heaven under Heaven is a divine instrument.
*The Dongling Jing* says:
Heaven under Heaven’s divine instrument,
The emperor’s weighty station—
It cannot be seized.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Heaven under Heaven is a great instrument,
It cannot be grasped, cannot be seized.
Those who seize meet defeat.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Those who love fame have never failed to be struck down,
The skilled swimmer drowns—
Each perishes by their own fondness,
Thus it is said: those who act meet defeat.
Those who grasp meet loss.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Those who contend for gain have never failed to be exhausted,
The skilled rider falls—
Each perishes by their own fondness,
Thus those who grasp meet loss.

Therefore, things—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
Calamity and fortune share the same gate,
Gain and harm reverse each other—
One must not fail to discern them.
Some advance, some follow,
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Some are long, some are short.
Some warm, some chill,
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Some are fair, some are foul.
Some are strong, some are weak,
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Some are joyous, some are stern.
Some are raised, some are ruined.
*The Chongxu Jing* says:
Some are right, some are wrong.

Thus the Sage—
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
To endure is not in contention,
To govern is not in sainthood.
Earth clings to the low yet does not contend for height—
Thus it is stable and not in peril.
Water flows to the low yet does not contend for swiftness—
Thus it departs and is not delayed.

Thus the Sage acts without action and thus meets no defeat,
Acts without grasping and thus meets no loss.

Avoiding excess,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The root of strength lies in guarding weakness,
Thus the Sage guards gentleness and does not grow strong,
And so can complete his kingship,
And Heaven under Heaven endures long.

Avoiding extravagance,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The root of moderation lies in rejecting excess,
Thus the Sage rejects extravagance and luxury,
Dares not be proud or wasteful,
And so can endure long.

Avoiding arrogance.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The root of avoiding arrogance lies in emptiness and nothingness,
Thus the Sage rejects arrogance and excess,
Dares not be proud or overflowing,
And so can endure long.

---
**Chapter 30: "Assisting the Sovereign with the Dao/Way"**
This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* states:
The ministers of the Seven Kingdoms raised unrighteous arms,
Until corpses lay piled and blood flowed,
Clashing before one another.
Where wars arise, Heaven under Heaven falls into great disorder—
The path of loving valor leads to peril and ruin.

Arms are cruel instruments.
Warfare is a perilous affair.
Ringing bells to destroy oneself,
Burning candles to scorch oneself—
The brave perish by their strength,
The eloquent perish by their cleverness.
Men die violently, not by fate,
Crushing few against many,
Weak against strong—
Arms kill them together.
The path of loving war leads to peril and ruin.

Now the Seven Kingdoms’ rebellious ministers love war and battle,
And Heaven under Heaven falls into great disorder.

To assist the sovereign with the Dao/Way,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, ministers governed the state,
Ruled within their borders,
Practiced **virtue/power** and morality,
Spread benevolence and bestow favors—
This was the highest path.
They did not use arms to coerce Heaven under Heaven,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, deceit did not arise,
Peace and ease knew no strife,
Heaven under Heaven was at peace,
The wise had no place to display their cleverness,
The brave had no place to wield their might—
They did not use arms to coerce Heaven under Heaven.
Their affairs returned to goodness.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Warring States resorted to arms for self-preservation,
Deceitful men flattered and schemed in secret,
Hastily provoking the ruler to use their #1,
And the chaotic aided their schemes.

Where the army encamps, thorns and brambles grow.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Seven Kingdoms raised arms in difficulty,
No leisurely steps on the road,
No standing crops in the fields,
Thorns and brambles grow.
After a great army passes, there will surely be years of famine.
*The Tongxuan Jing [Weiming Section]* says:
Raising ten thousand soldiers costs a thousand gold daily,
After the army’s departure, there will surely be years of famine.

Thus the virtuous are resolute yet yield,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The advantage of arms is not sufficient to rely upon for strength.
They dare not take strength from it.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, the brave dared not act recklessly and thus lived.
Resolute yet not boastful,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, those who guarded gentleness and weakness
Dared not rely on strength,
Thus they could boast.
Resolute yet not arrogant,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient brave did not use force violently,
This may be called being one with the Dao/Way.
Resolute yet unavoidable,
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
When the enemy attacks and one is forced to act.
This is resolute yet not strong.
*The Tongxuan Jing* says:
In ancient times, those who guarded gentleness and weakness
Were the living—
Heaven under Heaven thrived.

「Things grow strong, then grow old. The *Chōngxū Jīng* recounts how Confucius spoke to Zǐlù, saying: *"Yóu, you can be brave but cannot be cowardly."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*"Martial valor dies by reckless strength,
Wits perish by clever scheming.
This is called not abiding by the Dao/Way."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*"There are those who die by violence, not by fate.
They provoke the multitude with their few,
They defy the strong with their weak—
All soldiers slay them,
For their arms are not in accord with the Dao/Way.
Not in accord with the Dao/Way, they die early.
Not in accord with the Dao/Way, they die early."*

The *Nanhua Jing* says:
*"Not serene, not content—this is not the Dao/Way,
Not virtue/power.
Not in accord with the Dao/Way, they die early."*

**Chapter Thirty-One: The Virtue of Fine Arms**
This chapter in the *Nanhua Jing* recounts:
In ancient times, King Zhao of the Wén dynasty delighted in swords. Three thousand sword-wielders stood at his gates, clashing day and night before him, with a hundred or more dying or wounded each year. Yet he never tired of it. The heir apparent, Prince Dàn, was troubled by this and asked Zhuangzi to counsel the king to cease his fondness for swords.

Zhuangzi saw the king and said:
*"I have three swords—let Your Majesty choose which to use."*

The king replied:
*"I wish to hear of these three swords."*

Zhuangzi said:
*"There is the Sovereign’s Sword, the Noble’s Sword, and the Commoner’s Sword.
The Sovereign’s Sword rectifies the Nobles—then all under Heaven submit.
The Noble’s Sword within the Four Seals* has none who do not yield.
The Commoner’s Sword, once drawn, severs life—useless for state affairs.

Now, Your Majesty holds the Sovereign’s rank yet favors the Commoner’s Sword. I humbly think Your Majesty underestimates its weight."*

*The Four Seals (四封) refers to the boundaries of a feudal lord’s domain.*

The king was suddenly lost in thought, ascended the hall in silence, and the attendants served him food. He took three mouthfuls before Zhuangzi spoke again:
*"Your Majesty, now seated and composed, the matter of swords is already settled."*

Thus, King Zhao did not leave the palace for three months, and all the sword-wielders perished in their dwellings.

Discussing arms, there are six ill omens—never a good omen, never to be used lightly.

The *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*"Arms are instruments of ill omen,
War is a perilous affair.
The most unauspicious of tools."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*"Those who delight in arms provoke the utmost calamity.
Things may even come to hate them,
For arms ravage the land, causing unrest among the ten thousand households.
They slaughter without cause—
The people’s resentment rises,
And things come to hate them.
Thus, those who abide by the Dao/Way do not dwell upon them."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*"The virtuous/powerful* seek the Dao/Way and virtue/power,
Not the frequent use of arms.
The virtuous/powerful* at rest honor the left—
The *Dònglíng Jīng* says: *‘Yang virtue/power abides on the left.’*
In war, they honor the right—
The *Dònglíng Jīng* says: *‘Yin punishment abides on the right.’*
Arms are instruments of ill omen—
The *Tongxuan Jing* states: *‘Thus, arms are not the tools of the virtuous/powerful.*’
Not the treasure of the virtuous/powerful.
When used unavoidably, the *Tongxuan Jing* states: *‘There are five uses of arms; the second is *yìngbīng*—using them when unavoidable.’*
Using arms unavoidably is called *yìngbīng*.
To win by *yìngbīng* is the fulfillment of Heaven’s Dao/Way.
Serenity and detachment are supreme—
The *Tongxuan Jing* states: *‘Serene without thought, detached without concern,
Nurturing wisdom through serenity,
Serene and content without boastfulness, attaining harmony.’*
To win yet not revel in it—
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ *Shàng Rén Piān*] says: *‘Arms kill and wound—victory is not beautiful.’*
Arms are most cruel when they dwell in the heart—
*Mózhì* is the lowest.
Yet those who revel in it are those who delight in killing—
The *Tongxuan Jing* states: *‘The unrighteous arms of the Warring States reached the point where corpses piled and blood flowed in exchange—
This is delighting in killing.’*
Those who delight in killing—
The *Tongxuan Jing* states: *‘In the Spring and Autumn Period, those who raised arms for themselves were abandoned by the people.
Abandoned by the people, though great, they must perish.
They cannot fulfill their ambitions in the world.’*
For others’ battles, they cannot achieve kingship;
For their own gain, they cannot establish their merit.

Good omens honor the left—
The *Nanhua Jing* says: *‘The Dao/Way has a left.’*
Left is *yang*—
The *Nanhua Jing* says: *‘The Dao/Way has a right.’*
Right is *yin*.
The Left General* abides on the left—
The *Nanhua Jing* commentary says: *‘Left, yang—maintains life.’*
The Right General* abides on the right—
The *Nanhua Jing* commentary says: *‘Right, yin—brings death.’*
It is said to treat them with funeral rites—
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ *Shàng Rén Piān*] says: *‘Treat them with funeral rites.’*
When many are slain, mourn with sorrowful tears—
The *Tongxuan Jing* states: *‘After defeat, mourn with sorrowful tears.’*
When victorious, treat them with funeral rites.*

*The Left General (偏将军) and Right General (上将军) are military ranks, with the Left General typically overseeing defensive or auxiliary troops, and the Right General overseeing offensive or punitive campaigns.*

**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Dao/Way is Ever Nameless**
This chapter in the *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*"In the ancient times, those who practiced the Dao/Way and virtue/power
Were aided by Heaven, assisted by Earth, and helped by spirits.
The Four Barbarians* submitted to them,
Auspicious stars appeared, and benevolent clouds spread.
Yellow dragons descended, sweet dew fell,
Nectar springs flowed, and fine grains ripened.
Mushrooms sprouted, and twin branches grew.
Phoenixes gathered in their courts,
Qilins roamed their outskirts,
Qiaolongs dwelled in their marshes.
The seas did not surge, the rivers did not overflow.
The sun and moon, stars and constellations moved without deviation,
Winds and rains followed their seasons,
And the Five Grains flourished in abundance.
These are the ten divine responses and auspicious omens of the ancients who walked the Dao/Way."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ *Dào Yuán Piān*] says:
*"The ancient Dao/Way was nameless—
Namelessness is speechlessness.
Speechlessness is the origin of action.
The origin of action was the first knotting of cords*—
Though crude, it was sufficient.
The *Tongxuan Jing* states:
*The ancients’ Dao/Way sought smallness in the heart,
And greatness in aspiration.
Smallness in the heart meant restraining petty desires;
Greatness in aspiration meant nothing was beyond governance.
What they held was very small,
Yet what they entrusted was very great—
Thus, the Dao/Way’s method of governance, though simple, was enough to rule the world.
All under Heaven dared not defy them."*

The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
*"In the ancient age of the Great Dao/Way and supreme virtue/power, all things moved in simplicity yet harmonized without being coerced; their naturalness was preserved, and the people’s innate nature governed itself.
If sovereigns and kings could uphold this, the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
*'The ancients’ sovereigns and kings took non-action as their constant practice.
All things would naturally submit to them.*
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
*'By non-action, nothing remained undone; all things transformed spontaneously.
Heaven and Earth combined in harmony, and sweet dew descended.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'When yin and yang were in concord, sweet rain fell in due season.
The people required no command yet all was balanced.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The silent command—this was how the ancient sages became teachers.
Though they spoke no words, all under Heaven transformed.
Only when names were first established,*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'Later ages, with partial attainment and named methods, had speech.
Speech marked the completion of affairs.
The completion of affairs was the creation of books and learning tools.
Once names were set,*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'Now, the Dao/Way of later people seeks comprehensive wisdom yet rigid practice, abundant capability yet few deeds. Comprehensive wisdom means none were left unknowing; rigid practice means some things were not done; abundant capability means none were left ungoverned; few deeds mean restraint in what was held.
Yet what they saw as great was small in knowledge and unaware of loss—
Thus, the Dao/Way’s method of governance, though grand, was enough to bring chaos.
They would also know when to stop,*
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
*'Know when to stop?
What they did not know was the utmost.
Knowing when to stop was the way to avoid peril.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
*'No one can mirror themselves in flowing water,
Yet they are reflected in still water—
Only the still can still all things.
Compare the Dao/Way’s presence in the world,*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'Thus, the Dao/Way’s presence in the world is like the great rivers and seas that do nothing yet take all streams.
Like valleys and basins in relation to rivers and seas.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*'The great rivers and seas do nothing,
Yet they receive the hundred streams—
They do not seek yet are able to obtain.'*
The *Zhēn Dào Móde Jīng* concludes the fourth volume of the *Four Sons’ Collection of Ancient Dao/Way Commentaries*."*

**Volume Five of the *Four Sons’ Collection of Ancient Dao/Way Commentaries*
*Ancient Shàng’s Collection on the Talent of Kòu*
**Chapter 33: Knowing Others is Wisdom**

This chapter from the *Nánhuá Jīng* states:
In ancient times, the sages tempered their ambitions.
Wealth, honor, prestige, fame, and profit—these six things inflame ambition.
If the heart remains undisturbed, it is upright.
Uprightness brings emptiness, tranquility, and non-action—yet nothing remains undone.
Thus, the ancient sages possessed wisdom in knowing others and clarity in self-awareness,
strength to excel over others and resolve to overcome oneself,
wealth in contentment and resolve in steadfast action.
With these six virtues,
in life, their deeds were enduring and their path unerring;
in death, their names were immortal and their legacy eternal.
Hence, these are called the *Six Virtues of Longevity*—
wisdom in knowing others is called wisdom.

Knowing others is wisdom.
As the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
To recognize the worthy is wisdom,
and no wisdom surpasses knowing others.
Knowing others prevents chaotic governance.

Self-awareness is clarity.
As the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
To cherish the worthy is benevolence,
to revere the worthy is propriety.

To excel over others is to have strength,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* [missing text] *De* Chapter says:
Those who excel over others possess strength,
and those who can wield others’ strength must first win their hearts.
Those who win hearts also secure themselves.

To overcome oneself is to be strong,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
Self-mastery is strength,
and those who are strong must wield others’ strength.
There has never been one who gained themselves while losing others,
nor one who lost themselves while gaining others.

Contentment is wealth,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
The root of sufficiency lies in frugality—
without hoarding wealth, one is rich.

Steadfast action is resolve,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
In ancient times, they did not deem lowliness perilous,
nor poverty shameful,
nor criticism unjust.
Those who never stray from their path endure long.

To die without fading is longevity,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
Though the noble perish, their names do not vanish.
Their fame transmitted to posterity—
where could such be called not long-lived?

**Chapter 34: The Great Dao Flows**

This chapter from the *Nánhuá Jīng* states:
Nothing is more divine than Heaven,
nothing richer than Earth,
nothing greater than the sovereign.
Thus, the sovereign’s virtue aligns with Heaven’s grandeur and Earth’s abundance—
this is how the ancient sages rode upon Heaven and Earth’s riches,
nurturing all things and guiding humanity with the Dao of love and care.
The *Classic* does not claim greatness itself,
yet all things return to it,
and thus it accomplishes the greatness of the world—
following the Dao to nourish the people.

The Great Dao flows,
as the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
Observing the Dao broadly, all things respond in fullness.
To love all things universally is to recognize Heaven and Earth as one.
It can be wielded at will,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
The Great Dao knows no impossibility.
It abides in principle—
when seen as possible, do not rush;
when seen as impossible, do not abandon.
Possible and impossible are like left and right,
like exterior and interior.
All things depend upon it,
as the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
The Dao nourishes all things without transgression,
its blessings extend to ten thousand generations without being called benevolent.
It gives life without complaint,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
The Dao—all things rely upon it for life,
yet none know its virtue.
Its deeds accomplished, it claims no ownership,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
The Great Constant Dao gives life without possession,
its virtue unknown.
It nurtures all things without lordship,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
The Great Constant Dao stores and accumulates without growing rich,
gives and receives without impoverishing others,
nurtures all things without claiming mastery.
Ever without desire, it may be called small;
to have all things return without lordship is great,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
The Great Constant Dao transforms without domination.
The *Dònglíng Jīng* says:
The Dao is neither a treasury of schemes nor an office of affairs;
its work is most subtle, yet it claims no achievement.
Thus, the sage
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
Thus, the sage broadly loves through the Dao,
loving below and thereby accomplishing the great.
Yet it never claims greatness,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
Ancient wise rulers modeled themselves after rivers and seas.
Rivers and seas love what is below,
yet never claim greatness—
all streams return to them, and thus they accomplish greatness.
Thus, they accomplish greatness.
As the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
Through this, the Dao bestows a grand vision upon the world.

**Chapter 35: Grasping the Great Symbol**

This chapter from the *Nánhuá Jīng* states:
In the age of Shénnóng of old,
resting, they lay in ease;
arising, they moved with grace.
The people knew their mothers but not their fathers,
living alongside deer and elk,
plowing for food and weaving for clothing,
without hearts of harm or contention—
this was the age of the Great Dao,
the peak of virtue.
To grasp the Dao is to attain tranquility,
and thus the world is at perfect peace.

To grasp the Great Symbol,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
Those who grasp the Dao are complete in virtue.
Virtue complete is the sage’s Dao.
In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns established the Dao’s succession at the center,
acting without action yet in harmony with the Dao,
speaking without words yet in accord with virtue.
All under Heaven turned toward them.
Turning without harm,
as the *Tóngxuān Jīng* says:
In ancient times, calamities did not arise,
disasters did not occur,
and the world was in peace—
this was the foundation of governance.
Peace, stability, and harmony,
as the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
In ancient times, clever schemes were unused,
all returned to Heaven—
this is called perfect peace, the foundation of governance.
Delight and bait,
as the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
They found their food sweet,
their customs joyful.

「过客止。 [*Nánhuá Jīng*] says: Let them dwell in peace.
*Tóngxuān Jīng* says: In ancient times, the sage’s governance of the people was such—
they allowed each to follow their nature,
to dwell in their homes without interference.
The Dao’s expression in words,
*Tóngxuān Jīng* says: The Dao cannot be articulated in speech,
for speech that is not the Dao is false.
In ancient times, the Dao was practiced without words.
It was subtle, without savor.

*Tóngxuān Jīng* says: The Dao is subtle, without desire,
indifferent, without action.
Subtle, it lacks savor;
without contemplation, yet attained.

It cannot be seen by mere sight,
*Tóngxuān Jīng* says: The Dao cannot be clearly perceived,
for perception that is not true is false.
Those who strain their eyes to see clearly
weary their minds yet fail to grasp it.
If one waits for the eyes to discern,
then governance becomes arduous.
Knowing through sight is called losing the Dao;
in ancient times, one saw without straining.

It cannot be heard by mere listening,
*Tóngxuān Jīng* says: The Dao cannot be fleetly heard,
for hearing that is not true is false.
If one uses their ears to govern,
their minds labor in vain.
If one waits for the ears to hear clearly,
then governance becomes arduous.
Knowing through hearing is called losing the Dao;
in ancient times, one heard without straining.

Its use cannot be exhausted.
*Tóngxuān Jīng* records: Wénzǐ asked: How does the sovereign gain the people’s joy?
Lǎozǐ replied: Like the rivers and seas.
Subtle, without savor,
its use cannot be exhausted,
first small, then great.

In the chapter *Jiāngyù Qìzhī* (第三十六), this passage from *Nánhuá Zhēnjīng* (《南华真经□胠筐篇》) states:
The virtuous cannot stand without the sage’s Dao;
thieves cannot act without the sage’s wisdom.
When virtuous people are few and the unvirtuous many,
then the sage’s wisdom benefits the world little
while harming it greatly.
Rú’s wine was thin yet Hán Dān was besieged;
the sage arises yet great thieves multiply.
Thus, the sage’s wisdom—
though it restrains violence with laws and seals,
and though thieves may be granted their measures and weights,
their jewels and pearls—
the world will not be governed.
Hence it is said:
Fish cannot be removed from the deep;
the world’s useful tools,
should not be displayed to the people.
Tools for measure and weight
produce theft and deceit.

If one would weaken them,
one must first strengthen them;
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: In later ages, though sages valued wisdom to govern the world,
they were in fact valuing tools for theft and deceit.
If one makes measures and weights to weigh with,
then both the people and the weights will be stolen.
If one would weaken them,
one must first strengthen them;
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: In later ages, when sages arose and great thieves multiplied,
if one makes scales and balances to measure with,
then both the people and the scales will be stolen.
If one would abolish them,
one must first establish them;
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: In ancient times, when sages died and their wisdom perished,
great thieves did not arise,
and the world was peaceful without strife.
In later ages, if one makes seals and tokens to trust in,
then both the people and the seals will be stolen.
If one would take them away,
one must first give them.
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: In later ages, if sages’ wisdom did not perish,
great thieves would not cease;
if one uses virtue and righteousness to correct it,
then both the people and virtue will be stolen.
This is called subtle clarity.

*Nánhuá Jīng* (《南华真经□微明篇》) says: The Dao can be yin, can be yang,
can be hidden, can be manifest.
Bound yet expansive, hidden yet clear—
it contains yin and releases yang,
illuminating the three luminaries.
First revering the subtle and minute,
then attaining great clarity.
Softness overcomes strength.

*Tóngxuān Jīng* says: The Dao can be weak, can be strong,
can be yielding, can be firm.
Yielding yet firm, weak yet strong—
it begins in yielding and weakness,
achieves in strength and firmness,
accumulating yielding to become firm,
accumulating weakness to become strong.

Fish cannot be removed from the deep,
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: The beasts of a cage carapace,
if they leave the mountains, will not escape the nets;
the fish that can swallow boats, if they lose their water,
will be tormented by ants.
Thus, birds and beasts do not tire of heights,
fish and turtles do not tire of depths.
If the spring dries up,
fish will gather on land,
moistening each other with breath,
nourishing each other with spittle,
yet unable to save the drought—
it is better to forget each other in rivers and lakes.

Fish create their own conditions in water;
