those who create their own conditions in water
dig ponds to raise and provide for them,
thus it is said: Fish cannot be removed from the deep.

The world’s useful tools,
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: Sages of later ages are the world’s useful tools,
not meant to reveal the world’s wisdom—
for wisdom itself generates theft and deceit.
If thieves steal virtue and righteousness,
along with measures, weights, seals, and tokens,
even with the rewards of carriages and fine robes they cannot be persuaded,
nor with the threats of axes and halberds be deterred.
This is valuing tools for theft
and making them ungovernable—
the fault of later sages’ teachings to the people.
The sage does not weary of simplicity;
it is better to forget each other in Dao and skill.

People create their own conditions in the Dao;
those who create their own conditions in the Dao
live without strife, attaining steadfastness,
thus it is said: The world’s useful tools,
should not be displayed to the people.
Should not be displayed to the people.
*Nánhuá Jīng* says: Abolish the sage, discard wisdom,
and great thieves will cease;
discard jade, shatter pearls,
and petty thieves will not rise;
burn the seals, break the tokens,
and the people will be simple and unrefined;
abolish the measures, discard the weights,
and the people will not contend.
Should not be displayed to the people.»

**Chapter Thirty-Seventh: The Dao Ever Does Nothing**

This chapter of the *Nánhuá Jīng* speaks:
The One Dao does nothing—
it covers and nourishes all things,
vast and boundless, so great!
Thus, the virtuous person cannot but carve the heart to do nothing in the Dao.

To do nothing while doing is called great;
to do nothing while speaking is called virtue.
Great virtue—this is all.

In ancient times, through doing nothing, transformation occurred naturally,
and the world was in great harmony.

The Dao ever does nothing.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Doing nothing is the constant of the Dao,
yet nothing is left undone.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Great Dao does nothing,
yet all things arise naturally and nothing is left undone.

If lords and kings could guard it,
the *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
The virtuous person, governing the world,
does nothing—this is best.
Do nothing,
and thus the nature of life and destiny finds peace.
All things will transform naturally.

The *Nánhuá Jīng* recounts: Hongmeng said to Yunshang:
"You dwell in doing nothing,
and all things transform of themselves.
When transformation stirs to action,
the *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When the people desire to act and move,
it is because the orders are inconsistent.
The heart is not sincere in the Dao,
orders are labored upon by those above,
yet do not respond from below.
I shall govern them with the nameless simplicity.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Ancient sages governed with the Dao,
doing nothing above,
and the people transformed as if by divine will.
The nameless simplicity—
it too shall not desire.

The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
All things have distinct principles,
the Dao does not favor any;
thus it is nameless.
Nameless, hence it does nothing.

Above does nothing,
below also does nothing.
To not desire is to be still.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Ancient wise rulers did nothing—
doing nothing is guarding stillness,
guarding stillness can make one sovereign over the world.
The world will naturally align itself.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When the ruler returns to purity and stillness,
all things will naturally align.

---

**Chapter Thirty-Eighth: The Supreme Virtue Does Not Virtue**

This chapter of the *Nánhuá Jīng* [□ *Zhìběi Yóu* Section] speaks:
In the highest antiquity, the sages practiced the teaching of no-speech,
and through doing nothing, the world was governed.
Thus, the Way of the Three Sovereigns cannot be attained,
the virtue of the Five Emperors cannot be reached,
the benevolence of the Three Kings can be emulated,
the righteousness of Shang and Zhou can be diminished,
the rituals of the Five Hegemons were mere pretense,
and the wars of the Seven States brought only chaos.

Thus it is said:
Lose the Dao, then virtue arises;
lose virtue, then benevolence arises;
lose benevolence, then righteousness arises;
lose righteousness, then ritual arises;
lose ritual, then war arises.

Ritual is the superficial aspect of the Five Constants,
the ornament of the Dao,
the thinnest layer of loyalty and trust—
and the beginning of chaos.

The supreme virtue does not virtue,
and thus virtue exists.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* [□ *Upper Virtue* Section] says:
Nothing is higher than Heaven,
the Dao of Heaven is supreme and does nothing—
the sage follows it,
and the world is settled.

The Dao of Heaven is supreme virtue.
Supreme virtue does not virtue,
and thus virtue exists.

Inferior virtue does not lose virtue,
and thus virtue does not exist.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Nothing is lower than Earth,
human affairs are active and lowly—
the virtuous person embodies this,
and the world falls into disorder.

Human affairs are inferior virtue.
Inferior virtue does not lose virtue,
and thus virtue does not exist.

Supreme virtue does nothing yet does nothing,
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* [□ *Upper Benevolence* Section] says:
In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns of supreme virtue did nothing,
and the world turned to them.

Inferior virtue acts yet does nothing,
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
In later times, the Five Emperors of inferior virtue acted,
and the Four Seas turned to them.

Supreme benevolence acts yet does nothing,
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Three Sovereigns of supreme benevolence,
and the world within the seas turned to them.

Supreme righteousness acts and does nothing,
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Shang and Zhou of supreme righteousness,
and one kingdom turned to them.

Supreme ritual acts, yet none respond,
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Five Hegemons of supreme ritual,
and one village turned to them.
Then they rolled up their sleeves and forced compliance,
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Seven States, lacking these five virtues—morality, virtue, benevolence, righteousness, ritual—
the people did not turn to them.
When they were not turned to, they used force,
and the world fell into great chaos.

Thus, lose the Dao, then virtue arises,
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
The Five Emperors lost the Dao and then used virtue,
but virtue could not be reached.

Lose virtue, then benevolence arises,
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
The Three Kings lost virtue and then used benevolence,
but benevolence could be emulated.

Lose benevolence, then righteousness arises,
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
Shang and Zhou lost benevolence and then used righteousness,
but righteousness could be diminished.

Lose righteousness, then ritual arises.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
The Five Hegemons lost righteousness and then used ritual,
but ritual was mere pretense.

Ritual—
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
In the final age, those who practice ritual
call shallow conduct "benevolence and righteousness,"
call thin conduct "ritual and wisdom,"
and shallow conduct brings little blessing.

Ritual is the ornament of substance.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
The sage practices the teaching of no-speech;
the Dao cannot be attained,
virtue cannot be reached,
benevolence can be emulated,
righteousness can be diminished,
ritual is mere pretense.

Ritual is the ornament of the Dao,
the thinnest layer of loyalty and trust,
and the beginning of chaos.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
In the final age, practicing benevolence and righteousness
can rectify one kingdom,
practicing ritual and music
can rectify one village—
the virtue is thin, and the blessings are small.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Five Hegemons used ritual as a weapon,
and became the origin of chaos.

The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
Ritual—
it is the beginning of chaos.

Foreknowledge—
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Ancient practitioners of the Dao
called deep practice "morality and virtue,"
shallow practice "thick blessings,"
and complete practice "the world submits."

The ornament of the Dao—
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Ancient practitioners of morality and virtue
rectified the world—
thick virtue brings great blessings—
and the beginning of folly.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Five Sovereigns first relied on virtue and the Dao for their renown,
though ancient times were simple, their virtue remained abundant.

The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
In the age of supreme virtue,
their actions were measured and deliberate,
their enlightenment effortless and natural.
Thus, the true gentleman—

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Thus, the true gentleman remains serene without thought,
unconcerned without care,
walks where no path exists,
wanders without weariness,
exits without gates,
enters without walls,
harnessing his spirit,
laying aside his perceptions—
so that within he cultivates his essence,
not adorning its external trappings,
virtue and the Dao require no action.

He dwells in substance, not in ornament.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng [□ Upper Benevolence Section]*—Wénzǐ asked the Master:
How do benevolence, righteousness, and ritual cause chaos?
Because they are thin compared to the Dao’s virtue.

The Master said:
To act in benevolence is to govern through human sorrow and joy,
to act in righteousness is to take and give based on human affairs.
Yet sorrow and joy cannot spread universally,
and exhausting the treasury’s wealth cannot suffice for the extravagance of all people.
Thus, knowing is inferior to practicing the Dao and embodying virtue,
aligning with the natural order of Heaven and Earth—
all things will then self-regulate,
and the world will be sustained.
Benevolence and righteousness cling to human sentiment,
so they are thin—
hence the true gentleman dwells in substance, not in ornament.

He abides in truth, not in its trappings.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng*—Wénzǐ asked the Master:
Why do later ages use benevolence, righteousness, and ritual to justify war and punishment?
Because they are thin compared to the Dao’s virtue.

The Master said:
Ancient times followed nature’s course, calling it the Dao,
and realized its inherent nature, calling it virtue.
When virtue and the Dao were pure and profound,
war and punishment were unnecessary.
In later ages, virtue and nature were lost,
so benevolence and righteousness were employed—
but when these were shallow and insubstantial,
war and punishment were resorted to.
Thus, the true gentleman abides in truth, not in its trappings.
Hence, he discards the latter and embraces the former.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Hence, he discards the latter and embraces the former,
and Heaven’s calamities do not arise.
He who gains this forgets the other,
and thus misfortune departs, blessings come.

The chapter *"Obtaining the One"* (39th):
This chapter of the *Tōng Xuān Jīng* speaks:
In the most ancient age, the sage cultivated virtue beyond praise,
not seeking others’ praise,
but what he used to win the world—
it was not about occupying power or bearing noble titles,
but about mastering the heart’s governance over the world,
and harnessing its natural force.
Later ages had the name "ruler," yet lacked even one man’s praise,
and thus lost the world.
Jié and Zhòu did not rule as kings,
Shāng and Wǔ did not act as rebels.
In ancient times, the sage received countless praises of the world,
yet it did not make him better—
true praise is no praise,
and he grasped the essence of the Dao.
Without praise, he embodied Heaven’s will,
and thus the world endured long.

The chapter *"Obtaining the One"*:
The *Chōng Xū Jīng* says:
The Dao has no form or boundary,
one *qi* transforms into nine variations,
which then return to one.
This One—
the origin of all transformations in Heaven, Earth, and beings.
Heaven obtains the One to remain clear.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
Heaven acts without doing, and thus remains clear.

Earth obtains the One to remain tranquil.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
Earth acts without doing, and thus remains tranquil.

The spirit obtains the One to become responsive.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
He who is serene, unadorned, still, and without action—
this is the way to nourish the spirit,
so that worries cannot intrude,
and malevolent *qi* cannot assail,
and longevity is attained.
Once, Guāngchéngzǐ attained this from the Dao,
and lived a thousand two hundred years without aging.

The valley obtains the One to remain full.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
A hundred rivers flow together,
yet if they do not empty into the sea, they are not valleys.
The source of rivers and seas is deep,
flowing without ceasing.
All things obtain the One to flourish.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
All things cannot but thrive—
is this not their Dao?

Rulers obtain the One to govern the world.
The *Dònglíng Jīng* says:
Ancient rulers did not prize their ceremonies,
nor did they seek praise—
these were truly discerning.
Their governance—

The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
When governance is unified, it is not divided.
Heaven, without remaining clear, would fear fragmentation.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When Heaven’s *qi* is divided, it forms a rainbow,
and the sun cannot endure.
Winter and summer alternate,
moons forget their days,
suns forget their nights.

Earth, without remaining tranquil, would fear upheaval.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When Earth’s *qi* is divided, it leaks and conceals,
and the moon cannot endure.

The spirit, without remaining responsive, would fear exhaustion.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When a man’s *qi* is divided, he falls ill—
his body labors without rest, and thus exhausts,
his spirit is used without cessation, and thus perishes.
The spirit is harmed by the afflictions of joy, anger, and overthinking—
when the spirit is spent, the body dies.
The *Nánhuá Jīng* says:
When the body labors without rest, it becomes worn,
when the spirit is used without cessation, it becomes strained—
strain leads to depletion,
and depletion leads to extinction.

The valley, without remaining full, would fear drying up.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Ditches and ponds overflow in floods,
and wither in drought—
when rivers dry, valleys become empty,
and hills level while depths are sealed.

All things, without flourishing, would fear annihilation.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When things thrive, they bear flowers and fruit—
when things die, they wither and decay.

Rulers, without attaining true elevation, would fear decline.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
Those in high station must not be arrogant—
elevated yet not precarious,
full yet not overflowing—
this is how one endures wealth and honor.
If precarious, elevated, full, yet overflowing,
they cannot endure long.

Thus, elevation is rooted in humility,
and height is founded on lowliness.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng [□ Talisman of Speech Section]* says:
Man has three grievances—
do you know them?
The high in rank are envied by others,
the great in office are hated by their lords,
the rich in wealth are resented by the people.
The higher the rank, the lower the ambition,
the greater the office, the smaller the heart,
the richer the wealth, the broader the giving—
if one cultivates these three, grievances will not arise.
Thus, elevation is rooted in humility,
and height is founded on lowliness.

Hence, rulers call themselves *"gū guǎ"* (lonely and solitary), *"bù gǔ"* (not full).

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* [□ *Dao Yuan* Section] says:
The Sage calls himself *"gū guǎ"* (lonely and solitary), *"bù gǔ"* (not full), returning to his original root.
Is this not because he takes lowliness as his foundation?
Is it not so?

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When the will is weak yet deeds are strong,
he entrusts smallness to encompass greatness.
The Sage follows the Dao’s principle of *subtraction*,
thus humbling himself.

Subtraction brings greatness—
the world endures long.
This is the completion of the Dao’s movement.

Thus, though praised by many, he is without praise.
The *Nán Huá Jīng* [South Flower Sutra] says:
In ancient times, the Sage encompassed heaven and earth,
his virtue extending to all under heaven,
yet he did not know who he was.
Thus, in life he held no rank,
in death no posthumous name,
his substance did not accumulate,
his name did not stand.
This is called the Great Person.

Though the world praises him, it does not add to his virtue or vice—
how could one recognize his true worth?
Thus, praise becomes no praise at all.

He does not wish to be polished like jade,
nor dull like stone.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* [□ *Fú Yán* Section] says:
In ancient times, there were no named officials,
no scheming ministers,
no taskmasters,
no masterminds.
They hid in formlessness,
moved without weariness,
did not seek to precede in fortune,
did not initiate misfortune.
They began in namelessness,
acted when compelled.

If one desires fortune to precede, let him not see it;
if one desires profit, let him distance harm.
Thus, those who act without action and find peace—
if they lose their peace, they face peril;
those who govern without action—
if they lose their governance, they face chaos.

Thus, he does not wish to be polished like jade,
nor dull like stone.

**Reflection—The Dao’s Movement, Chapter Forty**
This chapter of the *Nán Huá Jīng* speaks:
Extreme yin is solemn and still,
extreme yang is radiant and fierce.
Solemn stillness arises from heaven,
radiant fierceness issues from earth.
These two—yin and yang—interact harmoniously,
and all things are born from them.

Some mark their course yet their form is unseen,
ebbing and flowing, full and empty,
darkening and brightening daily,
changing monthly,
ever acting yet unseen in their deeds.
Life sprouts from its origin,
death returns to its end.
Beginning and end oppose the boundless,
yet their limits are unknown.

The cold and heat of heaven and earth—
these are the Dao’s yin and yang in motion,
the generation and transformation of all things.

**Reflection—The Dao’s Movement**
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When yin-qi prevails, it transforms into yang—
this is the Dao’s movement of reversal.

The weak—this is the Dao’s application.
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
When yang-qi prevails, it transforms into yin—
the weak is the Dao’s application.

All things under heaven—
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
The Dao of heaven and earth:
having and not-having generate each other—
this is the transformation of all things.
The *Chōng Xū Jīng* says:
All things generate and transform,
entering and exiting the mechanism of change,
constantly generating, constantly transforming.

They generate from what exists—
The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
All things move and align with yang’s waves—
this is generation.
The *Chōng Xū Jīng* says:
All things that generate
emerge from the mechanism and constantly generate.
All things that generate cannot but generate.

Their end—this is the necessary conclusion of generation.
They generate from non-existence.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
All things stilled align with yin’s virtue—
this is transformation.
The *Chōng Xū Jīng* says:
All things that transform
enter the mechanism and constantly transform.
All things that transform cannot but transform.
Generation is the necessary end of transformation.

The Noble Hear the Dao, Chapter Forty-One

This chapter of the *Nán Huá Jīng* states:
In ancient times, when evil was hidden and distinctions of right and wrong arose,
it was all due to the teachings of the Zhou.
When hidden in the *Wén Huá* (cultural refinements),
the distinctions of Confucianism and Mohism emerged—
the prominence of Confucian and Mohist distinctions
is how the Dao was diminished.

In the Zhou era, many scholars of Confucianism and Mohism flourished,
all relying on their artificial methods and doctrines,
disturbing the world with their teachings—
this could not be remedied.

The so-called Dao-teachings of antiquity,
did they truly hide within Confucianism and the various schools?

There are those who carve their intentions and follow rigid conduct,
departing from the world and its customs,
uttering lofty theories and grievances—
this is mere arrogance.
These are the mountain-valley scholars,
not the world’s withered ones who rush toward the abyss,
the kind favored by Yíqí and Qūyuán.

There are those who speak of benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, trustworthiness,
respectfulness, frugality, and yielding—
this is mere cultivation.
These are the scholars of the common world,
teachers who dwell in ease and study,
the kind favored by Kǒngzǐ and Mèngzǐ.

There are those who speak of great achievements,
establishing great names,
ordering ruler and subject,
rectifying high and low—
this is mere governance.
These are the court scholars,
those who honor the sovereign and strengthen the state,
pursuing combined merit and power,
the kind favored by Yīyīn and Zhōugōng.

There are those who dwell in marshes and wilds,
reside in seclusion and leisure,
fish and drift without purpose—
this is mere inaction.
These are the river-sea scholars,
those who avoid the world and seek repose,
the kind favored by Cháo Fù and Xǔ Yóu.

But the ancient noble ones—
without rigid conduct yet lofty,
without benevolence and righteousness yet cultivated,
without fame and merit yet governing,
without rivers and seas yet at ease—
without forgetting anything,
their hearts are clear and still;
without lacking anything,
the Dao is effortless.
In their simplicity, without bounds,
all beauties follow them—
this is the effortless Dao of heaven and earth,
the still virtue of the Sage,
the great governance of the world.

The Noble Hear the Dao,
and diligently practice it.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
In the upper antiquity, they followed heaven’s effortless Dao—
following heaven is the Dao of governing heaven and earth,
stillness and effortlessness are called the Dao of heaven.

The Middle Hear the Dao,
and hold it as if it were both present and lost.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
In the middle antiquity, they valued the worthy and shaped teachings—
valuing the worthy is the origin of doubt and confusion.
In the middle antiquity, they upheld virtue but did not cherish the Dao,
the Dao faded while virtue flourished.

The Lowly Hear the Dao,
and laugh at it.

The *Tōng Xuān Jīng* says:
In the lower antiquity, they relied on ministers and learned skills—
relying on ministers is the Dao of peril and ruin.
In the lower antiquity, they anxiously clung to their teachings,
fearing they might lose them.

The *Nán Huá Jīng* (in the [Autumn Water] chapter) states:
North Sea If said to River Master:
“Bìyī declined it for the sake of reputation,
and Zhòngnǐ spoke of it for the sake of knowledge—
they both boast of their learning,
laughing at the Dao’s effortlessness,
not unlike how you, River Master, boast of your waters,
laughing at the North Sea’s paucity!”

The frog of a well cannot be spoken to of the sea,
for it is confined to the mud of the well.
The summer insect cannot be spoken to of ice,
for it is bound to the warmth of summer.
The rigid scholar cannot be spoken to of the Dao,
for he is enslaved by the teachings of learning.

Now you have emerged from the banks of the Jīng River,
gazing upon the great sea,
yet you know your own insignificance—
how then can you speak of the great principles?

The waters of the world are none greater than the sea—
all streams flow into it,
yet it does not know when to stop and overflows not;
its ebb drains it,
yet it does not know when to cease and empties not.
Spring and autumn remain unchanged,
and it knows not drought or flood.
Its currents surpass the rivers and seas—
this cannot be measured.

Yet I have never boasted of this,
for I compare my form to heaven and earth,
and receive my *qi* from yin and yang.
I exist between heaven and earth,
like a small stone or small tree in a great mountain.
While my existence is visible,
my understanding is limited—
how dare I boast?

Considering the vastness of the four seas within heaven and earth,
it is not like stacked emptiness in the great marshes—
how could it be vast?
Considering the multitude of China within the sea,
it is not like scattered grains in the great granary—
how could it be many?
Considering the abundance of people and things in the nine regions,
it is not like hairs and fur on a horse’s body—
how could it be abundant?

The five Emperors’ dominions,
the three Kings’ struggles,
the benevolent’s worries,
the capable’s labors—
all are bounded by greatness and smallness.

Seeking the utmost greatness with their utmost smallness,
they are thus confused and cannot find themselves.
From this perspective,
how can they know that heaven and earth suffice to exhaust the utmost greatness?
The one who views greatness from the small
becomes lost in himself—
not laughing is not enough to be the Dao.

The *Nán Huá Jīng* recounts how the debater Gōngsūn Lóng questioned Prince Móu of Wèi:
“I was young when I learned my Master’s Dao,
and grew long in understanding the practice of benevolence and righteousness.
I harmonize what is same,
separate what is different,
and exhaust the arguments of all mouths—
thus I laugh at Zhūangzǐ’s words,
which seem vague and contradictory.”

Prince Móu reclined, sighed deeply,
and laughed toward heaven:
“Have you not heard of the frog of a well?
To the turtle of the Eastern Sea, I say:
‘I delight in the great and boundless—
why does my Master not come to observe it?’

Before the Eastern Sea turtle’s left foot had entered,
its right knee was already bound—
thus it hesitated and withdrew,
telling the frog of its sea:
‘A distance of a thousand *lǐ*,
is not enough to display its greatness;
a height of a thousand *rèn*,
is not enough to exhaust its depth.’

The frog of the well, hearing this,
was startled and lost in confusion.

Observing Zhūangzǐ’s most wondrous words,
you are content with a moment’s gain—
are you not the frog of the well?

You now seek it with careful scrutiny,
and probe it with argumentative skill—
this is truly like using a tube to view heaven,
or a needle to point at earth—
is it not small?

#2 Away you go!

Gōngsūn Lóng’s mouth gaped but could not close,
his tongue rose but could not descend—
thus he faltered and fled.

Zhì Zhī spoke thus:

*"The* South Flower Sutra *records: Zhī Zhī said to Confucius: ‘I have heard that in antiquity, men tilled to eat and wove to clothe, with no hearts of mutual harm or slaughter—this was the height of virtue’s flourishing, the world at great peace. Yet the Yellow Emperor could not attain this virtue, and fought with Xiōng Yóu at Zhúlù’s wilds, staining the earth a hundred *lǐ* with blood. The Tang and Yú ruled, established ministers, the Shāng king was overthrown, the Wǔ king slew Jié. From then on, the strong oppressed the weak, the many tyrannized the few. The kings of Shāng and Zhōu onward were but agents of chaos—how could they be worthy of veneration?

Now you, refining the ways of the martial and the literary, wielding the world’s arguments to teach posterity—your robes are patched, your sash frayed, your conduct thin and insincere. You have lost the ancient simplicity, and the world is in disarray.

**Clarity of the Way seems obscured.**
*The* South Flower Sutra *says: The ancient worthies aligned their inner selves with the Dao, doing good without contrivance, and took Heaven as their companion. The worthies of today, though outwardly conforming to rites and respectful in action, take humanity as their companion.

**Advancing the Way seems like retreat.**
*The* South Flower Sutra *says: The ancient worthies cultivated non-action within, yet did not contrive without. The worthies of today contrive without, yet do not cultivate non-action within.

**The Way’s rectitude seems crooked.**
*The* South Flower Sutra *says: The ancients transformed outwardly yet remained untransformed within—they guarded their inner purity without contriving for the outer world. The worthies of today are transformed within yet untransformed without—they guard the outer world with contrivance, yet neglect their inner selves.

**Great virtue seems like a valley.**
*The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: The ancient worthies savored the Dao without abandoning it. The worthies of today abandon the Dao without savoring it. To forsake the Way and rely on learning is perilous; to discard virtue and employ talent is to invite ruin.

**Pure white seems like shame.**
*The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: The worthies of high antiquity, though pure and unadorned, were called ‘great white’ as if shamed. They returned to simplicity, their essence unspoiled. The worthies of later ages, treating cleverness as governance, labor their minds yet fail to enlighten; those who rely on wit, though they strain their hearts, achieve nothing.

**Ample virtue seems insufficient.**
*The* South Flower Sutra *says: The worthies of high antiquity had virtue so vast it seemed lacking. *The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: The superior worthies had virtue so broad it seemed insufficient. Yet today’s worthies, though they master a single learning or refine a single skill, can only offer partial explanations—unfit to broadly apply to governance and teaching.

**Great sound is rare in voice.**
*The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: The worthies of high antiquity, whose Dao was most vast, had no sound or voice—they returned to simplicity at their end, and the people acted without guile. The worthies of later ages, though they excel in minor arts, break harmony; those skilled in trifles lose their naturalness. To debate a single phrase, to master a single technique—though one may speak eloquently, it is unfit to broadly apply to governance and teaching.

**Great form has no shape.**
*The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: The worthies of high antiquity, whose simplicity was most vast, had no fixed form. Their eyes, though clear, did not see; their ears, though still, did not hear. Their mouths, though closed, did not speak; their hearts, though at rest, did not scheme. They abandoned cleverness, returned to the uncarved block, preserved their spirit, discarded knowledge—neither liking nor disliking—this is called Great Harmony. The Dao is hidden, nameless.

*The* South Flower Sutra *says: To revere antiquity while belittling the present is the way of the shallow scholar. Yet if one views the present through the lens of the Jié Wéi lineage, who could avoid eccentric learning? Only the truly wise, with profound virtue and perfect humanity, can wander the world in the Dao without eccentricity, follow humanity with non-action without losing their true nature. They do not teach through cleverness, nor do they cling to learning—they receive meaning from the Dao, not from study.

*The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: To dwell in obscurity, speak in silence, govern with non-action, yet see deeds accomplished without knowing how—this is the Dao of the Sage.

For only the Dao—
*The* Mysterious Transformation Sutra *says: The ancient true worthies were those whose nature aligned with the Dao. They cultivated within yet did not contrive without, wandering the roots of Heaven and Earth. Unconcerned with the dust of the world, they roamed freely in the business of non-doing. Mechanical cleverness did not burden their minds; they knew without learning, saw without looking, achieved without action, spoke eloquently without effort—adrift in non-action. The world transformed of itself; this was the true worthies’ pure wandering in the uncarved Dao.

**Generosity in lending, yet completion is assured.**
*The* South Flower Sutra *records: Lǎo Dān said to Yáng Zǐ Jū: ‘The governance of the enlightened king covers the world yet seems not of his own doing. He transforms and lends to all things, yet the people do not rely on him. He has no ostentatious name, yet things take joy in him. He stands firm yet unobtrusive, wandering in the Dao—he is nonexistent.’"*

The ancient **true worthies** stood in the realm of **non-lending** (*bù dài*), adrift in non-action. **Non-lending** means no excess—nothing is thrust upon things. The *Chapter of the Dao’s Generation* (Fourth Twenty-Two) of the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (*Tōng Xuán Jīng*) states: The Dao’s generation begins with the **origin** (*yuán*) and **production** (*chǎn*), having no beginning. It begins with **softness and weakness** (*róuruò*), yet completes as **firmness and strength** (*gāngqiáng*). Those who seek **firmness** must guard it with **weakness**, accumulating weakness to achieve strength. Observing what they accumulate reveals the Dao of **preservation and decay** (*cúnwáng*).

In the **Great Ancient Age**, the **sage** (*shèngrén*) used the Dao to **settle** (*zhèn*) it and **reduce** (*sǔn*) the **rushing qi** (*chōngqì*), guarding softness without forcing strength. By **transforming** (*huà*) himself through **merit and renown** (*gōngmíng*), he thus achieved his kingship.

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Nine Guarding Chapters*) says: Before Heaven and Earth took form, they were **dim and indistinct**, **mysterious and unified** (*yǎoyǎo mínghún ér wéi yī*), **silent and lucid** (*jìrán chéngqīng*)—this was the Dao. The Dao generates **One** (*yī*), which is Heaven (*tiān*); **One** generates **Two** (*èr*), which is Earth (*dì*), **pure and refined** (*qīngwēi*) for Heaven, **dense and turbid** (*chóngzhuó*) for Earth. **Two** generates **Three** (*sān*), which is **Human** (*rén*), with **vital essence and qi** (*jīngqì*) as the human form. **Three** generates **ten thousand things** (*wànwù*), with **coarse qi** (*cūqì*) forming insects. **Firmness and softness** (*gāngróu xiāngchéng*) intermingle, and thus ten thousand things are born.

All things **carry yin** (*fù yīn*) and **embrace yang** (*bào yáng*), hence **wood fruits** (*mùshí*) grow from the **heart** (*xīn*), and **grass fruits** (*cǎoshí*) from the **flower** (*yīng*). The **rushing qi** (*chōngqì*) harmonizes into **balance** (*hé*). The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Rushing qi** (*chōngqì*) harmonizes into **balance** (*hé*), and **balance** (*hé*) resides at the **center** (*zhōngyāng*), hence **embryonic and oviparous** (*tāiluǎn*) beings are born from the center. The *South Flower Sutra* says: All things generate from **form** (*xíng*). Those with **nine orifices** (*jiǔqiàng*) are born from the **embryo** (*tāi*), those with **eight orifices** (*bāqiàng*) from the **egg** (*luǎn*). The *Sutra of Penetrating Void* (*Chōng Xū Jīng*) says: **Rushing qi** (*chōngqì*) in **harmony** (*hé*) forms the human.

What people abhor, the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: The **petty man’s** (*xiǎorén*) emotional nature loves **strength** (*qiáng*) and abhors **weakness** (*ruò*), loves **firmness** (*gāng*) and abhors **softness** (*róu*), yet knows not the **loss** (*shī*). Only the **lonely, widowed, and childless** (*gūguǎ bùgǔ*), the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Chapter of the Dao’s Origin*) says: **Kings and nobles** (*wánggōng*) possess **merit and renown** (*gōngmíng*), while the lonely, widowed, and childless lack it. They guard **humility** (*qiān*) and **reduce** (*sǔn*), yet kings and nobles take this as their **standard** (*chēng*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: In ancient times, the **sage** (*shèngrén*) daily **reduced** (*sǔn*) his **rushing qi** (*chōngqì*), never daring to **be self-satisfied** (*zì mǎn*), daily advancing through **humility** (*qiān*). His **virtue and power** (*dé*) did not **decay** (*cuì*), for this was the **Way of Heaven** (*Tiāndào*). Thus, things may be **reduced** (*sǔn*) yet **benefit** (*yì*), or **benefit** (*yì*) yet **be reduced** (*sǔn*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Chapter of the Symbols*) says: Those with **little virtue yet much favor** (*dé shǎo ér chǒng duō*) are **mocked** (*jī*), those with **low talent yet high station** (*cái xià ér wèi gāo*) are **in peril** (*wēi*), and those with **no great merit yet thick rewards** (*hòu lù*) are **humiliated** (*wēi*). Thus, things may be **reduced** (*sǔn*) yet **benefit** (*yì*), or **benefit** (*yì*) yet **be reduced** (*sǔn*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **No greater blessing** (*fú*) than **no calamity** (*wú huò*), **no greater advantage** (*lì*) than **no loss** (*bù sǎng*). Thus, things may be **reduced** (*sǔn*) yet **benefit** (*yì*), or **benefit** (*yì*) yet **be reduced** (*sǔn*).

What people teach, the *South Flower Sutra* says: **Confucian scholars** (*rú*) **adjust** (*qū*) the **rites** (*lǐ*), teaching **respect** (*jìng*) and **humility** (*qiān*). I also teach this. The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Softness and weakness** (*róuruò*) are the **companions of life** (*shēngzhī tú*). **Softness and weakness** (*róuruò*) are **supreme** (*shàng*). **Harshness and rigidity** (*qiángliáng*) cannot **attain their end** (*bù dé qí sǐ*), for in the **end times** (*mòshì*), those who **harbor harsh and rigid qi** (*bào qiángliáng zhī qì*) cannot endure long—**harshness and rigidity** (*qiángliáng*) lead to **death** (*sǐ*). I will take this as my **teacher** (*jiào fù*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Firmness and strength** (*gāngqiáng*) are the **companions of death** (*sǐ zhī tú*). **Firmness and strength** (*gāngqiáng*) are **inferior** (*xià*). The **Supreme Softness of the World** (Chapter Forty-Three) of the *South Flower Sutra* (□ *Chapter of the Way of Heaven*) states: **Vacuity, stillness, tranquility, and ease** (*xūjìng tiándàn*), **silent, empty, and non-acting** (*jìmò wúwéi*), **pure and uncarved** (*chúncuì sūpǔ*)—this is the **balance of Heaven and Earth** (*Tiāndì zhī píng*) and the **supreme Dao of virtue** (*Dàodé zhī zhì*), the **root of ten thousand things** (*wànwù zhī běn*).

To **illuminate** (*míng*) this **non-action** (*wúwéi*) while ruling as **sovereign** (*jūn*), **Emperor Yao** (*Táo Tāng*) was a **lord** (*wáng*). To **illuminate** (*míng*) this **non-action** (*wúwéi*) while serving as **subject** (*chén*), **Emperor Shun** (*Yú Shùn*) was a **minister** (*shì*). The **sage-king** (*shèngwáng*) **dwells** (*chǔ*) in this **non-action** (*wúwéi*) at the **ruling position** (*jūnshàng*), a **virtue of emperors and kings** (*Dìshǐng Tiānzǐ zhī dé*). **Lǎo Dān** stood in this **non-action** (*wúwéi*) as a **subject**, the **Dao of the Mysterious Sage** (*Xuánshèng sūwáng zhī dào*).

The **recluse** (*yìshì*) **retired** (*tuìjū*) in this **vacuity and stillness** (*xūjìng*), wandering freely in the **rivers and seas** (*Jiānghǎi*), and the **mountain and forest dwellers** (*shānlín zhī shì*) **submitted** (*fú*). The **scholar** (*shìrén*) **advanced** (*jìnwéi*) through this **tranquility and ease** (*tiándàn*), **nurturing the world** (*fǔshì*), and thus achieved **great merit and renown** (*gōng dà míng xiǎn*), with the world unified in **one** (*yī*).

**Stillness** (*jìng*) brings **sageliness** (*shèng*), **action** (*dòng*) brings **kingship** (*wáng*). **Non-action** (*wúwéi*) yet **is honored** (*zūn*), **simplicity and purity** (*pǔsū*) yet the world **cannot rival** (*mò néng yǔ zhī zhēng měi*)—thus the ancient sage said: *"I thereby know the benefit of non-action, the teaching of silence, the advantage of non-action—few in the world can attain it."* **Non-action** (*wúwéi*) is indeed beneficial.

The **Supreme Softness of the World**, the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: The **supreme softness** (*zhì róu*) of the world **gallops** (*chíchèng*) over the **supreme firmness** (*zhì jiān*) of the world. **Galloping** (*chíchèng*) over the **supreme firmness** (*zhì jiān*) of the world.

The *South Flower Sutra* says: **Water flows** (*shuǐ liú*) in **formlessness** (*wúxíng*), **emerging** (*fāxiè*) in the **Great Clarity** (*Tàiqīng*). The **overflowing** (*shuǐ zhī jū*) of water is **non-acting** (*wúwéi*) yet **its talent** (*cái*) is **naturally** (*zìrán*) displayed. **No gap** (*wújiàn*) enters **no gap** (*wújiàn*), the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **No gap** (*wújiàn*) enters **no gap** (*wújiàn*).

The *South Flower Sutra* says: **Heaven’s Gate** (*Tiānmén*) is **no gap** (*wújiàn*). All things **emerge** (*chū*) from **no gap** (*wújiàn*). **Gap** (*yǒu*) cannot **with Gap** (*yǒu*) be **Gap** (*yǒu*), **Gap** (*yǒu*) must **emerge** (*chū*) from **no gap** (*wújiàn*), yet **no gap** (*wújiàn*) has **gap** (*yǒu*). **Gap** (*yǒu*) emerges, **Gap** (*yǒu*) dies, **Gap** (*yǒu*) emerges, **Gap** (*yǒu*) enters. **Entering and emerging** (*rù chū*) yet **no form is seen** (*wú xiàn qí xíng*), this is called **Heaven’s Gate** (*Tiānmén*).

I thereby know, the *South Flower Sutra* says: **Lǎo Dān** stood in **non-action** (*wúwéi*) as a **subject** (*chén*), the **Dao of the Mysterious Sage** (*Xuánshèng sūwáng zhī dào*). The benefit of **non-action** (*wúwéi*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Chapter of Naturalness*) says: The **Dao of Heaven and Earth** (*Tiāndì zhī dào*) is **complete** (*bèi*) through **non-action** (*wúwéi*), **obtained** (*dé*) without **seeking** (*qiú*). I thereby know its **non-action** (*wúwéi*) yet **is beneficial** (*yǒu yì*).

---

**The teaching of the unsaid** (*bùyán zhī jiào*), the *South Flower Sutra* says: In the **Age of High Antiquity** (*shànggǔ*), the **Sages** (*shèngrén*) practiced **the teaching of the unsaid** (*bùyán zhī jiào*), established **non-teaching** (*lì bùjiào*), sat without debate (*zuò bùyì*), **void** (*xū*) yet they went, **substantial** (*shí*) yet they returned. Thus, **the teaching of the unsaid** (*bùyán zhī jiào*) exists—those who act in **non-action** (*wúwéi*) yet their **heart is fulfilled** (*xīnchéng*).

The **Great Dao** (*dàdào*) does not proclaim itself, the **Great Discourse** (*dàbiàn*) does not speak. The benefit of **non-action** (*wúwéi*), the *South Flower Sutra* says: The **Mysterious Ancient Lord** (*Xuán gǔ zhī jūn*), in **non-action** (*wúwéi*) ruled the **world** (*tiānxìa*), merely **Heaven’s virtue** (*Tiāndé*) was all. **Non-action** (*wúwéi*) yet it is **Heaven** (*tiān*), **non-action** (*wúwéi*) yet it is **virtue** (*dé*). Above, **non-action** (*wúwéi*) prevails; below, **non-action** (*wúwéi*) also prevails. The **world** (*tiānxià*) rarely attains this. The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: The **root of benefit** (*yǒuyì zhī běn*) lies in **non-benefit** (*wúyì*). Thus, **non-benefit** (*wúyì*) is harmful to governance, yet **beneficial to chaos** (*yǒuyì yú luàn*), and the **Mysterious Sage** (*Xuánshèng*) does not act. **Chapter of Name and Body** (*Míng yǔ shēn zhāng*) **Forty-Fourth**: This chapter of the *South Flower Sutra* speaks: **The unbenevolent** (*bùrén*) yet greedy for **wealth, honor, fame, and profit** (*fùguì mínglì zhī huì*), in turn harm their **body** (*shēn*). The **King of Chu** (*Chǔ wēiwáng*) heard of **Zhuang Zhou’s** (*Zhuāng Zhōu*) virtue (*xián*), sent envoys with **rich gifts** (*hòubì zhī*), offering him the position of **prime minister** (*xiàng*), and **Zhuang Zhou** told the **Chu envoy** (*Chǔ shǐ*): "A thousand **gold** (*jīn*), heavy **profit** (*zhònglì*); **prime minister** (*xiàng*), an **exalted position** (*zūnwèi*). Have you not seen the **sacrificial ox** (*xīniú*) of the **autumnal sacrifice** (*jiāojì*)? Nurtured and fed for **many years** (*shùsuì*), adorned with **embroidered patterns** (*wénxiù*), given **grain and beans** (*chúshū*), until it is led into the **grand temple** (*tàimiào*), even if it wished to be a **lonely calf** (*gǔdú*), could it obtain this? I hasten to depart, lest I be **defiled** (*wū*). **Rulers** (*yǒuguó zhě*) are **captured** (*jī*), yet **Zhuang Zhou** served none his whole life, **self-contented** (*zìkuài*) in the **Dao of non-action** (*wúwéi zhī dào*).

The **Lord of Lu** (*Lǔ jūn*) heard that **Yan He** (*Yán Huò*) was a **man of the Dao** (*dézhī rén*), sent envoys with **gifts** (*bì*) first. **Yan He** did not accept them, truly **hating wealth and honor** (*zēn wù fùguì*). Hence it is said: The **true Dao** (*dàozhī zhēn*) is used to **cultivate the body** (*zhìshēn*), its **leftover threads** (*xùyú*) for **states**, its **coarse husks** (*tǔjū*) to **govern the world** (*zhìtiānxìa*). Observed thus, the **functions of emperors and kings** (*dìwáng zhī gōng*) are the **leftover tasks** (*shèngrén zhī yúshì*) of the **Sages**, not what is used to **perfect the body and nurture life** (*wánshēn yǎngshēng*). Today’s **common scholars** (*jūnzǐ*) often **endanger their body and abandon life** (*wēishēn qìshēng*) to **sacrifice themselves for things** (*xùnwù*) in **name and profit** (*mínglì*). Is this not tragic?

Now, there is a man here who uses the **Sui Hou’s jade** (*Suìhóu zhī zhū*) to shoot a **thousand-ren high bird** (*qiān rèn zhī què*). The **world** (*shì*) would surely **mock him** (*xiào*), why? Because what he **employs** (*suǒyòng*) is **heavy** (*zhòng*), yet what he **desires** (*suǒyào*) is **light** (*qīng*). Life (*shēng*) is not merely the **weight** (*zhòng*) of **Sui Hou’s jade**—**name and profit** (*mínglì*) destroy the **body** (*shēn*). **Knowing sufficiency** (*zhīzú*) prevents **humiliation** (*rǔ*), **knowing restraint** (*zhīzhǐ*) avoids **peril** (*dài*). Thus, one may **endure long** (*chángjiǔ*). Hence this chapter was composed.

**Name and body** (*míng yǔ shēn*), which is more **intimate** (*qīn*)? The *South Flower Sutra* says: **Bo Yi, Shu Qi, Bao Jiao, and Jie Zi Yi** (*Bó Yí, Shū Qí, Bào Jiāo, Jiè Zǐ Tū*), these four **men** (*zǐ*), **valued name yet despised death** (*zhòngmíng qīngsǐ*). **Body and wealth** (*shēn yǔ huò*), which is more **abundant** (*duō*)? The *South Flower Sutra* says: In ancient times, **Jie** (*Jié*) killed **Guan Longfeng** (*Guān Lóngfēng*), **Zhou** (*Zhòu*) killed **Wang Zi Biduan** (*Wáng Zǐ Bǐgān*), hence **Guan Longfeng** was **beheaded** (*zhǎn*), **Wang Zi Biduan** was **flayed** (*pōu*), **Peng Hong** was **castrated** (*jiāng*), and **Zi Yu** was **minced** (*mí*). These four **virtuous men** (*xiánzhě*) were **greedy for fame and profit** (*hǎomínglì zhě*), yet their **bodies** (*shēn*) were not spared from **slaughter** (*lù*).

**Gain and loss** (*dé yǔ wáng*), which brings more **suffering** (*bìng*)? The *South Flower Sutra* says: These are the **kindred** (*shǔlèi*) of **Bo Yi, Shu Qi, Wang Zi Biduan, and Zi Yu** (*Bó Yí, Shū Qí, Bǐgān, Zǐ Yú*). Thus, it **discourses the Dao** (*lùndào*) of **long life, bodily safety, and joyful intent** (*chángshēng āntǐ lèyì zhī dào*), is it not **far removed** (*bù yì yuǎn*)?

Hence, **excessive love** (*shèn ài*) brings **great expenditure** (*dàfèi*), the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: The **patterns of tigers and leopards** (*hǔbào zhī wén*) come to be **shot** (*shè*), the **agility of monkeys and barbarians** (*yóudí zhī jié*) comes to be **trapped** (*gé*). **Chimes** (*míngzhuó*) are **self-destructive** (*zìhuǐ*) by **sound** (*shēng*), **oils and candles** (*gāojìng*) are **self-consumed** (*zìjiān*) by **brightness** (*míng*). **Martial prowess** (*wǔyǒng*) perishes from **strength and force** (*yīnqiángliáng*), **eloquent scholars** (*biànshì*) perish from **ability** (*yīnnéng*). **Excessive hoarding** (*duōzàng*) leads to **great loss** (*hòuwáng*).

The *South Flower Sutra* says: **Forest trees** (*shān mù*) **self-destroy** (*zìkòu*), **oils and candles** (*gāohuǒ*) **self-cook** (*zìjiān*). **Sweet osmanthus** (*guì*) can be **eaten** (*kěyǐ*), hence it is **peeled** (*bō*), **lacquer** (*qī*) can be **used** (*kěyǐ*), hence it is **cut** (*gē*). All **know** (*rén jiē zhī*) that **sweet osmanthus and lacquer** (*guì qī*) have **use** (*yòng*), yet **do not know** (*wúzhī*) that the **oak tree** (*lìshù*), though **useless** (*wúyòng*), **preserves its life until Heaven’s end** (*dézhōng tiānnián*). **Knowing sufficiency** (*zhīzú*) prevents **humiliation** (*rǔ*).

The *South Flower Sutra*, **Zeng Zi** said: The **Son of Heaven** (*tiānzǐ*) cannot **obtain subjects** (*dé chén*), **feudal lords** (*zhūhóu*) cannot **obtain friends** (*dé yǒu*). **Nurturing the spirit** (*yǎngzhì zhě*) **forgets name** (*wàngmíng*), **nurturing the form** (*yǎngxíng zhě*) **forgets profit** (*wànglì*), **attaining the Dao** (*zhìdào zhě*) **forgets the heart** (*wàngxīn*). **Knowing restraint** (*zhīzhǐ*) avoids **peril** (*dài*).

The *Sutra of the Void and the Substantial* says: **Not greedy for wealth** (*bùtān huò*), why **covet profit** (*hé xiàn lì*)? **Not greedy for honor** (*bùtān guì*), why **covet name** (*hé xiàn míng*)? This is called **conforming to the people** (*shùnmín*). The *South Flower Sutra* says: **Name and profit** (*mínglì èr zhě*), **agitate the spirit** (*bózhì*). **Not stirring the heart** (*bùdòng xīnzhōng*) is **upright** (*zhèng*), **upright** (*zhèng*) is **void and tranquil** (*xūjìng wúwéi*) yet **non-doing** (*wúwéi*), and thus **nothing remains undone** (*wú bùwéi*). One may **endure long** (*chángjiǔ*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Jade and jade-like vessels** (*bìyàn zhī qì*), the **craft of carving** (*zhuāngyǒu zhī gōng*); **knives and hatchets** (*liàngzhǐ duànlì*), the **strength of whetstones** (*dǐlì zhī lì*). **Cunning hares die, yet hounds are boiled** (*jiǎotù sǐ ér lièquǎn pēng*), **high fowl are exhausted, yet fine bows are stored** (*gāoqín jìn ér liánggōng cáng*), **enemy states are annihilated, yet scheming ministers perish** (*dígú miè ér móuchén wáng*). **When the task is complete, withdraw** (*gōngsuì shēntuì*), such is **Heaven’s Dao** (*Tiāndào*). One may **endure long** (*chángjiǔ*).

**Chapter of Great Achievement as if Deficient** (*Dàchéng ruò quē zhāng*) **Forty-Fifth**: This chapter of the *South Flower Sutra* speaks: Though **Heaven and Earth** (*tiāndì*) are vast, their **transformations** (*huà*) are **uniform** (*jūn*). Though **all things** (*wànwù*) are numerous, their **governance** (*zhì*) is **one** (*yī*). Though **people** (*réncú*) are many, their **master** (*zhǔ*) is **only the lord** (*wéijūn*). The lord **originates from virtue** (*yuán yú dé*) yet **achieves through Heaven** (*chéng yú tiān*), hence it is said: The **Mysterious Ancient Lord** (*Xuán gǔ zhī jūn*), in **non-action** (*wúwéi*) ruled the **world** (*tiānxìa*), merely **Heaven’s virtue** (*Tiāndé*) was all. Thus, the **Sages of High Antiquity** (*dàgǔ shèngrén*) were **greatly skilled yet seemingly clumsy** (*dàqiǎo ruò zhuō*), **greatly eloquent yet seemingly tongue-tied** (*dàbiàn ruò nè*). **Embodying the Dao** (*tǐdào*), they were **void and tranquil** (*qīngjìng wúwéi*), **the rectifiers of the world** (*tiānxià zhī zhèng*), **teaching the use of the inexhaustible** (*jiào qí yòng wúqióng*). Thus they could **endure long** (*chángjiǔ*), hence this chapter was composed.

**Great achievement as if deficient** (*dàchéng ruò quē*), the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Heaven’s roundness** (*Tiān yuán*) does not **conform to compasses** (*zhōng guī*), **Earth’s square** (*dìfāng*) does not **conform to rulers** (*zhōng jǔ*). The **Dao** (*dào*) is the **greatest** (*zhìdà*), yet it has **no compasses or rulers** (*wú guījǔ*). Its **use is not exhausted** (*qí yòng bùbì*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Void and tranquil** (*qīngjìng*) is the **mirror of the Dao** (*dào zhī jiàn*). **Void and pure** (*qīngxū*) is **Heaven’s brightness** (*tiān zhī míng*). **One yet unchanging** (*yī ér bùbiàn*), **the utmost of tranquility** (*jìng zhī zhì*). **Great fullness as if empty** (*dàyíng ruò chōng*), the *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Heaven’s rectitude** (*tiānzhèng*) does not **conform to hooks** (*zhōng gōu*), **Earth’s straightness** (*dìzhí*) does not **conform to plumb lines** (*zhōng shéng*). **Virtue** (*dé*) is the **greatest** (*zhìdà*), yet it has **no hooks or plumb lines** (*wú gōushéng*). Its **use is inexhaustible** (*qí yòng bùqióng*).

The *Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says: **Non-action** (*wúwéi*) is the **constant of the Dao** (*dào zhī cháng*). **Non-action** yet it is **Heaven** (*wúwéi wéizhī tiān*), merely **Heaven’s virtue** (*tiāndé*) was all.

**The Way’s Great Straightness as if Bent**
*The Zhuangzi* says:
In ancient times, there was the constant Dao of the world—
the curved was not measured by hooks,
the straight was not measured by plumb lines,
**the greatest straightness as if bent.**

**The Way’s Great Skill as if Clumsy**
*The Zhuangzi* says:
In ancient times, there was the constant virtue of the world—
the round was not measured by compasses,
the square was not measured by squares,
**the greatest skill as if clumsy.**

**The Way’s Great Eloquence as if Tongue-Tied**
*The Zhuangzi* says:
The Great Dao does not speak,
the greatest eloquence does not argue,
**the greatest eloquence as if tongue-tied.**

**Agitation Overcomes Cold**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
When **yang-qi** prevails, it transforms into **yin**,
thus **skill cannot be overfilled.**

**Tranquility Overcomes Heat**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
When **yin-qi** prevails, it transforms into **yang**,
thus **eloquence cannot be pushed to extremes.**

**Pure Tranquility as the World’s Rectitude**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
The ancient sages, who practiced **non-action**,
guarded **stillness**—
guarding stillness could rectify the world.

---
**Chapter 46: When the World Has the Dao**
This chapter from *The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Upper Virtue Section*) says:
In ancient times, those who wielded arms did not covet lands or riches,
but acted when danger loomed, pacifying chaos and removing harm for the people—
thus turning peril into security.
The **Red Sovereign** brought great calamity,
so the **Yellow Emperor** subdued him.
**Gonggong** caused the flood disaster,
so **Zhuànxū** executed him.
Now the Seven States are without the Dao—
greedy, covetous men ravage the world,
the people restless, finding no peace.
When the Dao is absent, arms are used—
warhorses roam the suburbs,
the world descends into great disorder.

**When the World Has the Dao**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Precision and Sincerity Section*) says:
In ancient times, those who summoned the distant
let them act in **non-action**,
and those nearby
were told to do nothing—
only those who walked secretly in the Dao
could truly possess it.

**Turning Warhorses to Manure**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Precision and Sincerity Section*) says:
Turning warhorses to manure,
their tracks do not reach beyond the farthest lands—
this is called **sitting while galloping, floating while submerged.**

**When the World Lacks the Dao**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
In the Spring and Autumn period, when the world lacked the Dao,
war became the master of chaos.
Warhorses roamed the suburbs.
*The Sutra of the Cavern Spirit* says:
Now, in the Spring and Autumn era, few rulers had the Dao,
while many lacked it—
one cannot abandon the root for the branches,
nor use war as the primary means.

**No Greater Crime Than Covetousness**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
**No greater crime than lacking the Dao.**
Fighting over trivial matters, unable to restrain the heart—
this is **the army of rage**, and thus it fails.
This is the Dao of Heaven.

**No Greater Calamity Than Insatiability**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
**No greater calamity than lacking virtue.**
Taking others’ lands, desiring their wealth—
this is **the army of greed**, and thus it perishes.
This is the Dao of Heaven.

**No Greater Blame Than Desiring More**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
**No greater blame than lacking virtue.**
Relying on the greatness of one’s state,
boasting of one’s people’s multitude,
and seeking to surpass enemy nations—
this is **the army of arrogance**, and thus it is destroyed.
This is the Dao of Heaven.

Thus, **knowing sufficiency is sufficiency itself.**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
In ancient times, those who nurtured life knew sufficiency—
thus crime did not burden them.

---
**Chapter 47: Not Leaving the Door**
This chapter from *The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
In ancient times, rulers taught without leaving their doors,
knowing thus the governance of the world—
observing things through things,
knowing people through people.
First cultivating oneself, then one could govern the people.
Ordering the home and managing affairs,
only then could one extend to officials and the world—
thus the world was governed.

Later generations issued ever-farther decrees,
bringing chaos and confusion,
while those who knew governance grew fewer—
for they did not cultivate their own roots.

Thus the ancient sages practiced **non-action**,
rectifying themselves,
and without commands, their virtue took hold;
without movement, their wisdom was known;
without being seen, their names were honored;
without action, their deeds were complete—
the world self-governed.

**Not Leaving the Door, Knowing the World**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Precision and Sincerity Section*) says:
In ancient times, teaching did not leave the door
to know the world’s governance—
this speaks of **precision and sincerity in non-action**, emanating from within.
*The Zhuangzi* says:
The ancients governed their inner selves by the Dao,
and did not govern the outer.

**Without Gazing Through the Window, Seeing the Dao of Heaven**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
Not gazing through the window to see the Dao of Heaven—
this speaks of **spirit and qi**, pure and tranquil, moving with Heaven.

**The Farther One Goes, the Less One Knows**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* (□ *Nine Guardians Section*) says:
The farther one’s actions extend,
the less one knows—
this speaks of **spirit and vitality** in **action**,
which must not be allowed to stray outward.
*The Zhuangzi* says:
Now people govern the outer while neglecting the inner.

**The Less One Knows**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
When **precision and sincerity** stray outward,
and **thought and calculation** scatter inward,
one cannot govern.
When the **form and spirit** are used far,
what is neglected grows near—
thus one knows less.

*The Zhuangzi* says:
Now people guard the outer while neglecting the inner.

Thus the sage,
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
**The root of no-command lies in rectifying oneself.**

**Without Movement, Knowing**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
The ancient sages cultivated the root—
their bodies issued no commands yet restrained,
they did not act yet were benevolent,
they did not move yet were wise.

**Without Being Seen, Named**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
The ancient sages cultivated the root—
their eyes did not gaze yet were clear,
their ears did not listen yet were sharp,
they were not seen yet their names were honored.

**Without Action, Achieved**
*The Sutra of Penetrating Mystery* says:
The ancient sages cultivated the root—
they did not speak yet were trusted,
they did not seek yet obtained,
they did not act yet succeeded.

---
**Chapter 48: Daily Learning Increases**
This chapter from *The Zhuangzi* says:
In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns—
the people used knotted cords and had no writing,
and in such times, the world was at **great peace**—
this was the governance of the Dao.

Now it has come to this:
people stretch their necks and lift their feet to attend lectures,
saying, *“There is a sage in such-and-such a place,”*
and with exhausted provisions, they rush to learn—
abandoning their inner duties and outer service to their lords.
Thus, warhorses’ tracks connect the states,
and chariot ruts stretch a thousand *li*—
the world descends into great disorder.

This is the chaos of learning—
it is the fault of the rulers above who love knowledge and learning.

For those who love knowledge and learning to bring chaos to the world,
it began with the Three Kings and their descendants,
who still valued literary arts as the way.

They abandoned the simple and pure people,
and delighted in the scheming, laboring, and cleverly learned flatterers,
discarding the tranquil, effortless Dao,
and taking pleasure in the clamor and superficiality of words.

The clamor and superficiality of words,
the shallow and ornate learning of later scholars,
has already spread its disorder across the world.

*Daily Learning Increases*, the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The Zhou era was vast in learning and abundant in eloquence,
yet it could not escape chaos.

Now, if we take petty learning as education,
we cannot attain the great Dao’s clarity and establish the sage.
If we cling only to written laws,
governing the world will be difficult indeed.

*The Way Daily Decreases*
The *Nanhua Jing* says: In ancient times, those who cultivated the Dao did so by daily diminishing.
Diminishing again and again,
the *Nanhua Jing* says: Diminishing again and again.
Until they reached *wuwei*.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: Until they reached *wuwei*.
*Wuwei* yet nothing remained undone.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: *Wuwei* yet nothing remained undone—
above, there was *wuwei*,
below, there was also *wuwei*.

To take the world,
one must always act with *wu* (no affairs).
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient skillful rulers,
took the world yet had no affairs.

The root of *wu* (no affairs) lies in the Dao’s daily diminishing.
When affairs arise,
the *Tongxuan Jing* says: Wood breeds worms, which return as its rot;
people breed affairs, which return as their thieves.

Each follows what they delight in,
only to harm themselves in return.
With learning as their affair,
and affairs used to stop affairs,
it is like stirring water yet hoping for clarity—
difficult indeed,
and insufficient to take the world.

Insufficient to take the world.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Those of high station cannot be burdened with affairs;
if affairs burden them, governing is difficult,
and insufficient to take the world.

**Chapter Forty-Nine: The Sage Has No Fixed Heart**

This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* states: In later ages, those who promulgated laws and offered rewards yet failed to transform customs and mores did so because their governance and punishments did not align with the Dao. In ancient times, the sage listened to the people’s music and knew their customs; observed their rituals and knew their mores; saw their mores and understood their transformation. Knowing the Dao’s unity, though he spoke not a word, all under heaven and the myriad people were transformed by him. This was because he sought only one thing: sincere adherence to the Dao—the supreme virtue of divine transformation.

Thus it is said: The sage has no fixed heart; he takes the people’s heart as his own. Nurturing the people as an infant, all under heaven will return to him.

The sage has no fixed heart.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient sage’s heart, night and day, never forgot benefiting the people. His virtue extended far, reaching the four seas.

Taking the people’s heart as his own.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: When the sage is above, the realm is governed; when below, the people emulate his intentions.

To the virtuous, I am virtuous.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient sage saw virtue as if he could not attain it himself; saw vice as if it were an omen. Teaching without descending from his hall, he rectified all under heaven. The people transformed and turned toward goodness—this was because he sought only his own virtue.

To the wicked, I am also virtuous.
Virtue is power.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient rulers saw gains as belonging to the people, losses as their own, and in retreat, blamed themselves.

To the trustworthy, I trust them.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Trust is the noble’s word; loyalty, the noble’s intent. When loyalty and trust manifest within, they resonate without—this is the transformation of the worthy and sage.

To the untrustworthy, I also trust them.
Virtue is trust.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient rulers saw righteousness as belonging to the people, perversity as their own, and in retreat, blamed themselves.

The sage in all under heaven.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The fundamental duty of governing is to secure the people.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: The ancient rulers saw one person lose their form and in retreat blamed themselves.

Diligent, for all under heaven.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient perfect ones refined their sincerity within, their virtue flowing to the four quarters. Seeing benefit for all under heaven, they rejoiced yet did not forget; seeing harm for all under heaven, they grieved as if in mourning.

Unified in heart.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Thus, to rejoice with all under heaven, to grieve with all under heaven—yet never failing to rule—is without precedent.

The people all focus their ears and eyes.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: He who grieves with the people’s grief, the people also grieve his grief; he who rejoices with the people’s joy, the people also rejoice his joy.

The sage treats them all as children.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Above views below as children, below views above as father; above views below as younger siblings, below views above as elder brothers. Viewing below as children—thus ruling all under heaven; viewing above as father—thus rectifying all under heaven; viewing below as younger siblings—thus not causing their deaths through hardship.

---

**Chapter Fifty: Birth and Death**

This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* states: Today, the seven kingdoms’ lords and ministers engage in birth-and-death struggles—knowing the suitability of lands, mastering the advantages of rugged terrain, discerning the shifts of oppressive governance, and probing military formations. Blades clash, arrows fly, the wounded are carried, blood flows for thousands of *li*, bones litter the wilds. Victory and defeat both bring ruin, and war becomes the realm of death—not the way of those who truly nurture life.

Truly, those ancient masters who skillfully governed their troops avoided the death-ground of arms—this was excellence. Now, the seven kingdoms’ arms have become that death-ground.

Birth and death.
The *Dongling Jing* says: There is one who, in war, can revive the dead. Used well, it brings fortune; used poorly, it brings calamity.

The living, thirteen in number.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: There are those who die unnaturally, not by fate. The few attack the many; the weak assault the strong—soldiers kill them.

The dead, thirteen in number.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Skilled warriors kill, each according to their desires, only to harm themselves in return. Killing ten thousand, they lose three thousand of their own.

Human life.
The *Dongling Jing* says: Human nature desires life and abhors death.

Driven to the death-ground.
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ *Upper Virtue Section*] says: War kills and wounds—thus it is called the death-ground.
Also thirteen in number.
The *Chongxu Jing* says: War is the death-ground, yet half who fight die in it.

Why is this so?
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□ *Nine Guards*] says: Why do the people of the seven kingdoms not complete their natural years? It is because of war’s slaughter.

Because of their thick accumulation of life.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Thus, because of their thick accumulation of life.

Truly, those who nurture life well.
The *Chongxu Jing* says: Even those who seek to nurture life do not rely on beasts—bears, tigers, leopards, and panthers serve as their banners, yet outwit them.

Traveling by land, they avoid rhinoceroses and tigers.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: To traverse water without fearing dragons is the fisherman’s courage; to travel land without fearing rhinoceroses and tigers is the hunter’s courage.

Entering battle, they do not don armor.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: When blades clash before them and they regard death as life, this is the warrior’s courage.

The rhinoceros has no place to thrust its horn.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: In the age of supreme virtue, beasts gathered in herds—thus they could be tethered and led without harming people.

The tiger has no place to strike with its claws.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: In the age of supreme virtue, men dwelled with beasts—thus they could climb and peer among them without danger.

Weapons find no place to wield their blades.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, deceit and evil did not arise, peace and ease prevailed, the world was harmonious, and the wise had no need to employ their strategies, nor did the brave need to wield their might. Why is this?
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, the strong did not oppress the weak, the many did not tyrannize the few, and people preserved their lives without dying young. Why? It was because there was no place for war and death.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, calamities and disasters did not arise, nor did misfortunes and chaos take shape—the world was at great peace, for there was no place for death.

**Chapter Fifty-One: "Dao Generates"**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* states:
Form does not arise without Dao; life does not flourish without virtue/power. Thus, Dao abides in nature, and virtue/power sustains life.
Dao and virtue/power—these are the great furnace of heaven and earth, the great alchemist of transformation, using yin and yang as fuel and the four seasons as timing to mold all things.
When spring’s breath unfurls, all grasses sprout;
When autumn arrives, all treasures mature.
Metal and stone cannot but resound—
They store Dao’s qi.
All things cannot but thrive—
This is their Way, is it not?
Carving and shaping myriad forms, yet Dao does not contrive artifice.

The **Heavenly Gate**—it does not exist.
All things emerge from non-existence,
All things have being in birth,
Yet none see their root—
They emerge, yet none see their gate;
They are born, they die, they emerge, they return—
Entering and exiting without revealing form—
This is called the **Heavenly Gate**.

Heaven does not give birth, yet all things are nurtured;
Earth does not grow, yet all things transform.
Heaven and Earth act without action,
Yet all things act without fail.
It is Dao that generates all things.

Dao generates them,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Dao is that which nurtures and sustains growth.
Without generation or growth, all things could not flourish.
To generate and sustain—
This is called the **Great Dao** generating all things.

Virtue/power nourishes them,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Virtue/power is that which nurtures and feeds.
Without nurturing or feeding, all things could not mature.
To nurture and feed—
This is called **Heaven’s virtue/power** sustaining all things.

Form shapes them,
The *Tongxuan Jing*: Heaven’s movement through the four seasons, rain and dew descending in due time—this generates all things.
Momentum completes them.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Earth’s depth bears all things, containing and responding to their uses—this nourishes all things.
Thus, all things do not fail to revere Dao and honor virtue/power.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: That which gives birth to things is Dao.
That which sustains their growth is virtue/power.
Dao’s preservation of life, virtue/power’s securing of form—
Dao and virtue/power are the parent and mother of all things.

Dao is revered, virtue/power is honored,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Dao is what the people revere.
Virtue/power is what the people honor.
None confer rank upon it,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: What the world reveres and ranks is Dao.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: Where Dao resides, the sage honors it—how dare any not revere it?
Yet it remains ever natural.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: The Way of Heaven and Earth—
None act upon it, yet it acts naturally.

Thus, Dao—
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Heaven’s Way is pattern, Earth’s Way is principle, yin and yang are the rhythms, the four seasons are the messengers. It is called Dao.
Sweet rains descend in season, the five grains flourish and multiply, and all things are born and completed.
To generate and nurture,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Spring generates all things.
All things are born at the spring equinox—
To generate and nurture.
To lengthen and cultivate,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Summer lengthens all things.
All things grow in full summer—
To lengthen and cultivate.
To complete and ripen,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Autumn harvests all things.
All things mature at the autumn equinox—
To complete and ripen.
To nourish and shelter,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Winter stores all things.
To gather and preserve without increasing wealth,
To distribute and receive without adding poverty—
To nourish and shelter.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: None are as divine as Heaven, none as abundant as Earth.
They nourish all things yet do not claim righteousness,
Their blessings extend to all ages yet they do not claim benevolence.
They give birth yet do not possess,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Great Constant Dao generates things yet does not possess what it generates. None see its nurturing, yet all things grow—
This is called the **mysterious, effortless Dao**.

The *Chongxu Jing* says: Thus, all things that have color have Dao coloring their colors.
All things colored by color reveal their hues,
Yet Dao colors all things’ colors without ever displaying its own hue—
This is the Dao’s role of **effortless action**.

It acts yet does not rely,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Great Constant Dao—
All things depend on it for their growth,
Yet none see what it grows—
This is called the **mysterious, effortless Dao**.
The *Chongxu Jing* says: Thus, all things that have sound have Dao sounding their sounds.
All things sounded by sound reveal their tones,
Yet Dao sounds all things’ sounds without ever emitting its own—
This is the Dao’s role of **effortless action**.

It lengthens yet does not rule,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Great Constant Dao transforms and completes without governing. None know its virtue/power—
This is called the **mysterious, effortless Dao**.
The *Chongxu Jing* says: Thus, all things that have taste have Dao tasting their tastes.
All things tasted by taste reveal their flavors,
Yet Dao tastes all things’ tastes without ever presenting its own—
This is the Dao’s role of **effortless action**.

This is called **mysterious virtue/power**.
The *Chongxu Jing* says: Dao can be yin, can be yang,
Can be soft, can be hard,
Can be short, can be long,
Can be round, can be square,
Can give birth, can die,
Can bring heat, can bring cold,
Can float, can sink,
Can be high-pitched, can be low,
Can emerge, can vanish,
Can be dark, can be yellow,
Can be sweet, can be bitter,
Can be rancid, can be fragrant.

The Dao is without knowing, without ability—
Dao is without action, yet all things are without not-knowing, without not-ability, without not-action.
This is called **mysterious virtue/power**, the role of **effortless action**.

**Chapter Fifty-Two: The Beginning of All Things**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* states:
Heaven has six poles and five phases,
Yet governs without action.
When emperors and kings follow without action, the realm is governed;
When they oppose without action, disaster ensues.

The affairs of the Nine Regions,
When governance is complete and virtue/power is full,
Overseeing the common folk,
The realm bears them—
This is called the **Supreme Sage**.

The ancient rulers of the world,
Though their wisdom rivaled Heaven and Earth,
Did not ponder for themselves;
Though their eloquence surpassed all things,
They did not boast for themselves;
Though their abilities exhausted the land,
They did not act for themselves.

Heaven does not produce yet all things transform,
Earth does not grow yet all things nurture,
The emperor and king act without action yet the realm prospers,
The Sage follows the Dao without action yet the realm is governed.

The world has a beginning,
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□Natural Section] says:
The world has a beginning,
Yet none know its principle—
Only the ancient Sage could know it.
Thus Heaven and Earth were completed,
Yin and Yang took form,
All things were born,
And the Dao named them—
Without words, without deeds,
This is **effortless action**.

This is the **Mother of the World**.
The *Tongxuan Jing* [□Sincere Section] says:
The Son of Heaven takes Heaven and Earth as his standard,
All things as his resources,
His virtue and power being most sublime,
His prestige and name most weighty,
The beauty of these two virtues/powers matches Heaven and Earth—
Thus he cannot but follow the Great Dao as the **Mother of the World**.

Once he has obtained its Mother,
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Heaven and Earth act without action.
Yet he does not fail to act—
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
Once he knows its children,
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The Great Dao acts without action yet without failing to act.
Returning again to guard its Mother,
The *Nanhua Jing* says:
The Sage above guards without action while things transform effortlessly.
His body perishes yet he is not harmed—
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The root of effortless action is to discard cleverness,
Close the four gates,
Cease the five passions,
Thus merging with the Dao.
Divine clarity hides in the formless,
Spirit merges with the True Dao,
His body perishes yet he is not harmed.

Seal its **joy**,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient Sage of old followed the Great Dao without action,
His mouth open yet not speaking,
His heart clear yet not pondering or scheming,
Straightforward in his natural emotions and wisdom—
Thus he could not be harmed.
So the essence remains in his mouth,
His speech is apt,
Gathers in his heart,
His thoughts are clear.
Moving the world without action,
He does not speak yet is public,
He does not scheme yet is wise.
Close its **gates**,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient Sage followed the Great Dao without action,
His ears keen yet not listening,
His eyes bright yet not seeing,
Forgotten yet not acting,
Wise yet not contriving—
Thus his essence remains in his ears,
His listening is keen,
Remains in his eyes,
His seeing is bright.
Moving the world without action,
He does not listen yet is keen,
He does not see yet is bright.
Thus his whole life is without toil—
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Thus closing the four gates,
His whole life is without calamity,
His limbs and nine orifices do not generate desire or disease—
This is called the **True Person**.

Open its **joy**,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Later generations, with partial enlightenment and contrived action,
Open their **joy**,
Speak with their mouths,
Scheme with their hearts,
Twisting eloquence to sway the world—
Their ears and eyes, though keen and bright,
Do not listen or see in accord with the Dao—
Thus their whole life is without salvation.

**Chapter Fifty-Three: The Sage’s Clarity**
This chapter of the *Dongling Jing* states:
From the recent past to now,
The world’s deceitful and perverse are many,
The upright and honest are few,
The frivolous and licentious are many,
The virtuous and retiring are few.

*(Note: "兑" [duì] was translated as "joy" in the previous section, but here it may also carry the connotations of "mouth" or "open expression" depending on context. For consistency, it is kept as "joy" in the first instance and "gates" in the second, where it aligns with the four gates [四关] mentioned earlier. "九洛" [Jiǔ Luò] and "九窍" [Jiǔ Qiào] are translated as "Nine Regions" and "nine orifices" respectively, following the previous context. "九洛" may also be interpreted as "Nine Passes" or "Nine Rivers," but "Nine Regions" is retained for parallelism with the previous section.)*

The deceitful and perverse in speech deceive the loyal and upright,
Thus causing the people of the world to mutually indulge in licentiousness and corruption.

In ancient times, Heaven acted naturally and Earth remained tranquil,
Ten thousand things refined and transformed of their own accord—
Ghosts and spirits lacked efficacy.

In the latter age, the Son of Heaven is strange and eccentric,
Great ministers are bizarre and perverse—
Then the arts of ghosts and spirits arise.

When the spirits are enraged, it is not because they are not worshipped.
