*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The ancient masters of men were like the many feet of a centipede,
each aiding without harming,
or like the tongue and teeth—
firm and yielding, grinding without destruction.

Though the summer jade may have flaws,
and the moon’s pearl may bear blemishes,
yet the world treasures them—
the worthy do not let minor faults obscure great beauty.
To fixate on others’ shortcomings,
forgetting their virtues,
and yet seek the worthy in the world—
this is difficult indeed.

This is called the virtue of non-contention—
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
Those who win the hearts of men must first win themselves;
those who win themselves must be soft and weak.
Those who achieve hegemony and kingship must first be conquerors;
those who conquer must be virtuous.

This is called the power of using men—
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
To love the worthy is benevolence;
to respect the worthy is ritual.

This is called aligning with Heaven—
the ancient ultimate.

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
A thousand-chariot state practicing cultural virtue becomes king;
a ten-thousand-chariot state fond of war perishes.

**Chapter Sixty-Nine: The Five Kinds of War**
This chapter from *The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
There are five kinds of war:
There is the righteous war,
there is the responsive war,
there is the wrathful war,
there is the greedy war,
and there is the arrogant war.

To punish tyranny and rescue the weak—this is the righteous war.
When an enemy attacks and there is no alternative—this is the responsive war.
To quarrel over trifles, unable to master one’s own heart—this is the wrathful war.
To covet another’s land, to desire another’s wealth—this is the greedy war.
To boast of one’s state’s grandeur and people’s multitude,
and to seek superiority over enemy states—this is the arrogant war.

The righteous war brings kingship,
the responsive war brings victory,
the wrathful war brings defeat,
the greedy war brings death,
the arrogant war brings annihilation—
this is Heaven’s Way.

Now, the warring states’ rebellious ministers and traitors
use war lightly against enemies,
ambush the unprepared,
and first invite disaster—
no calamity is greater than underestimating the enemy.

There is a saying about war:
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious [Upper Section on Rites]* says:
To use the unusual in war,
the unusual becomes weary as if labored,
the unusual becomes hungry as if starved,
the unusual becomes restless as if disturbed,
the unusual becomes chaotic as if disordered,
the unusual becomes forward as if retreating.
Where does one go without victory?
The latter prevails.

I dare not act as master.

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The one who strikes first is on the path to exhaustion.

Yet as a guest, *The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The one who moves last is the source of establishment.

I dare not advance even an inch,
but retreat a foot.

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
To bend an inch to extend a foot—this is the way of the virtuous.

This is called "action without action,"
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
Raising an army for others brings their aid;
raising an army for oneself brings their departure.

There is no arm to ward off,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
Where the multitude aids, though weak, it must grow strong.

There is no enemy to oppose,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
Where the multitude departs, though great, it must stand firm.

There is no weapon to grasp.
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The ancients held victory and triumph in not waging war while yet disbanding armies—when lords and vassals were in submission, this was the highest use of war.

No calamity is greater than underestimating the enemy,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The bold and courageous die by their reckless strength;
the eloquent and clever are constrained by their own cleverness.
One who knows victory through cleverness is capable;
one who does not know defeat through cleverness is not.

Brave in one act, discerning in one word,
one may be persuaded by subtle arguments,
but not yet trusted to broad application.

Lightly underestimating the enemy almost loses one’s treasure.

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious • Upper Virtue Section* says:
Laozi observed shadows to know the principle of yielding last—
the one who moves last never fails in propriety.

Hence the Sage says:
"Do not follow convention;
always yield last and never strike first.
Like stacking firewood,
the last is the highest.

Thus when armies clash,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
If one hears that a sovereign of an enemy state is oppressing his people with cruelty,
he should raise his army and approach its borders.
When the enemy is ready to engage and meet in battle,
the one who yields last will prevail.
The compassionate will prevail."

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The so-called "one who yields last"
is the Sage who guards the Dao of stillness,
restrains the feminine principle,
follows convention while responding to change,
always yields last and never strikes first—
his great achievements are without rigidity,
and he cannot be contested.
The one who yields last will prevail.

My words are found in *The Chapter of Easy Knowing*, the seventy-first chapter.
This chapter of *The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* states:
The商太宰 saw Confucius and said:
"Is Master Kong a Sage?"
Confucius replied:
"A Sage? How dare I claim such a title! Yet I am one who studies widely and knows much."

The商太宰 said:
"Are the Three Kings Sages?"
Confucius replied:
"The Three Kings were skilled in employing those of wisdom and courage—
whether they were Sages, I do not know."

The商太宰 said:
"Are the Five Emperors Sages?"
Confucius replied:
"The Five Emperors were skilled in employing those of virtue and righteousness—
whether they were Sages, I do not know."

The商太宰 said:
"Are the Three Sovereigns Sages?"
Confucius replied:
"The Three Sovereigns were skilled in adapting to the times—
whether they were Sages, I do not know."

The商太宰 was greatly startled and said:
"Then who is truly a Sage?"
Confucius, moved in expression, replied:
"In the Western Zhou, there was the Grand Historian Lao Dan, who may be called a Sage—
without governing yet without chaos,
without speaking yet self-assured,
without transforming yet self-directed,
he drifts among the people who cannot name him.
He may be called a great Sage indeed."

Hence the Old Sage said:
"My words speak of the Great Dao,
the transformation of non-action."

My words,
*The Southern Flower Canon* says:
"Lao Dan was clear about the virtue/power of Heaven and Earth,
their non-action, and their simplicity—
this is called the Great Ancestor, the Great Root,
the Great Master."

Easy to know,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
To govern a great state, the Dao cannot be slighted.
When the land is vast, the measures cannot be narrow.
When the king’s position is exalted, the affairs cannot be burdensome.
When the people are many, the teachings cannot be harsh.
Burdensome affairs are difficult to govern,
harsh laws are difficult to enforce,
scant resources are hard to sustain—
this is where cleverness may be of little use,
and subtle arguments cannot bring order.

Hence what is not beneficial to governance,
what is beneficial yet causes disorder—
the Sage does not do this.

What is not beneficial to war,
what is beneficial yet causes waste—
the wise do not employ this.

Thus, achievements are not weary of being frugal,
affairs are not weary of being simplified,
demands are not weary of being few.
Frugal achievements are easy to complete,
simplified affairs are easy to govern,
few demands are easy to sustain—
this is easy to practice.

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The River flows with winding ease, hence it can reach far;
The Mountain rises with gradual ascent, hence it can stand high;
The Dao proceeds with gentle leisure, hence it can transform.

He who is skilled in one technique,
proficient in one matter,
discerning in one ability—
may be persuaded by subtle arguments,
but cannot yet be broadly applied to governance.

The words of the Dao say:
Vague and indistinct,
it harmonizes with Heaven’s qi.
He who harmonizes with qi becomes an Emperor,
he who harmonizes with virtue becomes a King,
he who harmonizes with power becomes a Conqueror,
he who lacks any of these will perish.

Thus, without words yet governance is achieved,
without affairs yet transformation is complete—
this is why the heart of Heaven moves and transforms through non-action.

Hence, with the Dao to govern,
few affairs suffice for governance;
without the Dao to govern,
many affairs suffice only to bring disorder.

In the end times,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
The people of the world are overly constrained by petty pleasures
and obstructed by the Great Dao,
unable to know or practice it.
None can know,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
In the end times, petty learning obscures virtue,
and none can know it.

*The Southern Flower Canon* says:
In ancient times, virtue was not slighted by petty knowledge;
in later times, petty knowledge injured virtue,
and none could know it.

None can practice.
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
In the end times, petty goodness blocks the Dao,
and none can practice it.

*The Southern Flower Canon* says:
In ancient times, the Dao was not slighted by petty practice;
in later times, petty practice injured the Dao,
and none could practice it.

Words have a root,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* [• *Clarifying the Mysterious Section*] says:
Words have a root,
affairs have a master—
the highest words transcend words.
Affairs have a master,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* [• *Sincere Integrity Section*] says:
Words have a root,
affairs have a foundation—
the highest practice transcends practice.

He who lacks knowledge alone,
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
In the end times, petty learning—
those who vie for fish are drenched,
those who chase beasts are exhausted,
the shallow concerns of the vulgar—
these are the ends of things.
He who lacks knowledge alone."

Thus, none truly know me.
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
In the end times, petty Dao is abandoned,
its root and foundation lost.
Though skills may be many,
they are shallow, muddled, and dry—
better to speak little.
To harm the multitude by burdening them with tasks
and then sever their fingers to demonstrate
that great skill cannot be forced—
thus, none truly know me.

Few know me,
*The Scripture of the Void and Emptiness* says:
In the Western Zhou, the Grand Historian Lao Dan was a sage—
he ruled without governing, spoke without persuading,
transformed without teaching,
and drifted effortlessly, beyond the people’s naming.
Truly, he was a great sage.

Those who follow me are honored.
*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
In silent repose, in profound stillness,
governing through non-action,
unaware of doing yet deeds accomplished—
this is the sage.
What is honored in the Dao is its dragon-like transformation,
for divine transformation alone is noble.

Thus, the sage,
*The Scripture of the South* says:
Does not stray from the root—
this is the Heavenly Human.
Taking Heaven as root,
virtue as foundation,
the Dao as gate,
manifesting in transformation—
this is the sage.
Clad in coarse robes yet holding jade.

*The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysterious* says:
Jade in the mountains makes grasses flourish,
pearls in the depths keep shores from withering,
the Dao within brings form to self-regulation.

**Chapter 71: "Knowing the Unknown"**
This chapter of *The True Scripture of the South* speaks:
The Dao does not desire mixture.
Scholars of many schools abound;
many, they disturb;
disturbed, they are disordered;
disorder brings sorrow and calamity.
Sorrowed, they cannot be saved.

Their books teach the virtuous:
to level instruction, to rectify lawsuits.
The worthy govern, the capable serve—
benevolence spreads below, and the people cherish virtue.
But when it decays,
cliques form, each promoting their own,
abandoning the public for private gain,
inner and outer factions rise,
the deceitful take office, the worthy hide away.

In ancient times, the Dao and virtue were obscured
in the books of the Hundred Schools.
The Confucians—Hui Shi and Du Shi—
their writings filled five chariots,
their teachings contradictory and tangled,
their words chaotic and useless.
The Mohists—Mo Di and Jin Hui—
did not align with the ancient kings,
abolishing the rites and music of old,
Mo Zi extolling Yu’s Way,
forcing his followers into laborious toil.

The Logicians—Sun Pin and Yin Wen—
crafted the *Hua Mountain Cap* to display themselves,
claiming it benefited nothing in the world.
The Legalists—Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao—
their teachings did not suit the living,
but only the logic of the dead.
The Sophists—Gongsun Long and his disciples—
could persuade with words but not with truth,
all teaching cleverness and deceit,
indulging in perverse and licentious ways,
then abandoning them to law, following with punishment.
Though they ravaged the world, they could not curb their treachery.

Skill alone is perilous,
and when lost, it multiplies.
To love knowledge and exhaust technique—
to govern with knowledge is to ruin the state.

Mountains yield gold, stones yield jade,
yet they erode each other;
trees breed worms, which devour them;
humans study, yet only bring chaos.
The more the ruler studies, the more the people labor;
the more the ruler acts, the more the people are troubled.

Thus, to stop the boiling with firewood only makes the boiling worse—
one who knows the principle of *wuwei* governance knows to remove the fire alone.
*Wuwei* naturally aligns itself.

Now, the teachings of the various schools—perverse doctrines and deceitful words—
are the very obstructions that bring chaos to the world.

To know what one does not know—
the *Tongxuan Jing*[*Symptoms of the Mysterious*] says:
*"The ancient sages who skillfully employed the Dao
let *wuwei* transform itself naturally,
and thus never caused harm.
Therefore it is said: To know what one does not know—*shàng* [superior]."*

To not know what one knows—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Now, those who do not know the Dao
actively interfere, bringing self-ruin.
Fortune becomes misfortune.
Therefore it is said: To not know what one knows—*disease*."*

Only the diseased who recognize their disease—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The common people and lesser scholars all indulge in book-learning for profit,
profiting from learning while unaware that their disease is their disease."*

Thus, they are not truly diseased—
the *Nanhua Jing*[*Southern Flower Sutra*] says:
*"Nan Rong Zhi said: When a villager falls ill,
and others ask about it, the sick person can name their ailment—
how could they not be ill?
The Sage is not ill,
yet those who recognize their disease—
even they are not truly ill.
For by recognizing their disease,
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
as if Zhi had drunk medicine to worsen his illness—
they can remove the disease of being diseased,
and thus are not ill.
Therefore, they are not ill."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Only the ancient sages knew that the disease of disease is no disease.
The people do not fear coercive laws—
this chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
The ancient sages governed with the Dao,
maintaining softness without force,
allowing virtue to transform naturally,
and thus achieved their kingship.
Human nature responds to virtue, not to force.
Virtue comes from within—
self-love enables nobility,
and love for the people brings kingship,
so all under heaven return.
When the people do not fear coercion,
true awe naturally arises.
The *Chongxu Jing*[*Sutra of the Void*] says:
Yet to desire others to revere you—
this is impossible to attain through the Dao.
If others do not revere you,
how can danger or humiliation reach you?

Do not narrow your dwelling—
the *Nanhua Jing* says:
The enclosures of the Weishi clan,
the hunting grounds of the Yellow Emperor,
the palaces of Tang and Yu,
the halls of Tang and Wu—
as virtue declines, their dwellings grow ever narrower.

Do not exhaust what sustains you—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"To govern a great state, the Dao must not be constrained.
The broader the land, the broader the governance must be.
An emperor who cannot win the people cannot achieve the world;
one who wins the people but loses the Dao cannot retain it.
Only the unbounded remains unbounded."
The *Chongxu Jing* says:
*"Laws and measures are within oneself;
examination lies with the people.
If the people love me, I must love them in return;
if the people hate me, I must hate them in return.
Thus, the Sage—
the *Chongxu Jing* says:
Tang and Wu loved the world, so they ruled;
Jie and Zhou hated the world, so they perished.

Self-knowledge—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"The foundation of winning the people lies in self-knowledge.
When one blames others for their faults,
does one truly know oneself?
Not seeing oneself—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
Thus, the Sage does not force,
and so achieves kingship.
Self-love—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"No virtue surpasses loving others;
to love others is to have no grievance with punishment."
Not esteeming oneself—
the *Tongxuan Jing* says:
*"Thus, the Sage loves themselves,
and so achieves nobility.
Therefore, abandon that and take this.
Heaven’s calamities do not arise—
those who attain this are blessed.
Forgotten is that,
and guilt departs while fortune arrives."*

「**Chapter 73: The Bold in Daring Bring Death**
This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* speaks of: Seven states waging war, slaying the innocent, nourishing the unrighteous, and causing harm beyond measure. Indulging one’s private desires while ignoring the calamities within the realm—this is what Heaven detests, and what the people resent, and what Heaven punishes. Thus, the bold in daring bring death; what Heaven abhors, who knows its reason?

The Heavenly Net is vast and far-reaching, yet it does not fail to ensnare. The bold in daring bring death, but the *Tongxuan Jing˙Dao De* says: In the latter days of the Warring States, men have hearts of profit and loss, and from the outset, they harbor murderous intent. Thus, the bold in daring bring death, while the bold in restraint bring life.

The *Chongxu Jing* says: Those who practice the Dao make others so that though they be bold enough to strike, the strike will not take hold; though they be clever enough to attack, the attack will not find its mark. Though they strike and do not take hold, though they attack and do not find their mark, yet they still bring shame—how much less so than the ancient Sages, who made others so that though they be bold, they dare not strike, and though they be clever, they dare not attack! Those who dare not strike are not without murderous intent, but how much less so than the Sages, who from the outset have no murderous intent at all! Those who have no murderous intent at all have not yet cultivated a heart of love and benefit. Thus, they make all men and women under Heaven—without exception—rejoice and willingly embrace love and benefit, yet they dare not kill. If such is the case, without murderous intent, there is no place where they are not rulers, no office where they are not leaders. All under Heaven willingly embrace peace and benefit, and so the bold in restraint bring life.

Understanding these two principles—one brings benefit, the other harm. The *Tongxuan Jing* says: To love daring is the Dao of peril and ruin; the bold and daring die by force and tyranny. What Heaven abhors, who knows its reason? The *Chongxu Jing* says: Laozi spoke to Guan Yin: *"What Heaven abhors, who knows its reason?"* Therefore, even the Sage finds it difficult.

Under Heaven, nothing is easier than doing good; what is meant by doing good is to remain still and take no action, and so doing good is easy. Under Heaven, nothing is harder than doing evil; what is meant by doing evil is usurpation and regicide, deceit and falsehood, which go against human nature, and so doing evil is hard. The Dao of Heaven, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: Heaven’s Dao diminishes the full and benefits the few; the Dao of ghosts and spirits is proud and overflowing, not to be shared.

Without contention yet achieving victory, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: Those with hidden virtue must have manifest reward; those with secret deeds must have clear response. The sower of millet does not reap grain; the planter of evil does not receive virtue in return. Without speech yet responding well, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: To do evil in the light of day, men may punish you; to do evil in darkness, ghosts may punish you.

Without summoning yet they come, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven is the sun of winter and the shade of summer; all things return to it, yet none compel them. It reaches the utmost of natural and refined influence—coming without summoning, departing without being driven away. Thus, it is effortless yet good at planning.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Heaven’s Dao is broad and open; to abandon the self is not far. If one turns toward good, even if they err, there will be no resentment. If one does not turn toward good, even if they are loyal, they will bring misfortune. The Heavenly Net is vast and far-reaching, and the *Dongling Jing* says: *"If the prohibitive nets are sparse and wide, they are easy to evade yet hard to violate."* The Heavenly Net is vast and far-reaching, sparse yet never failing. Sparse yet never failing. The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In the Warring States, those who dare to fight kill the innocent; the people resent them, and Heaven punishes them.

**Chapter 74: The People Do Not Normally Fear Death**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* speaks of: The Five Hegemons suddenly speaking of punishments and rewards, yet how can they govern the people? This is merely knowing the tools of governance, not the essential Dao of governance. One may use the tools of governance throughout the realm, but this is not enough to apply the Dao of governance throughout the realm.

The laws of the latter days set high standards yet fail to reach the guilty; impose heavy burdens yet punishments do not overcome them; create great difficulties yet executions are not dared. The people are burdened by these three evils, adorned with cleverness and deceiving their superiors, committing wickedness and courting danger. Even with harsh laws and severe punishments, they cannot suppress their deceit.

Wild beasts when cornered will strike; birds when cornered will peck; men when cornered will deceive—this is what is meant. Thus, the Five Hegemons’ harsh laws and severe punishments make the people crafty, and the rulers demand much while the people resent much. The more they demand, the less they receive; the more they prohibit, the fewer they overcome.

And so, to stop the boiling by adding more fire—though it seems a strange remedy—the *Tongxuan Jing* says: The essential principle of governance is to remove the fire itself. To be clear and still, to return to rectitude.

Thus, the Five Hegemons cruelly punish, taking the guilty into their own hands and killing them. Those who substitute for the officials in killing are rare indeed who do not soil their hands. The people do not normally fear death, and the *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Five Hegemons’ harsh laws and severe punishments are not enough to instill awe; killing the innocent is not enough to suppress deceit. How can you rely on death to instill fear?

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Those who carve laws and impose executions are not the work of an Emperor or King; those who frequently use rods and whips are not the measures of one who seeks the distant. If you wish to make the people fear death, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Five Hegemons’ harsh laws and severe punishments make the people crafty; the more the rulers demand, the more the people contend below. The more they demand, the less they receive; the more they prohibit, the fewer they overcome.

And yet, there are those who are strange, the *Tongxuan Jing□Xia De* says: The people of the latter days adorn themselves with cleverness and deceive their superiors, commit wickedness and court danger. Even with harsh laws and severe punishments, they cannot suppress their deceit. Wild beasts when cornered will strike; birds when cornered will peck; men when cornered will deceive—this is what is meant.

I seize them and kill them, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: Ancient rulers who governed well accumulated their virtue; accumulating virtue, the people could be transformed. Today’s rulers who govern by cruelty and violence breed chaos, and chaos is the wind of ruined states. Who dares?」

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The laws of the ancient kings were not their own creation.
With laws broad and punishments lenient, the prisons stand empty, and the realm unified in custom—none harbor deceitful hearts. This is the benevolence of the Sage.

Let those who hold office kill when they must. The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, officials were appointed to restrain the people, lest they act without restraint. The establishment of rulers was to control these officials, lest they act unilaterally. Laws, measures, and Daoist arts were to restrain the ruler, lest he act arbitrarily.

To take the place of the executioner and kill—this is called usurping the Master Carpenter’s axe.

The *Tongxuan Jing* [in the *Shangren* section] says: A whale cast upon dry land is dominated by an ant. When a ruler abandons his proper role and contests matters with his ministers, he is dominated by his officials. Ministers hoard their wisdom and do not employ it, yet instead usurp authority from above.

If a ruler does not entrust capable men but insists on acting himself, his wisdom grows ever more constrained while his burdens increase. When cornered by his subordinates, he cannot rectify matters. His wisdom proves insufficient for governance, his authority inadequate for enforcing law—thus he cannot engage with the realm.

To take the place of the executioner and kill—this is called usurping the Master Carpenter’s axe. And he who usurps the Master Carpenter’s axe will rarely escape unscathed.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: When a ruler’s joys and angers manifest in his heart, and his desires are visible without, those who guard their posts abandon principle to curry favor, and officials pervert the law to follow the wind. Rewards fail to match merit, punishments fail to fit crimes—thus superior and subordinate grow estranged, ruler and minister harbor grudges, officials grow chaotic, and wisdom cannot resolve slander while clarity cannot illuminate one’s own faults. Instead, they blame themselves, and so the sovereign grows ever more weary while his ministers grow ever more insolent.

Thus, he who usurps the Master Carpenter’s axe will rarely escape unscathed.

**On the People’s Hunger (Chapter 75)**
This chapter of the *Nanhua Jing* states: When learning is prized for cleverness but lacks the Dao, the world falls into chaos. How do we know this is so?

When the cunning of bows, crossbows, snares, and mechanical traps abounds, the birds grow disordered above. When the cunning of hooks, baits, nets, and weirs abounds, the fish grow disordered in the waters. When the cunning of traps, snares, and pitfalls abounds, the beasts grow disordered in the marshes. When the cunning of deceit, poison, sophistry, and debates over *jiehua* (contradictions), *jianhua* (subtleties), *firmness*, *whiteness*, *freshness*, *filth*, and distinctions abounds, then customs grow disordered in argumentation.

From ancient times to today, tyrants like Jie and Zhou have repeatedly plunged the world into great chaos, and the fault lies in their love of cleverness. Now, the people of Zhou are skilled yet difficult to govern, and so this chapter is composed.

**On the People’s Hunger**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: When the people show signs of hunger, it is not by fate that they perish young or die of starvation. It is because of the heavy taxes imposed upon them.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Moreover, in sustaining life, one man plows and does not increase ten *mu* of land. Even in a middle-quality field, the harvest does not exceed four *dan*. His wife, children, and aged dependents rely on this for sustenance. At times, there are calamities of flood or drought. When tribute is demanded, the ruler may pity them—but still, they hunger.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In the latter days, greedy tyrants drain their people like fish to satisfy their boundless desires, and so the common folk do not receive the harmonies of Heaven or the virtues of Earth. Thus, the people hunger.

**On the Difficulty of Governing the People**
The *Nanhua Jing* says: The people are difficult to govern because their cleverness is excessive.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In later ages, when rulers act, they exhaust the world’s resources yet fail to govern. When superiors desire much, inferiors suffer much. When superiors engage in many affairs, inferiors adopt many guises. To teach the people and then expect them to cease their ways is like fanning flames and hoping they will not burn—it is difficult.

Thus, to stop boiling by adding more water only makes it worse. Those who know the root of governance know to remove the fire. Hence, they are difficult to govern.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: When superiors act, inferiors also act.

**On the Corruption of Learning and the Lightness of Death**

Scholarly learning opens the door to deceit among the people, yet when their licentious and perverse ways are unleashed, they are abandoned to the law and pursued by punishment—though this only ravages the world. Unable to curb their deceit, they are thus difficult to govern.

**On the Lightness of Death**
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: When the people grow light in death, they perish not by fate but by their own indulgence, ever seeking without restraint, ever desiring without end—until punishment kills them.

Because their craving for life is excessive,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In the latter ages, dim-witted rulers take from the people without measure, demand of them without limit, so that men and women cannot tend to farming and weaving. Their days are numbered, yet they labor endlessly to serve their superiors, their strength exhausted, their wealth depleted—thus they grow light in death.

Thus, the people grow light in death.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: When superiors indulge in boundless taking, inferiors become avaricious without restraint. The people, impoverished and suffering, give rise to strife—thus they grow light in death.

Those who do not value life above all,
The *Nanhua Jing* says: In ancient times, rulers prized life, and prizing life, they held gain in light regard. In later ages, when wealth is scarce, theft arises—yet whom can one blame for this? Blame the rulers who value gain over life.

This is wiser than valuing life.
The *Tongxuan Jing* [Nine Guardians Chapter] says: Only those who do not value life above all can attain true longevity.

**On the People’s Lifespan (Chapter Seventy-Six)**
This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* speaks: In the Warring States, warfare drained the world’s resources to satisfy one man’s desires—what greater calamity could there be? To indulge one man’s desires is to prolong the suffering of the land, a transgression against Heaven’s order, a grievance of the people, a sin inviting Heaven’s retribution. Thus, the strong in arms perish, for the overmighty are doomed to destruction—how could they not perish?

The people’s birth is gentle and weak,
The *Nanhua Jing* says: Human life is the gathering of harmonious *qi*.
Their death is rigid and strong.
The *Nanhua Jing* says: Human death is the scattering of *qi*—when *qi* scatters, death comes.

All things—grasses and trees—are gentle and tender in life,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In their birth, plants flourish in bloom.
But rigid and withered in death.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In their death, plants wither and decay.

Thus, the rigid and strong are doomed to death,
The *Tongxuan Jing* [Dao Yuan Chapter] says: The rigid and strong are doomed to death.
The gentle and weak are destined to life.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The gentle and weak are destined to life.

Thus, the strong in arms cannot prevail,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Those who seek rigidity must guard with gentleness;
Those who seek strength must guard with weakness.
Accumulated gentleness becomes rigidity,
Accumulated weakness becomes strength.
Observe what they accumulate to know their fate—
Thus, the strong in arms perish.

The rigid tree snaps,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The rigid tree breaks, the rigid hide splits.
The mighty fall from their height,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says:

**Chapter on Supreme Virtue (上德篇)**

The *Laozi* looked upward at the trees in the eaves, then gazed at the rivers below. The strong and rigid trees dwell in the lower eaves, the great in stature occupy the lower branches. The gentle and weak rise above.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The small and slender trees dwell in the higher eaves, the weak and slight occupy the upper branches—like stacking firewood, the smallest is placed on top.

**Chapter 77: The Dao of Heaven**
The *Tongxuan Jing* speaks: In ancient times, enlightened rulers governed with moderation, nurtured themselves with restraint, and calculated harvests according to the seasons. They measured the people’s stored provisions, knowing the surplus and deficit, then took only what was needed. Thus, they received what Heaven and Earth bestowed and avoided the suffering of hunger and cold.

When the people were distressed—those who starved ate food without relish, those who froze endured winter without furs—rulers shared in their hardships. Thus, there were no grieving subjects under Heaven.

In later ages, who could use their surplus to nourish the world? Only the ancient Dao-bearing rulers could sustain the widows, orphans, and the lonely, following Heaven’s way to aid the people.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven diminishes the full and benefits the scarce. Is it not like drawing a bow?

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: A bow is drawn for shooting, yet without a string, it cannot release. Heaven’s shooting requires no bow—it strikes true.

The high are humbled,
The *Tongxuan Jing* [Guard Weakness Section] says: The Dao of Heaven humbles the high.
The low are raised;
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven raises the low.
The excessive are diminished,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven diminishes the excessive.
The deficient are supplemented.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven supplements the deficient.

The Dao of Heaven diminishes the excessive and supplements the deficient,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven diminishes the excessive to nourish the deficient.
The Dao of Earth diminishes the high and benefits the low.
But the Dao of humanity is otherwise,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of the ruler is otherwise, differing from Heaven’s diminishing and benefiting.

It diminishes the deficient,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Yet the people, in their tilling, rely on their wives, children, aged, and weak for sustenance. Facing floods, droughts, and heavy taxes, they struggle to provide.
To nourish the excessive.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ruler takes from the people to serve above, seeking only the surplus.

Who can use their surplus to nourish the world?
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In the later ages, greedy tyrants drain their people to satisfy boundless desires, leaving the masses hungry and cold. Who can nourish the deficient?

Only the Dao-bearing ones.
The *Zhuangzi* says: The ancient sages took care of the old, weak, orphans, and widows, ensuring all were provided for.
Thus, the sages
The *Zhuangzi* says: The ancients were complete—they nurtured all things, and their benevolence extended to the people.

They act without claiming merit,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven and Earth—all things rely on it for existence, yet none know its virtue.
Their work accomplished, they take no credit,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of Heaven and Earth—all things complete their work without dwelling on it, yet none know its virtue.
They do not seek to be seen as virtuous.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The sage is empty of self, seeing only insufficiency. Seeing insufficiency, they achieve true virtue.

**Chapter 78: The Gentle and Weak of the World**
The *Tongxuan Jing* speaks: The sea does not reject the smallest streams to become great; the forests do not reject the crooked and bent to flourish. The sage does not reject humble words to broaden their name. If speech is true, even a common laborer’s words should not be discarded. If speech is false, even the ruler’s ministers should not be heeded. Right and wrong cannot be judged by rank or status.

When the people drown and the ruler does not grieve, he is not a virtuous sovereign. To guard one’s post and die in battle is the duty of a subject. In later ages, few ministers offer sincere loyalty, risking their lives. Those who exhaust their strength and loyalty go unheard.

Thus, the ancient sages modeled themselves after water’s gentleness—enduring shame, accepting criticism, and receiving insults. Rulers should emulate water in receiving defilement.

Nothing in the world is as gentle and weak as water,
The *Tongxuan Jing* [Dao Yuan Section] says: Nothing is as gentle and weak as water.
Yet it conquers the rigid and strong,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of water—it pierces metal and stone, its strength flows through the world.
And none can overcome it,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of water—its course cannot be exhausted, its subtlety cannot be grasped.
Nothing can replace it.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The Dao of water—struck, it leaves no wound; pierced, it suffers no harm; cut, it is not severed; burned, it is not scorched. It flows smoothly, never dispersing.

Thus, the gentle overcomes the rigid,
The *Tongxuan Jing* [Supreme Virtue Section] says: The *Laozi* saw the tongue and guarded gentleness. Though teeth are firmer than the tongue, they decay first.
The weak overcomes the strong.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Accumulated gentleness becomes rigidity; accumulated weakness becomes strength. Without accumulation, this cannot be.

The world
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In the later ages, human nature favors strength and rejects weakness, rigidity and rejects gentleness, yet they do not know their loss and cannot embrace gentleness.

None know,
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The virtuous discern truth and do not trust slander.
None can practice it.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: If counsel is ignored, the ruler is unenlightened.

Thus, the sage
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: The ancient wise rulers modeled themselves after water’s gentleness.

Gentleness and frailty, though subtle and seemingly insignificant, thus attain their lofty heights—virtue and renown arise naturally. It says: *"To bear the filth of the state is to be called the sovereign of the ancestral altars;"* the *Tongxuan Jing* states: *"The ancient sages did not seek deeds that might be criticized, nor did they resent others’ censure of themselves. Though the world condemns them, they do not falter."*

*"To bear the state’s calamities is to be called the king of the world."* The *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"In ancient times, the sages allowed those who slandered them to do so, for it was their own fault."* Words once spoken cannot be recalled by others; actions begun nearby cannot be restrained from afar. Straight speech seems like perversion. The *Tongxuan Jing˙Upper Virtue Chapter* declares: *"In later ages, those who speak are silenced by words, those who advise are executed by law—yet such rulers still hope to preserve harmony across all lands. How far they are from wisdom!"*

**Chapter on Reconciling Great Grievances (79)**
This chapter of the *Tongxuan Jing* states: *"To reconcile great grievances will inevitably leave lingering resentment—how can this be called good?"* In ancient times, the near were drawn close not by words, the distant not by words; the near were made content, the distant were made to come. To share desires with the people brings harmony; to share their defenses brings stability; to share their thoughts brings understanding; to gain their strength brings wealth; to gain their praise brings renown. In the later ages, actions invite bandits, words invite disaster—no one speaks first, yet others have already conspired behind them. Rumors spreading a thousand miles are a calamity. The tongue is a trigger: once words are spoken, they cannot be recalled, even by four swift horses.

This exhorts the Warring States to reconcile their grievances, to cease hostilities and not contend, thus achieving universal peace—now that is good. To reconcile great grievances, the *Tongxuan Jing˙Subtle Brilliance Chapter* says: *"The ancient sages reconciled the world’s grievances, their virtue acting as the binding contract—non-contention was their goodness."* There must be lingering resentment—how can this be called good? The *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"The Warring States lacked the Dao; their conflicts bred resentment—there must be lingering resentment. How can this be called not good?"* Thus, the sage—

the *Zhuangzi* states: *"The ancient sages were fully prepared: to harmonize the world."* Holding the left contract, the *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"Yin harms things; yang transforms them."* The sage’s Dao follows the brilliance of yang to harmonize the world, follows the darkness of yin to reconcile its grievances and ill-will, yet holds no blame toward others. The *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"The greater the resentment, the deeper the calamity."* To harbor resentment without resolution is unheard of. Hence, virtue acts as the binding contract—the *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"Accumulated virtue becomes kingship; accumulated stone becomes mountains."* Without accumulation, this cannot be. Without virtue, there is no binding contract—the *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"Accumulated resentment brings ruin; accumulated water becomes rivers."* Without accumulation, this cannot be.

The Dao of Heaven favors none, always aiding the virtuous. The *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"The Dao of Heaven favors none, only virtue is rewarded."*

**Chapter on Small States and Few People (80)**
This chapter of the *Zhuangzi* (in the *Inner Chamber of the Chest* section) states: *"In the ancient age of supreme virtue, the clans of Róngchéng, Dàtíng, Bóhuáng, Zhōngyāng, Lìlù, Líxù, Hèxiū, Zūnlú, Zhùróng, Fúxī, Shénnóng, and Xuānyuán ruled. At that time, the people tied knots in cords for use, relished their food, adorned their dress, found comfort in their dwellings, and delighted in their customs. Neighboring states could see one another, and the sounds of roosters and dogs mingled in the air—yet the people lived until old age and death without visiting one another."* Such a time, when knots were tied in simplicity and the people were unadorned, universal peace meant supreme governance.

Small states, few people—the *Tongxuan Jing˙Symbolic Speech Chapter* says: *"To command ten thousand soldiers is not as good as to hear a single apt word; to possess the pearl of Duke Hòu is not as good as to grasp the proper means of governance; to hold the jade of Héshì is not as good as to align with the fitting course of affairs."* Though the world be vast, those who delight in war perish; though the state be stable, those who love contention are in peril. Thus, small states with few people, though they possess myriad implements, do not use them.

Though they possess myriad implements, they do not use them—the *Dònglíng Jing* says: *"The ancient sage-kings flourished by encouraging agriculture and teaching the people to till the land, to make death weighty and not wander far."* The *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"When Fúxī ruled the world, his virtue gave life without taking it."* At that time, there was no heart of profit or harm; the world was at peace. Though there were boats and carriages, there was no need to ride them—the *Zhuangzi* says: *"In the age of Shénnóng, when resting he slept soundly, when moving he walked leisurely, the people knew their mothers but not their fathers, and lived with deer and elk without malice. This was the peak of supreme virtue."*

Though there were armor and weapons, they were not displayed—the *Tongxuan Jing* says: *"Huángdì’s governance of the world refined the essence of sun and moon, regulated the qi of yin and yang, balanced the rhythms of the four seasons, established the laws of music and the calendar, distinguished male and female, clarified high and low. Farmers yielded the edges of their fields, and travelers did not pick up lost goods."* The people once again tied knots in cords for use—the *Zhuangzi* says: *"In the upper antiquity of supreme virtue, there was no action—yet the world was governed with ease, and both ruler and ruled were without strife."* The ruler did not act; the people did not act. They relished their food—the *Zhuangzi* says: *"The ancient people gathered acorns and chestnuts by day, and found their food sweet—thus they were called the people who knew life."* They adorned their dress—the *Zhuangzi* says: *"The ancient people did not know clothing, yet in summer they piled firewood, and in winter they burned it—thus their dress was beautiful, and they were called the people who knew life."*

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, their clothing was warm yet undecorated. They settled comfortably in their dwellings—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: The ancient people lived in nests and caves, resting in trees at dusk, finding comfort in their homes, thus they were called the people of the *Youchao Clan*.

The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times of great peace, neither soldiers nor horses crossed the borders of neighboring states, nor did armored chariot tracks extend beyond a thousand miles—all found comfort in their dwellings. They delighted in their customs—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: The ancient people delighted in their customs.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, deceit did not arise; they lived in ease without strife, the world at harmony, their customs joyful.

Neighboring states lay in sight—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: In ancient times, neighboring states lay in sight, yet mountains had no paths or tunnels, marshes no bridges or ferries.
The sounds of roosters and dogs reached one another—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: In ancient times, the sounds of roosters and dogs reached one another, all creatures thrived in groups, bound to their lands.
The people lived until old age—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: In ancient times, the people lived until old age.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, the strong did not oppress the weak, the many did not harm the few, so the people preserved their lives and did not die young.

They did not interact with one another—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: The ancient people lived alongside deer, hunted for food, and knew how to clothe themselves, with no intent to harm one another. They did not interact—this was the height of virtue.

*"True words are not ornate"*—Chapter Eighty-One of *Nanhua Zhenjing* speaks of the ancient Dao/Way and its techniques, which lay in *wuwei* (non-action). Laozi heard its teachings and rejoiced. He established it with the constant *wu* (non-being), governed it with *Taiyi* (Supreme Oneness), taking gentleness, humility, and yielding as its outward form, and emptiness, non-destruction of all things as its substance.

Laozi said: *"While others take what comes first, I take what comes last—thus I bear the world’s impurities. While others take what is solid, I take what is empty—thus I have no hoarding, and so I have abundance. While others seek fortune, I preserve myself—thus I avoid blame. I root myself in depth, bind myself with restraint, saying: ‘The rigid is destroyed, the sharp is blunted.’ In non-action, I laugh at cleverness, always yielding to things, never harming others—this is the utmost extreme."*

Laozi—truly the ancient sage of vast truth!

*"True words are not ornate,"*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, the Great Dao was loyal yet simple, and the people had no deceit.
*"Ornate words are not true."*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Later generations knew contracts yet trust declined; they knew machinery yet substance declined. Minor cleverness breaks the Dao, harshness injures virtue.

*"The good do not argue,"*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times, the Dao was upright, non-action was not perilous, so the people easily followed.
The *Dongling Jing* says: Knowing without arguing is called the Dao; the Dao brings peace to the people.
*"Those who argue are not good."*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Later generations knew writing yet the Dao declined; minor arguments harmed the upright.
The *Dongling Jing* says: Knowing and then arguing is called discernment, used to govern people.
*The Nanhua Jing* says: Yet argument is never beneficial.

*"The wise are not all-knowing,"*
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: In ancient times of perfect governance, there was no labor, all were at ease, so the common people were not harmed.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: Later generations knew calculations yet benevolence declined; minor achievements harmed governance.
*The Nanhua Jing* says: Yet knowledge without breadth is not wisdom.

*"The sage does not accumulate,"*
*The Nanhua Jing* says: The sage’s Dao flows without hoarding, thus all under heaven submitted.
Clear in Heaven, harmonious with the sage, with six penetrations and non-action, four openings in emptiness and stillness—those who embody the virtue of the sovereign—things act of themselves. In darkness, there is nothing that is not still.

*"To accumulate is insufficient; to hoard is to lack."*
The sage is cautious in what is accumulated.
Having given to others—
*The Nanhua Jing* says: Having given to others, the self becomes ever more abundant.
The *Tongxuan Jing* says: If kings and nobles uphold the Yang Dao, all things flourish; if they uphold the Yin Dao, the world perishes. If Yang does not descend to Yin, all things cannot form; if the ruler does not descend to the subject, virtue cannot spread.

*"The self becomes ever more abundant,"*
The *Chongxu Jing* says: Yao governed the world, wandering in broad streets, hearing children’s songs:
*"Establish our boiling people,
None but you are supreme."*
Not knowing, not knowing—
Following the Emperor’s way.
Having given to others...

*The Nanhua Jing* says: Having given to others, the self grows ever more abundant.

*The Tongxuan Jing* says: Kings and nobles dwell above the people, illuminating virtue and Dao. The noble withdraws from vice and embraces goodness, and the people do not stray far. Thus, the people have choices to depart or remain—when faults are severe, departures increase; when goodness prevails, those who stay grow ever more abundant.

*The Chongxu Jing* says: Yao rejoiced and asked the children, *"Who taught you to speak thus?"* The children replied, *"It is an ancient poem, and all the people sing with full bellies."* The Way of Heaven—

*The Nanhua Jing* says: The Heavenly Dao moves without accumulation, and thus all things flourish. It benefits without harming.

*The Tongxuan Jing* says: The sun rises from the earth, and all things thrive. When thunder moves, all things awaken; when rain nourishes, all things are refreshed.

The Sage’s Dao—

*The Nanhua Jing* says: The Imperial Dao moves without accumulation, and thus the world unites. The Sage’s mind is still, a mirror of Heaven and Earth, a reflection of all things. The world is at peace. Acting without contention.

*The Tongxuan Jing* says: The noble’s actions resemble the Dao. The movement of Yin and Yang follows fixed rhythms; the noble’s actions do not exhaust things. The wind does not stir without cause, fire does not flare without fuel. The noble does not speak, and the petty cannot fabricate. When fire emerges, it must await kindling; when the noble speaks, it must be true. With truth and sincerity, what cannot be accomplished?

*The Daoist Virtue and Power Classic: Four Masters’ Ancient Dao Exegesis* concludes here.
